Rule of Jami' Mudhakkar Salim Including Females When Mixed
Explaining the scope of masculine plural when females are present

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for the inclusion of females under the sound masculine plural and apply it to legal documents such as contracts of security (aman).

Core Idea
The sound masculine plural includes females only when both genders are mixed, by linguistic predominance (taghlib), not when females are alone.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the scope of plural forms is crucial for interpreting legal texts. This lesson examines a key linguistic rule: when does the sound masculine plural (جمع المذكر السالم) include females? The Hanafi position, supported by Quranic evidence and practical examples from early Hanafi works, holds that inclusion occurs only when males and females are mixed together (ikhtilat), by way of taghlib (predominance). This rule has direct consequences for contracts, oaths, and other legal formulations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Read the opening statement: the sound masculine plural includes both males and females when mixed, but not females alone.
The key term is 'عند الاختلاط' (when mixed).
2
Understand the condition of ikhtilat
Taghlib only applies when both genders are present. If only females are present, the masculine plural does not include them.
Contrast with the feminine plural, which exclusively refers to females.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that even when mixed, the masculine plural does not include females. Understand the basis of the disagreement.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf rooted in different linguistic assumptions.
4
Apply to the aman example
Work through the example from al-Siyar al-Kabir: if someone says 'grant security over my sons (bani)' and he has both sons and daughters, the security covers all children. If he says 'over my daughters (banati)', it excludes sons.
The rule is consistently applied: masculine plural includes both when mixed; feminine plural excludes males.
5
Note the Quranic evidence
Verse 33:35 explicitly mentions both 'muslimin' and 'muslimat'. This is cited to show that the masculine form alone would have been sufficient to include women, but Allah mentioned both for emphasis and honor.
This supports the validity of taghlib in the Quran itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان جمع المذكر وجمع المؤنث
فقال: الجمع المذكور بعلامة الذكور عندنا يتناول الذكور والإناث عند الاختلاط، ولا يتناول الإناث المنفردات؛ لأنّ تناول الجمع المذكر للإناث إنما هو للتغليب، والتغليب إنّما يتحقق عند الاختلاط دون الإناث المنفردات وعند الشافعي له: لا يتناول الإناث عند الاختلاط أيضًا؛
وإن ذكر بعلامة التأنيث يتناول الإناث خاصة؛ لأنّ الرجل لا يكون تبعا للأنثى حتى يدخل في تغليب الأنثى.
حتى قال في "السير الكبير" : إذا قال: "آمنوني على بني"، وله بنون وبنات، إنّ الأمان يتناول الفريقين؛ لأن الجمع المذكر يتناول الذكور والإناث عند الاختلاط. ولو قال: "آمنوني على بنائي" لا يتناول الذكور من أولاده؛ لأن الجمع للمؤنث لا يتناول الذكور على سبيل التغليب. ولو قال: على بني وليس له البنات لا يثبت الأمان لهنّ؛ لأن الجمع المذكر إنما يتناول المؤنث عند الاختلاط تغليباً دون الانفراد لعدم التغليب.
وأخرج الترمذي في "جامعه" رقم: ۳۲۱۱ باب ومن سورة الأحزاب، عن أمّ عمارة الأنصارية لها أنها أتت فقالت: ما أرى كلّ شيء إلا للرجال، وما أرى النساء يذكرون بشيء، فنزلت هذه الآية وإنَّ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَالْمُسْلِمَاتِ وَالْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَالْمُؤْمِنَاتِ ﴾ (الأحزاب:(٣٥) الآية
Explanation of the masculine plural and feminine plural. He said: The plural marked with the sign of masculinity according to us includes males and females when mixed, and does not include females alone; because the inclusion of females by the masculine plural is only by way of predominance (taghlib), and predominance only occurs when mixed, not when females are alone. According to al-Shafi'i, it does not include females even when mixed either. And if [the plural] is mentioned with the sign of femininity, it includes females exclusively; because a male is not subordinate to a female such that he enters into the predominance of the female. So much so that it is stated in al-Siyar al-Kabir: If someone says 'grant me security over my sons (bani)' and he has sons and daughters, security encompasses both groups; because the masculine plural includes males and females when mixed. If he says 'grant me security over my daughters (banati)', it does not include his male children; because the feminine plural does not include males by way of predominance. If he says 'over my sons (bani)' and he has no daughters, security is not established for them [if daughters exist?]; because the masculine plural only includes females when mixed, by predominance, not when they are alone due to lack of predominance. And al-Tirmidhi narrated in his Jami', no. 3211, chapter of Surat al-Ahzab, from Umm 'Amara al-Ansariyya that she came and said: 'I see everything is only for men, and I see women not mentioned with anything,' so this verse was revealed: 'Indeed, the Muslim men and Muslim women, the believing men and believing women...' (33:35).
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the scope of the sound masculine plural (جمع المذكر السالم) in legal language. The Hanafi position is that when the masculine plural is used in a context where both males and females are present (اختلاط), it includes both genders by way of predominance (تغليب). However, if the females are alone (without males), the masculine plural does not include them. This is because predominance requires the presence of both groups. The Shafi'i position differs: even when mixed, the masculine plural does not include females. The evidence for the Hanafi view includes the Quranic verse 33:35 which explicitly mentions both 'muslimin' and 'muslimat', indicating that the masculine plural alone would have included the women, but Allah mentioned both to honor them. Practical application is given from Imam Muhammad's al-Siyar al-Kabir: in a contract of security (aman), if a man says 'over my sons' and he has both sons and daughters, the security covers all children; but if he says 'over my daughters,' it excludes sons. If he says 'over my sons' and he has no daughters, security does not extend to any daughters (implying that if daughters are present, they are included; if not, they are not). The passage also clarifies that the feminine plural exclusively refers to females; a male cannot be included by predominance.

Key Terms
جمع المذكر السالم (jam' al-mudhakkar al-salim): A plural formed by adding واو ونون or ياء ونون to the singular masculine noun, e.g., مسلمون.
التغليب (al-taghlib): Linguistic predominance: using the masculine form to include females when both are present, making the masculine gender dominant.
الاختلاط (al-ikhtilat): Mixed presence of males and females in the referent group.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone says 'I appoint my sons (bani)' as beneficiaries, and he has both sons and daughters, who is included?
A) Only the sons.
B) Both sons and daughters.
C) Only the daughters.
D) Neither, because the term is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The sound masculine plural includes females only when mixed with males, by linguistic predominance; this rule is applied in Hanafi legal interpretation of texts like contracts and wills.

Taghlib: Masculine Plural Includes Females When Mixed
Explains the rule of taghlib for masculine and feminine plurals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of taghlib to determine the scope of masculine and feminine plurals in legal texts and derive rulings accordingly.

Core Idea
Masculine plural includes females when mixed; feminine plural excludes males—taghlib is a foundational Hanafi usul principle.

In Hanafi legal theory, the scope of plural terms is not always symmetrical. The sound masculine plural, when used in a context where both males and females are present, encompasses females through the principle of taghlib (predominance). The feminine plural, however, remains exclusive to females. This lesson unpacks the rule, its justification, and its application to practical examples, such as security pacts and Qur'anic verses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two plural forms
Recognize the sound masculine plural (جمع المذكر السالم) and the sound feminine plural (جمع المؤنث السالم). The masculine plural is inherently for males; the feminine plural is inherently for females.
The signs are: masculine adds waw-nun or ya-nun; feminine adds alif-ta.
2
Understand the rule of taghlib
Taghlib is the linguistic principle that when males and females are intermingled, the masculine plural is used to refer to the whole group, including females. This is not a metaphor but an established rule of language.
The text argues that taghlib is either a direct rule from the language-founder or a form of general metaphor that does not require combining reality and metaphor.
3
Analyze the evidence from the hadith
The hadith of Umm 'Amara (Umm Salama) shows that women complained they were not mentioned in the Qur'an. In response, Allah revealed the verse mentioning both 'Muslimin' and 'Muslimat.' The text argues that 'Muslimin' alone would have sufficed to include women via taghlib, but the explicit mention was for consolation.
The hadith is from al-Tirmidhi and is considered hasan gharib.
4
Apply the rule to the example from al-Siyar al-Kabir
If a commander says 'آمنوني على بني' (grant me security over my sons) and he has sons and daughters, the security covers both groups because 'bani' is masculine plural and the children are mixed. If he says 'آمنوني على بنائي' (over my daughters), it covers only daughters. If he says 'bani' and has no daughters, security does not extend to hypothetical daughters because taghlib requires actual mixing.
This example illustrates the practical effect of the rule in legal transactions (security/amān).
5
Address the objection about metaphor
Some may argue that taghlib creates a combination of reality (masculine plural for males) and metaphor (for females). The commentary clarifies that taghlib is not a metaphor but a language-functional rule, or if it is considered a general metaphor, it does not involve the problematic combination.
This step deepens understanding of the epistemological status of taghlib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان جمع المذكر وجمع المؤنث
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث لأنّ كلّ علامة مخصوصة لمعنى هو حقيقتها، فلو تناول الإناث لزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، ولزم التكرار في قوله: "إنّ المسلمين والمسلمات". قلنا: نزول الآية في حقهن لتطييب قلوبهنّ حيث قلن: ما بالنا لم نُذكر في القرآن صريحا واستقلالاً؟ فنزلت الآية في أي كما يذكر الرجال حقهن لأجل هذا لا أنهن لم يُدخلن في الجمع المذكر والتغليب باب واسع في القرآن.

أي الجمع أي الألف والتاء يدخل في تغليب الأنثى.

الكافر
الفريقين؛ لأن الجمع المذكر يتناول الذكور والإناث عند الاختلاط. ولو قال: "آمنوني على بنائي" لا يتناول الذكور من أولاده؛ لأن الجمع للمؤنث لا يتناول الذكور على سبيل التغليب. ولو قال: على بني وليس له . البنات لا يثبت الأمان لهنّ؛ لأن الجمع المذكر إنما
يتناول المؤنث عند الاختلاط تغليباً دون الانفراد لعدم التغليب.
مخصوصة لمعنى هو : أي ذلك المعنى حقيقة تلك العلامة لحقيقة علامة جمع المذكر السالم هي الذكور، فلو تناول إلخ . (القمر) ولزم التكرار إلخ لشمول المسلمين للمسلمات. (القمر)
حيث قلن: ما بالنا إلخ كذا في مسند أحمد عن أم سلمة لأجل هذا كذا قال البيضاوي.(القمر) باب واسع إلخ وهذا التغليب في الجمع ليس بمجاز فإن اعتباره من الواضع حين بناء قاعدة الجمع؛ فلا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز أو يقال: إن التغليب من باب عموم المجاز؛ فلا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز.(القمر) لا يثبت الأمان لهن إلخ فيه أنه ينبغي أن يثبت الأمان لهنّ بأن يراد من البنين الأولاد مجازا إطلاقا للمقيد على المطلق احتياطًا لثبوت الأمان، ويمكن أن يقال: إنّه متى أمكن العمل بالحقيقة لا يثبت المجاز، تدبّر . (القمر) أخرج الترمذي في "جامعه" رقم: ۳۲۱۱ باب ومن سورة الأحزاب، عن أمّ عمارة الأنصارية لها أنها أتت فقالت: ما أرى كلّ شيء إلا للرجال، وما أرى النساء يذكرون بشيء، فنزلت هذه الآية وإنَّ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَالْمُسْلِمَاتِ وَالْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَالْمُؤْمِنَاتِ ﴾ (الأحزاب:(٣٥) الآية، قال الترمذي: هذا حديث حسن غريب، وإنما يعرف هذا الحديث من هذا الوجه.
النبي
ولو ذكر هذه الأمثلة على سبيل النشر المرتب لكان أولى وأخصر .
Explanation of masculine plural and feminine plural. Explanation of the divisions of the third division because every specific sign is for a meaning that is its reality. If it were to include females, it would necessitate combination of reality and metaphor, and would necessitate repetition in His saying: 'Indeed the Muslim men and Muslim women.' We say: The revelation of the verse concerning them was to appease their hearts when they said: 'Why are we not mentioned in the Qur'an explicitly and independently?' So the verse was revealed about them as the men are mentioned, for this reason, not because they were not included in the masculine plural, and inclusion (taghlib) is a broad chapter in the Qur'an. And if mentioned with a sign of femininity, it specifically includes females; because a man cannot be a follower of a female, meaning the plural with alif and ta enters into inclusion of the female. Until he said in 'Al-Siyar al-Kabir': If he said 'Grant me security over my sons (bani)' and he has sons and daughters, the security includes both groups; because the masculine plural includes males and females when mixed. If he said 'Grant me security over my daughters (banati),' it does not include the male children; because the feminine plural does not include males by way of inclusion. And if he said 'over my sons' and he has no daughters, security is not established for them (if there were daughters); because the masculine plural only includes females when mixed, as inclusion, not when alone, due to lack of inclusion. (Commentary:) 'Specific for a meaning' means that meaning is the reality of that sign, so if it were to include females it would necessitate combination... (al-Qamar) 'And would necessitate repetition' etc. because 'Muslimin' includes 'Muslimat'. (al-Qamar) 'When they said: Why not us etc.' as in Musnad Ahmad from Umm Salama; 'for this reason' as said by al-Baydawi. (al-Qamar) 'A broad chapter' etc. This inclusion in the plural is not metaphor, because its consideration is from the language-founder when constructing the rule of plurals; so combination of reality and metaphor is not necessitated. Or it is said: Inclusion is from the category of general metaphor; so combination is not necessitated. (al-Qamar) 'Security is not established for them' etc. There is the point that it would be better to establish security for them by intending from 'banin' the meaning of 'awlad' (children) metaphorically, as an unrestricted application of a restricted term, as a precaution to establish security. And it can be said: Whenever it is possible to operate on the reality, metaphor is not established. Reflect. (al-Qamar) Al-Tirmidhi narrated in his Jami', no. 3211, chapter from Surat al-Ahzab, from Umm 'Amara al-Ansariyya that she came and said: 'I see everything is for men, and I do not see women mentioned in anything.' So this verse was revealed: 'Indeed the Muslim men and Muslim women...' (al-Ahzab:35). Al-Tirmidhi said: This hadith is hasan gharib, and this hadith is only known from this chain. The Prophet. And if he had mentioned these examples in the form of ordered (nashr murtab) it would have been better and more concise.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
The passage is a ruling on the linguistic-legal principle of taghlib (inclusion) as applied to plurals. It begins by stating that each plural form has a specific meaning: the sound masculine plural (جمع مذكر سالم) is inherently for males. If it were to include females, it would combine reality and metaphor, and the Qur'anic wording 'the Muslim men and the Muslim women' would be redundant. The author responds that the verse was revealed specifically to console women who complained they were not mentioned explicitly; it does not prove they were excluded from the masculine plural. Taghlib is a widespread principle in the Qur'an. Then the rule: if a term is in the feminine plural (with alif and ta), it specifically denotes females only, because a male cannot be subordinate to a female. The text then cites Imam Muhammad's Al-Siyar al-Kabir with examples: if a commander says 'grant me security over my sons (bani)' and he has both sons and daughters, the security includes both due to taghlib in the masculine plural when mixed genders are present. If he says 'over my daughters (banati)', it does not include sons. If he says 'over my sons' and he has no daughters, the security does not include potential daughters because taghlib only operates when there is a mixed group, not when the plural is used alone. The marginal commentary (al-Qamar) addresses objections: taghlib is not metaphor but a rule established by the language-founder, or it is a form of general metaphor that avoids combining reality and metaphor. Another point: if the ruler says 'bani' but intends all children, it could be a metaphor; but caution (ihtiyat) in security matters might prefer the literal meaning. Finally, the hadith of Umm 'Amara (Umm Salama) is cited as evidence for the verse's occasion of revelation, showing that women were concerned about not being mentioned, and Allah revealed the verse including both. The passage ends with a note that the author could have arranged the examples in a more ordered way (nashr murtab) for clarity.

Key Terms
جمع المذكر السالم (jam‘ al-mudhakkar al-salim): The sound masculine plural form (adding waw and nun or ya and nun) which by its reality denotes males, but when mixed with females includes them via taghlib.
جمع المؤنث السالم (jam‘ al-mu’annath al-salim): The sound feminine plural form (adding alif and ta) which specifically denotes females only and does not include males even by taghlib.
تغليب (taghlib): The linguistic principle of giving precedence to one gender (usually masculine) over the other when both are present, so that the masculine plural is used to include females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a commander says 'آمنوني على بني' and he has no daughters, does the security cover potential daughters?
A) Yes, because 'bani' includes all children by default.
B) No, because taghlib only applies when the group is mixed, and here there are no daughters.
C) Yes, because caution requires including them.
D) No, because the term 'bani' is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The masculine plural includes females when the group is mixed (taghlib); the feminine plural excludes males entirely.

Definition and Ruling of Kinayah in Divorce: The Case of 'Ba'in'
Analyzing the linguistic category of kinayah in talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the word 'ba'in' according to the Hanafi usul definition of kinayah and explain why it results in an irrevocable divorce when intended.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'ba'in' is not a true kinayah; it causes irrevocable divorce, not revocable.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar delves into the precise definition of kinayah (indirect expression) in the context of divorce. It examines whether commonly used phrases like 'anti ba'in' (you are separate) are truly kinayah according to the principles of usul al-fiqh or only according to the science of eloquence (bayan). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings when a husband uses ambiguous language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul definition of kinayah
The passage defines kinayah as an expression whose intended meaning is hidden, not its literal meaning. Apply this to 'ba'in'.
Literal meaning of 'ba'in' is clear (separate), but intended meaning is hidden (divorce).
2
Analyze the objection
An objector argues that 'ba'in' fits the usul definition, so it should be true kinayah and cause revocable divorce.
The objector's dalil: intended meaning is hidden, thus kinayah.
3
Distinguish the Hanafi response using bayan vs usul
The Hanafi response differentiates the usul definition from the bayan definition. True kinayah in bayan involves transfer from literal meaning to a necessary implication; divorce is not a necessary implication of 'ba'in'.
Example: 'long sword-belt' implies tall stature; 'ba'in' does not imply divorce necessarily.
4
Identify the exceptions
Three words ('i'taddi', 'istabri'i rahimak', 'anti wahida') are exceptions because they implicitly contain the word 'divorce' and thus cause revocable divorce.
This is based on the hadith of Sawda bint Zam'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث
تعريف الكناية وحكمها
لا إبهام فيه؛ إذ معنى البائن واضح لكن لا يعلم من أيّ شيء بائن؟ أ من الزوج، أو من العشيرة، أو من المال، أو الجمال؟ فإذا نوى أنّها بائن عنّى زال الإبهام؛ فكان عاملاً بموجبه ولذا وقع الطلاق البائن بها، ولو كانت كنايات حقيقة لكانت من قبيل أن يذكر "أنت بائن ويراد به أنت طالق"، فيقع الطلاق الرجعي.
واعترض عليه بأنّ الكناية ما كان معناه المراد به مستتراً لا معناه اللغوي، وههنا كذلك؛ فإنّ البائن وإن كان معناه اللغوي واضحًا لكن معناه المراد به مستتر، وهو أنها بائن عن الزوج، فكانت كنايات حقيقة، ولهذا قالوا: إنّها كنايات على مذهب علماء البيان دون الأصول؛ فإنّ الكناية عندهم أن يذكر لفظ ويراد به معناه الموضوع له، لا من حيث ذاته بل من حيث ينتقل منه إلى ملزومه كما في طويل النّجاد يراد به طول النّجاد لا من حيث ذاته بل من حيث ينتقل منه إلى ملزومه الذي هو طول القامة، وههنا كذلك، فإنّ علماء البيان بائنا محمول على معناه لينتقل منه إلى ملزومه وهو الطلاق بصفة البينونة عند النية، لكن لا يعلم إلخ فبهذا الإبهام صارت هذه الألفاظ مشبهة بالكنايات الحقيقية.
(القمر) زال الإبهام ولزم الطلاق البائن. بموجبه : فإنّ موجب الكلام البينونة ولذا أي لكون العمل بموجب هذه الألفاظ، وعدم جعلها كناية عن صريح الطلاق. لكانت إلخ فإنّه يكون معناها حين كونها كناية عن الطلاق، معنى الطلاق. (القمر) الطلاق الرجعي إلخ لأنّ في هذه الإرادة يكون الطلاق مسلوبًا عنه صفة البينونة؛ فيكون رجعيًا. (السنبلي) فكانت كنايات إلخ فيه أنه هذا لا يضر المصنف له؛ فإن غاية ما لزم من تقرير الاعتراض أن هذه الألفاظ صارت كنايات عن البينونة عن الزوج؛ فيلزم البينونة من هذه الألفاظ، لا أنها صارت كنايات عن الطلاق بأن يكون معنى هذه الألفاظ الطلاق؛ فتسميتها بإضافة الكنايات إلى الطلاق مجاز ، وهذا هو مرام المصنف له، فتأمل. (القمر) معنى دون الأصول فيه أنه ثبت من تقرير الشارح له أن هذه الألفاظ كنايات عند علماء البيان عن البينونة عن الزوج، و لم يثبت أنها كنايات عندهم عن الطلاق وأهل الأصول يقولون: إنّ تسميتها كنايات الطلاق بإضافة الكنايات إلى الطلاق مجاز؛ فلا مخالفة، فتدبّر . (القمر) لا من حيث ذاته فإن طول النّجاد ليس مقصود أصلي. (القمر)
عند النية : أي نية الزوج بأنّ المراد البينونة من النكاح ، وهذا متعلق بقوله: ينتقل إلخ. (القمر)
وهو أيضًا لا يخلو من خدشة، فتأمل.
إلا "اعتدي واستبرئي رحمك وأنت واحدة". استثناء من قوله: "حتى كانت بوائن" يعني أنّ ألفاظ الكنايات كلّها بوائن إلا هذه الألفاظ الثلاثة فإنّها رجعيّة؛ لأجل وجود لفظ الطلاق فيها تقديراً. أما في قوله: "اعتدي"؛ فلأنه يحتمل اعتداد نعمة الله عليها، ويحتمل اعتداد الحيض للفراغ عن العدّة. فإذا نوى هذا يقع الطلاق الرجعي، فإن كانت مدخولا بها يثبت الطلاق اقتضاء كأنه قال: "اعتدي لأنّي طلقتك، أو طلقي ثم اعتدي، أو كوني ثم اعتدي": فيقع الطلاق وتجب العدّة ، وإن كانت غير مدخول بها فحينئذ لاعدة عليها أصلاً؛
وهو إلخ : أي كون هذه الألفاظ كنايات على طور علماء البيان أيضًا لا يخلو عن خدشة؛ فإنه ليس فيها انتقال من اللازم إلى الملزوم، بل لم ينتقل من معانيها إلى شيء آخر؛ إذ المراد بهذه الألفاظ البينونة أو الحرمة أو القطع لكن على وجه مخصوص وفي محلّ فيه الاستتار، كذا في "التلويح". (القمر) فتأمل إلخ وجه التأمّل أنّ البائن إذا كان محمولاً على معناه اللغوي أي المنفصل، فحينئذ لا ينتقل إلى الطلاق بصفة البينونة؛ لأن معناه اللغوي عام والشرعي خاص، ولا ينتقل من العام إلى الخاص بل بالعكس؛ ولأن الكناية عند علماء البيان أن يذكر لفظ ويراد به المعنى الحقيقي مع الانتقال إلى لازمه لا إلى ملزومه؛ لأن اللازم من حيث إنه لازم لا ينتقل الذهن منه إلى ملزومه؛ لأنّ اللازم قد يكون عامًا، بخلاف الملزوم، فإنه ينتقل منه إلى لازمه؛ لأن الملزوم لا يكون عامًا قطعًا كما في طويل النّجاد ؛ فإنه ينتقل من معناه إلى لازمه، وهو طول القامة فإن طويل النّجاد ليس لازماً لطويل القامة بل بالعكس أي طول القامة لازم لطويل النجاد، فإذا علم هذا لا يكون قوله: "أنت بائن وأمثاله كناية عندهم؛ لأنّ الطلاق ليس بلازم للبائن بمعنى اللغوي حتى ينتقل إليه. فلأنه يحتمل إلخ ولأنه قال عليه السودة بنت زمعة ها اعتدي ثم ،راجعها، كذا في "التحقيق".(السنبلي) :هذا أي اعتداد الحيض للفراغ عن العدة. (القمر) اقتضاء: لأنه لما أمرها بالاعتداد ولا يجب العدة إلا بالموجب؛ فلابد من اعتبار الطلاق مقدما؛ ليصح الأمر، والضرورة تقع بإثبات أصل الطلاق؛ فلا حاجة إلى إثبات أمر زائد كالبينونة؛ فلذلك كان الواقع بهذا اللفظ رجعيًا لا بائنا. (القمر) اقتضاء إلخ: هذا إذا كان بعد الدخول، والحكم بالطلاق الاقتضائي في الكل تغليبي؛ لأن الطلاق اقتضاء في قوله: أعتدي واستبرئي رحمك بعد الدخول، فهو مستعار عن الطلاق في هذين القولين.(السنبلي)
Statement of the divisions of the third division. Definition of kinayah and its ruling. There is no ambiguity in it; for the meaning of 'ba'in' is clear, but it is not known from what thing it is separate? From the husband, or from the clan, or from wealth, or from beauty? So if he intends that she is separate from me, the ambiguity is removed; thus it acts according to its implication, and therefore irrevocable divorce occurs by it. If they were true kinayah, they would be of the type that one says 'You are separate' and means 'You are divorced,' and then revocable divorce occurs. An objection was raised against this: that kinayah is that whose intended meaning is hidden, not its literal meaning, and here it is so; for 'ba'in' even though its literal meaning is clear, its intended meaning is hidden, which is that she is separate from the husband, so they are true kinayah. For this reason they said: They are kinayah according to the scholars of eloquence, not according to usul; for kinayah according to them is that a word is mentioned and its literal meaning is intended, not in itself but in that one transfers from it to its necessary implication, as in 'long of the sword-belt' meaning the length of the sword-belt not in itself but in that one transfers from it to its necessary implication which is tall stature. And here it is so; for the scholars of eloquence take 'ba'in' upon its meaning so that one transfers from it to its necessary implication which is divorce with the attribute of separation upon intention. But it is not known, etc. So by this ambiguity these words became similar to true kinayah. (al-Qamar) The ambiguity is removed and irrevocable divorce is established. By its implication: for the implication of the speech is separation, and therefore, because of acting by the implication of these words and not making them kinayah for explicit divorce. If they were... etc. For its meaning when it is kinayah for divorce would be the meaning of divorce. (al-Qamar) Revocable divorce, etc., because in this intention the divorce is stripped of the attribute of separation; thus it becomes revocable. (al-Sanabali) So they were kinayah... etc. In it is that this does not harm the author; for the utmost that necessitated from the establishment of the objection is that these words became kinayah for separation from the husband; thus separation is necessitated from these words, not that they became kinayah for divorce such that the meaning of these words is divorce; so calling them 'kinayah of divorce' by adding kinayah to divorce is metaphorical, and this is the intent of the author, so reflect. (al-Qamar) The meaning of 'without usul' is that it is established from the exposition of the commentator that these words are kinayah according to the scholars of eloquence for separation from the husband, and it is not established that they are kinayah according to them for divorce. And the scholars of usul say: Calling them 'kinayah of divorce' by adding kinayah to divorce is metaphorical; so there is no contradiction, so ponder. (al-Qamar) Not in itself, for the length of the sword-belt is not the primary intent. (al-Qamar) Upon intention: i.e., the intention of the husband that the intended meaning is separation from marriage, and this is related to his saying 'one transfers' etc. (al-Qamar) And it also is not free from fault, so reflect. Except 'Observe the waiting period,' 'Seek to clear your womb,' and 'You are one.' An exception from his saying 'until they were irrevocable' meaning that all the words of kinayah are irrevocable except these three words, for they are revocable; due to the presence of the word 'divorce' in them implicitly. As for his saying 'Observe the waiting period': because it can mean observing the favor of Allah upon her, and it can mean observing the menstruation for completion of the waiting period. So if he intends this, revocable divorce occurs. If she is one who has been consummated, divorce is established by implication, as if he said: 'Observe the waiting period because I have divorced you, or divorce and then observe the waiting period, or be [divorced] and then observe the waiting period': so divorce occurs and the waiting period becomes obligatory. If she is not consummated, then there is no waiting period at all. And it is... etc.: i.e., that these words being kinayah according to the manner of the scholars of eloquence is also not free from fault; for there is no transference from the necessary to the necessary consequence, rather their meanings are not transferred to anything else; for the intended meaning by these words is separation, or prohibition, or cutting, but in a specific manner and in a place where there is concealment, as in al-Talwih. (al-Qamar) So reflect... etc. The aspect of reflection is that if 'ba'in' is taken in its literal meaning i.e., the separated, then there is no transference to divorce with the attribute of separation; because its literal meaning is general and the legal meaning is specific, and one does not transfer from the general to the specific but rather the opposite. And because kinayah according to the scholars of eloquence is that a word is mentioned and its real meaning is intended with transfer to its necessary consequence, not to its necessary; because the necessary in that it is necessary does not cause the mind to transfer to its necessary consequence; because the necessary may be general, unlike the necessitated, for one transfers from it to its necessary; because the necessitated is definitely not general, as in 'long of the sword-belt'; one transfers from its meaning to its necessary, which is tall stature, for 'long of the sword-belt' is not a necessary for tall stature but rather the opposite—tall stature is necessary for long of the sword-belt. So if this is known, the statement 'You are separate' and its likes are not kinayah according to them, because divorce is not a necessary for 'ba'in' in the literal sense such that one transfers to it. For it can mean... etc. And because the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to Sawda bint Zam'a: 'Observe the waiting period' then he took her back, as in al-Tahqiq. (al-Sanabali) This: i.e., observing the menstruation for completion of the waiting period. (al-Qamar) By implication: because when he commanded her to observe the waiting period, and the waiting period is only obligatory due to a cause, so it is necessary to consider divorce prior to it to make the command valid, and necessity falls upon establishing the original divorce; so there is no need to establish an additional matter like separation; therefore what occurs by this word is revocable, not irrevocable. (al-Qamar) 'By implication' etc.: This is if after consummation. And the ruling of implicational divorce in all is preponderant; because divorce by implication in his saying 'Observe the waiting period' and 'Seek to clear your womb' after consummation, so it is borrowed from divorce in these two sayings. (al-Sanabali)
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the category of kinayah (indirect expression) in the context of divorce (talaq) according to Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by examining the word 'ba'in' (separate). Although its literal meaning is clear, it is ambiguous as to what aspect of separation is intended—from the husband, family, wealth, or beauty. When the husband intends separation from himself, the ambiguity is resolved, and the word acts as an indirect expression that causes an irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. The author then presents an objection: according to the definition of kinayah as 'that whose intended meaning is hidden,' 'ba'in' qualifies as true kinayah because its intended meaning (separation from the husband) is hidden. The objectors argue that if it were true kinayah, saying 'you are separate' while meaning 'you are divorced' should result in a revocable (raj'i) divorce, as is the case when one uses an explicit word for divorce indirectly. The author responds by distinguishing between the usul and bayan (rhetoric) definitions of kinayah. In bayan, kinayah involves using a word in its literal meaning while implying a necessary consequence (lazim), like 'long of the sword-belt' implying tall stature. Here, 'ba'in' would be used literally to imply the necessary consequence of divorce with separation. However, usul scholars consider this metaphorical language, not true kinayah, because the intended meaning (divorce) is not a necessary consequence of the literal meaning (separation) in a strict sense—divorce is a specific legal concept, while separation is general. The commentary then addresses exceptions: three words—'i'taddi' (observe waiting period), 'istabri'i rahimak' (clear your womb), and 'anti wahida' (you are one)—are revocable exceptions to the general rule that kinayah words cause irrevocable divorce. The reason is that these words implicitly contain the concept of divorce (e.g., 'i'taddi' implies 'because I have divorced you'). The commentary further refines the discussion by noting that the classification of these words as kinayah in the bayan sense is also not without flaw, as there is no clear transfer from a necessary to a necessary consequence. The overall ruling is that words like 'ba'in' cause an irrevocable divorce when accompanied by the appropriate intention, while the three exceptions cause revocable divorce due to the implicit presence of the word 'divorce'.

Key Terms
الكناية (al-kināyah): An indirect expression whose intended meaning is hidden, requiring intention to clarify; in usul, distinct from bayan usage where it involves transfer from literal to a necessary consequence.
البائن (al-bā'in): Literally 'separate'; used as an indirect expression for divorce, causing irrevocable divorce when intended.
الطلاق الرجعي (al-ṭalāq al-rij'ī): Revocable divorce where the husband can take his wife back without a new contract during the waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling if a husband says to his wife 'anti ba'in' (you are separate) with the intention of divorce?
A) It results in no divorce because the word is not explicit.
B) It results in a revocable divorce.
C) It results in an irrevocable divorce.
D) It results in an irrevocable divorce only if the wife agrees.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul view classifies 'ba'in' as not true kinayah, causing irrevocable divorce when intended, except for three words that implicitly contain 'divorce' and cause revocable divorce.

Kinayah vs Explicit: The Kaaf of Generality in Hadd Penalties
Example of kinayah not incurring hadd penalties, with a critical exception for comparative phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the kaaf al-tashbih in 'kama' creates generality (umum), thereby making an otherwise kinayah statement explicit for legal penalties.

Core Idea
A comparison with 'kama' becomes explicit because the kaaf necessitates generality — removing it from kinayah.

In Hanafi usul, not all indirect statements are treated equally. This lesson explores a key distinction: why 'huwa kama qulta' incurs hadd while 'sadaqta' does not. We'll trace how the kaaf of comparison transforms an allusion into a definitive acknowledgment, relying on the Hanafi rule that general terms are qat'i in their application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Compare the legal outcomes of 'sadaqta' (you told the truth) and 'huwa kama qulta' (he is as you said). The first remains kinayah; the second incurs hadd.
Both are responses to an accusation of zina.
2
Isolate the operative word
The key difference is the particle 'kama' (as). The kaaf of comparison is the linguistic element that changes the ruling.
'Kama' contains the kaaf al-tashbih.
3
Apply the Hanafi usul rule
In Hanafi usul, the kaaf of comparison necessitates generality (umum) in all attributes described. This principle makes the statement explicit and removes it from kinayah.
This is derived from the rule that general terms are qat'i al-dalalah.
4
Derive the ruling
Because the statement becomes explicit, it constitutes an acknowledgment of zina, incurring the hadd of qadhf. Without 'kama', it remains kinayah and no hadd applies.
The same logic applies to other scenarios in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع كما إذا أقر على نفسه: "بأني جامعت فلانةً جماعًا حراما"، لا يجب عليه حد الزنا، وكذا إذا قال لأحد: "جامعت "فلانة" لا يجب عليه حد القذف ما لم يقل: نكتها أو زنيت بها، وكذا إذا قال لآخر: "زنيت"، فقال: صدقت" لا يُحدّ حدّ الزنا؛ لأنه يحتمل أي الآخر أن يكون معناه صدقت قبل ذلك فلم كذبت الآن؟ بخلاف ما إذا قذف رجلاً بالزنا فقال الآخر: "هو كما قلت" يحدّ هذا المصدّق حد القذف؛ لأن كاف التشبيه يوجب العموم في جميع ما وصف به؛ فبطل كونه كناية. حد الزنا : فإنّه ليس بإقرار بالزنا؛ إذ يمكن أن يكون المراد بالجماع المباشرة الفاحشة. (القمر) لا يحد حد الزنا إلخ وإنما لم يحدّ هذا المصدّق وإن وجد الإقرار التزاما؛ لأنّ تصديق الزنا قبل وجود الزنا لا يتصوّر، فكأنه لم يُقرّ. (السنبلي) بخلاف ما إذا إلخ: هذا جواب لسؤال مقدّر، تقريره: أنه يشكل هذا القانون أي أنّ الكناية لا يثبت بها العقوبات بما لو قذف رجل رجلاً بالزنا، فقال الثالث هو كما قلت، فإن الثالث يُحدّ مع أنه ليس بتصريح بالنسبة إلى الزنا؟ وخلاصة الدفع أنّ كاف التشبيه يوجب العموم في المحل الذي يحتمله، حتى قلنا في قول علي : "إنما أعطينهم الذمة وبذلوا الجزية ليكون دماءهم كدمائنا وأموالهم كأموالنا": إنه يجري العموم فيما يسقط بالشبهات وفيما يثبت بها؛ فهذا الكاف أيضًا يوجب العموم؛ لأنه حصل في محلّ يحتمله، فكان نسبته إلى الزنا قطعا كما هو موجب العام . (السنبلي) يحدّ هذا المصدّق أي الآخر، ولو قذف رجلا بالزنا، فقال الثالث: صدقت في قولك هذا، يحدّ هذا الثالث للصراحة كذا قال بحر العلوم (القمر) يوجب العموم ولما كان كذلك فلا إبهام فيه؛ لأنّه تقرّر عند العلماء أنّ تناول العام إلى أفراده قطعية؛ فلا يكون كنايات. (القمر)
Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division: as when someone acknowledges against himself: 'I had sexual intercourse with so-and-so, forbidden intercourse,' the hadd punishment for zina is not obligatory on him. Similarly, if someone says to another: 'I had sexual intercourse with so-and-so,' the hadd punishment for qadhf is not obligatory unless he says: 'I had vaginal intercourse with her' or 'I committed zina with her.' Similarly, if he says to another: 'I committed zina,' and that other says: 'You have told the truth,' he is not given the hadd punishment for zina because it is possible that the meaning of 'you have told the truth' is that he had told the truth before (that he did not commit zina) and is now lying? Contrary to the case where someone accuses a man of zina and another says: 'He is as you have said,' this confirmer is given the hadd punishment for qadhf because the letter of comparison (kaaf al-tashbih) necessitates generality in all that is described; thus it ceases to be a metaphor. Hadd for zina: it is not an acknowledgment of zina because the meaning of 'intercourse' could be lewd behavior. (al-Qamar) 'He is not given the hadd for zina etc.' And the reason this confirmer is not given the hadd even though there is an acknowledgment of commitment is that confirming zina before its occurrence is inconceivable, so it is as if he did not confess. (al-Sunbuli) 'Contrary to the case where etc.': this is an answer to an implied question: that this rule is problematic, namely that metaphor does not necessitate penalties, in the case where someone accuses a man of zina and a third person says 'he is as you have said,' the third person receives hadd even though it is not explicit regarding zina. The summary of the response is that the letter of comparison necessitates generality in the context that allows it, such that we have said in the statement of Ali: 'We gave them the dhimma and they paid the jizya so that their blood is as our blood and their wealth as our wealth': that generality applies to matters that are dropped due to doubts and to matters that are established by them; so this kaaf also necessitates generality because it occurs in a context that allows it, so its relation to zina is decisive as is required by the general term. (al-Sunbuli) 'This confirmer is given the hadd' i.e., the third person, and if someone accuses a man of zina and a third person says: 'You have told the truth in this statement of yours,' this third person is given the hadd for explicitness, as stated by Bahr al-Ulum. (al-Qamar) 'It necessitates generality' and since it is thus, there is no ambiguity in it because it is established among scholars that the application of a general term to its individuals is definitive; so it is not a metaphor. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth division of textual inference within the context of Hanafi usul. It presents several scenarios demonstrating that kinayah (allusion) does not trigger hadd punishments unless explicit wording is used. For instance, saying 'jama'tu' (I had intercourse) without specification is kinayah and thus not sufficient for hadd of zina or qadhf. However, when a statement includes the comparative particle 'kama' (as), like 'huwa kama qulta' (he is as you said), it becomes explicit because the kaaf of comparison necessitates generality, applying to all aspects described. The commentators elaborate that this principle is consistent with broader Hanafi legal theory, where a general term is considered definitive. The example of Ali's statement about dhimma is cited to show how 'kama' creates generality in rulings. This underscores the importance of precise wording in legal effects, particularly in criminal penalties.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An allusion or indirect expression that does not explicitly denote the intended meaning.
كاف التشبيه (kaf al-tashbih): The letter 'kaf' used to create a comparison, which in this context necessitates generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does 'huwa kama qulta' incur hadd while 'sadaqta' does not?
A) Because 'huwa kama qulta' is an explicit statement, while 'sadaqta' is ambiguous.
B) Because the kaaf of comparison makes the statement general and thus explicit, removing it from kinayah.
C) Because 'sadaqta' is a denial of the accusation.
D) Because 'huwa kama qulta' contains a reference to the past.

Key Takeaway
The kaaf al-tashbih in 'kama' transforms an allusion into an explicit statement by necessitating generality, a distinctive Hanafi principle in hadd cases.

Understanding ʿIbārat al-Naṣṣ: The Expression of the Text
Defines ʿibārat al-naṣṣ and distinguishes it from other textual indications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ʿibārat al-naṣṣ, distinguish it from other types of dalāla, and identify the scope of its application in Hanafi uṣūl including the intra-school dispute with Ṣadr al-Sharīʿa.

Core Idea
ʿIbārat al-naṣṣ includes both primary and secondary meanings intended by the text, not just explicit statements.

This passage defines one of the four fundamental types of textual inference in Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh: ʿibārat al-naṣṣ. It clarifies that the term 'naṣṣ' here means the wording of the Qur'an generally, and that ʿibārat al-naṣṣ is the meaning for which the speech is directed, even if not explicitly stated. The passage also addresses a key dispute among Hanafi scholars regarding its scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Extract the definition of ʿibārat al-naṣṣ from the passage: it is the meaning that the speech is directed to convey, whether primarily or secondarily.
The passage distinguishes between the wording (naṣṣ) and the inferred meaning (ʿibāra).
2
Understand the broad usage of 'naṣṣ'
Recognize that 'naṣṣ' in this context refers to the Qur'anic wording in general, not the specific term opposite to ẓāhir.
This is a technical point: the definition applies to any Qur'anic expression, whether naṣṣ, ẓāhir, mufassar, or khāṣ.
3
Work through the example
Analyze the verse 'Marry what is lawful for you' – it is ʿibārat al-naṣṣ for the permissibility of marriage, even though the primary purpose is to indicate the permissible number.
The permissibility is a secondary intended meaning, yet still counts as ʿibārat al-naṣṣ according to the majority.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Contrast the majority view with Ṣadr al-Sharīʿa who restricts ʿibārat al-naṣṣ to only primary meanings.
The practical consequence: under Ṣadr's view, the marriage verse would not be ʿibārat al-naṣṣ for permissibility.
5
Apply to a new text
Practice by determining if a given verse is ʿibārat al-naṣṣ for a particular ruling, considering both primary and secondary intentions.
Use the distinction between naṣṣ and ẓāhir to gauge the directness of the indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع هو ذات عبارة النص، وما ثبت به هو الحكم الثابت بعبارة النص، والاستدلال هو الانتقال من الأثر إلى المؤثر أو بالعكس، والأخير هو المراد ههنا. والنص هو عبارة القرآن أعم من أنّه يكون نصا أو ظاهرًا أو مفسّرًا أو خاصا، وهذا الإطلاق شائع في عرف الفقهاء من غير نكير؛ ولذا جاء في التعريف بقوله: "ما سيق الكلام له دون ما سيق النص له والعمل هو عمل المجتهد أعني الاستنباط دون عمل الجوارح؛ فيصير حاصل المعنى، وأمّا انتقال الذهن من عبارة القرآن إلى الحكم، فهو استنباط المجتهد من ظاهر ما سيق الكلام له والمراد من هذا السوق أعم مما يكون في النص، فإنّ السوق في النص ما يكون مقصودًا أصليًّا، وفي عبارة النص ما كان مقصودًا أصليًّا، هو ذات عبارة النص : فالنّظم يسمّى نصاً أو ظاهراً بالنظر إلى نفس الكلام ويسمّى عبارة النص بالنظر إلى استدلال المستدل؛ فالذات واحدةٌ، والفرق بالاعتبار، وكذا الفرق بين الإشارة والظاهر والنص. ثم اعلم أن هذا على رأي الشارح له، وأما على رأي الآخرين فالنظم يسمّى الدال بعبارة النص والدال بإشارة النص، وهكذا. والدلالة تسمّى بعبارة النص وبإشارة النص، وهكذا كما قد مرّ سابقا أو بالعكس أي الانتقال من المؤثر إلى الأثر. (القمر) والأخير: أي الانتقال من المؤثر إلى الأثر . هو عبارة القرآن أي ليس المراد بالنص ما هو قسيم الظاهر، بل المراد منه لفظ القرآن وعبارة النص هو عين النص، فالإضافة من قبيل إضافة قولهم نفس الشيء. (القمر) وهذا الإطلاق أي إطلاق النص على لفظ القرآن (القمر) ولذا أي لكون المراد من النص اللفظ جاء في التعريف إلخ، فلو كان المراد بالنص ما تقدّم ذكره، لكان تعريفه بالكلام تعريفا بالأعم، وذلك غير جائز، كذا قال ابن الملك. ولذا جاء إلخ : أي لبدل على أن المراد بالنص ههنا مطلق القرآن دون ما اصطلح عليه القوم. (السنبلي) فهو استنباط إلخ كما يقال: "الصلاة فريضة"؛ لقوله تعالى: أَقِيمُوا الصَّلاةَ﴾ (الأنعام: ۷۲) (القمر) من ظاهر ما سيق إلخ كلمة "ما" عبارة عن المدلول والحكم، والمراد بالظاهر ما يقابل المعنى أي النظم لا ما يقابل الخفي، أي فهو استنباط المجتهد وإثبات الحكم من نظم مدلول سيق الكلام لأجله. (القمر) والمراد إلخ يعني أن المراد ههنا من كون الكلام مسوقاً له أن يدلّ عليه مطلقًا، فهذا السوق أعم من السوق الذي يكون إلخ، وهذا على اصطلاح الجمهور خلافاً لصدر الشريعة؛ فإنّه شرط في عبارة النص السوق الذي يكون في النص المقابل للظاهر . (القمر) مقصودًا أصليًا أي يكون السوق بالذات له. (القمر) أولاً. فإذا تمسك أحد لإباحة النكاح بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَانْكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُمْ (النساء: ٣)﴾ كان عبارة النص وإن لم يكن نصاً فيه بل ظاهرًا، بخلاف العدد؛ فإنّه نص فيه.
Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division: it is the essence of the expression of the text (‛ibārat al-naṣṣ), and what is established by it is the ruling established by the expression of the text. Inference is the transition from the effect to the cause or vice versa, and the latter is intended here. Al-naṣṣ is the wording of the Qur’an, broadly encompassing that it may be naṣṣ (explicit), ẓāhir (apparent), mufassar (explained), or khāṣ (specific). This usage is common in the custom of jurists without objection; hence it came in the definition: 'That for which the speech is directed, not that for which the text is directed, and the work (‛amal) is the work of the mujtahid, meaning deduction, not the work of the limbs.' Thus the meaning becomes: the transition of the mind from the wording of the Qur’an to the ruling is the mujtahid’s deduction from the apparent meaning for which the speech is directed. And the intended meaning of 'directed' (sūq) here is broader than that which is in the naṣṣ, for the sūq in the naṣṣ is what is primarily intended, and in ‛ibārat al-naṣṣ what was primarily intended is the essence of ‛ibārat al-naṣṣ. So the wording is called naṣṣ or ẓāhir with respect to the speech itself, and is called ‛ibārat al-naṣṣ with respect to the inference of the inferrer; the essence is one, and the difference is by consideration. Similarly is the difference between ishārat (indication), ẓāhir, and naṣṣ. Then know that this is according to the opinion of the commentator (al-shāriḥ), but according to others, the wording is called the indicator (al-dāll) by ‛ibārat al-naṣṣ and the indicator by ishārat al-naṣṣ, etc. And the indication is called ‛ibārat al-naṣṣ and ishārat al-naṣṣ, etc., as has passed earlier, or vice versa, i.e., the transition from the cause to the effect. (al-Qamar) And 'the latter': i.e., the transition from the cause to the effect. 'It is the wording of the Qur’an': i.e., the intended meaning of naṣṣ is not what is the counterpart of ẓāhir, but rather the intended meaning is the wording of the Qur’an, and ‛ibārat al-naṣṣ is identical to the naṣṣ, so the addition is of the type of addition in 'the same thing'. (al-Qamar) 'And this usage': i.e., the usage of naṣṣ for the wording of the Qur’an. (al-Qamar) 'Hence it came': i.e., because the intended meaning of naṣṣ is the wording, it came in the definition, etc. For if the intended meaning of naṣṣ was what was mentioned earlier, then defining it as 'speech' would be a definition by the more general, and that is not permissible, as Ibn al-Malak said. 'Hence it came', etc.: i.e., to indicate that the intended meaning of naṣṣ here is the absolute Qur’an, not what the people have conventionally agreed upon. (al-Sanbalī) 'So it is deduction', etc., as it is said: 'Prayer is obligatory' because of His words: 'Establish prayer' (al-An‛ām 72). (al-Qamar) 'From the apparent of what the speech is directed for', etc.: the word 'mā' refers to the indication and the ruling, and the intended meaning of 'apparent' is what is opposite to meaning, i.e., the wording, not what is opposite to hidden. So it is the mujtahid’s deduction and establishment of the ruling from the wording of the indication for which the speech is directed. (al-Qamar) 'And the intended meaning', etc.: means that the intended meaning here of the speech being directed is that it indicates it absolutely, so this sūq is broader than the sūq that is, etc. This is according to the terminology of the majority, contrary to Ṣadr al-Sharī‛ah, for he stipulates for ‛ibārat al-naṣṣ the sūq that is in the naṣṣ as opposed to ẓāhir. (al-Qamar) 'Primarily intended': i.e., the sūq is for it essentially. (al-Qamar) First: if someone argues for the permissibility of marriage using His words: 'Marry what is lawful for you' (al-Nisā’ 3), that is ‛ibārat al-naṣṣ, even if it is not naṣṣ (explicit) for it, but rather ẓāhir (apparent), unlike the number [of wives], for it is naṣṣ for it.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of one of the four types of textual inference in Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh: ʿibārat al-naṣṣ (the expression of the text). The author begins by stating that the fourth division is the essence of ʿibārat al-naṣṣ, and the ruling established by it is the ruling established by the expression of the text. He then clarifies that the term 'naṣṣ' here is used broadly to refer to the wording of the Qur'an, which may be explicit (naṣṣ), apparent (ẓāhir), explained (mufassar), or specific (khāṣ). This usage is common among jurists. The author then quotes a definition: 'That for which the speech is directed, not that for which the text is directed, and the work is the work of the mujtahid meaning deduction.' The commentary (by al-Qamar and al-Sanbalī) expands on this: ʿibārat al-naṣṣ is the meaning that the speech is primarily intended to convey, even if the wording itself may not be exclusively explicit for that meaning. For example, the verse 'Marry what is lawful for you' is ʿibārat al-naṣṣ for the permissibility of marriage, even though it is not explicit (naṣṣ) for that permissibility but rather apparent (ẓāhir). However, the number of wives (two, three, four) is explicitly mentioned and is thus naṣṣ for that number. The passage also notes a disagreement with Ṣadr al-Sharīʿah, who restricts ʿibārat al-naṣṣ to only those meanings that the speech is primarily intended to convey, whereas the majority (jumhūr) include primary and secondary intended meanings.

Key Terms
عبارة النص (ʿibārat al-naṣṣ): The expression of the text - the meaning that the text is directed to convey, either primarily or secondarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling on using the verse 'Marry what is lawful for you' to prove the permissibility of marriage?
A) It is not valid because the verse is not explicit about permissibility.
B) It is ʿibārat al-naṣṣ for permissibility, even though it is only ẓāhir.
C) It is ishārat al-naṣṣ because the permissibility is not the primary intent.
D) It is dalālat al-naṣṣ because the permissibility is understood by necessary implication.

Key Takeaway
ʿIbārat al-naṣṣ is the direct meaning intended by the text, encompassing both explicit and apparent indications, and is a primary tool for Hanafi istinbāṭ.

Defining Ishārat al-Naṣṣ and Its Distinctions
Defines ishārat al-naṣṣ with exclusionary clauses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of ishārat al-naṣṣ, distinguish it from ʿibārat al-naṣṣ, dalālat al-naṣṣ, and iqtiḍāʾ al-naṣṣ, and identify the source of each type of textual inference.

Core Idea
Ishārat al-naṣṣ is a textual indication from wording, not intended, and weaker than ʿibārat but still binding.

In the previous lesson, you learned about ʿibārat al-naṣṣ—the explicit intended meaning. Now we turn to ishārat al-naṣṣ: a meaning that springs from the wording itself but is not the primary purpose of the text. This lesson will walk you through the precise definition given by the author and the marginal clarifications by al-Qamar, and show you how to distinguish ishārat from other types of textual inference.

Guided Path
1
Unpack the Definition
The author defines ishārat al-naṣṣ as acting upon what is established 'by its wording linguistically, but it is not intended, nor is the text directed to it, and it is not apparent from every aspect.' Identify each component: 'by its wording' (naẓm), 'linguistically' (lughatan), 'not intended' (ghayr maqṣūd), 'not directed' (lā musāq lahu), and 'not apparent' (lā ẓāhir).
These components serve to exclude other types of dalālah.
2
Exclude Dalālat al-Naṣṣ
The phrase 'by its wording' excludes dalālat al-naṣṣ because implication is based on the meaning of the wording, not the wording itself. Dalālat al-naṣṣ is derived from the maʿnā (meaning) of the naẓm, not the naẓm directly.
Example: The verse forbidding saying 'uff' implies prohibition of hitting through understanding the severity, but that is dalālat al-naṣṣ.
3
Exclude Iqtiḍāʾ al-Naṣṣ
The term 'linguistically' excludes iqtiḍāʾ al-naṣṣ because iqtiḍāʾ is not established by language but by sharʿ (law) or ʿaql (reason). Iqtiḍāʾ is a meaning necessary for the validity of the wording, such as the requirement of 'touch' for 'ablution' in the verse.
Iqtiḍāʾ relies on external necessity, not linguistic structure.
4
Clarify 'Not Intended'
The author says 'not intended' means not primarily intended. It could be not intended at all (then it is not ʿibārat) or intended but not primarily (then it may be part of ʿibārat but secondary). The gloss explains that if it is not intended at all, it is purely ishārat; if it is intended but not primarily, it is still considered ishārat because the primary sūq (directedness) is for another meaning.
The example from the marriage verse: the permissibility of marriage is ʿibārat (primary intent), the number is naṣṣ (primary intent), but the gloss clarifies that the number is not ishārat.
5
Apply the Definition to an Example
Consider the verse (Quran 4:3): 'Marry what is good for you of women, two, three, or four.' The permissibility of marriage is ʿibārat al-naṣṣ. The number is explicit (naṣṣ) for the count, not ishārat. But suppose a hadith states: 'The best of you are the best to their families.' The explicit meaning is praise; the ishārat might indicate the recommendation of good treatment, since it is derived from the wording linguistically but is not the primary intent.
This example helps solidify the boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاستدلال بإشارة النص فهو العمل بما ثبت بنظمه لغةً لكنه غير مقصود، ولا سيق النص له، وليس بظاهر من كل وجه، فقوله: "بنظمه" شامل للعبارة والإشارة، ولكن تخرج به دلالة النص؛ لأنه ليس بثابت بالنظم بل بمعنى النظم. وقوله: "لغة" يخرج به المقتضى؛ لأنه ليس بثابت لغةً بل شرعًا أو عقلاً. وقوله : "لكنه غير مقصود ولا سيق له النص" من أولاً : أي لا يكون مقصودًا أصليًا، وهذا أعم من أن لا يكون مقصوداً أصلاً أو يكون مقصودًا لكنه لا يكون مقصودًا أصليًا، هذا بحسب ظاهر العبارة، لكن ما لا يكون مقصوداً أصلاً ليس بعبارة النص؛ فلابد الصرف عن ظاهر العبارة، فيقال: إن معنى قوله أولا أو يكون مقصودًا لا أصليًا بأن يكون السوق لمعنى آخر بالذات ويكون السوق لهذا المعنى بالعرض بأن يقصد هذا المعنى باللفظ لغرض إتمام معنى آخر، فإذا تمسك إلخ. فيه أي في إباحة النكاح؛ لأنّ هذا القول ليس مسوقا لهذه الإباحة بالذات.(القمر) بل ظاهرًا إلخ: لأنه علم إجازة النكاح بهذا الكلام بنفس سماعه، فصار ذلك ظاهرًا في حق الإطلاق، نصا في بيان العدد أي اثنين اثنين وثلاثة ثلاثة وأربعة أربعةٍ. وإنما ذكر العدد في الآية مكرّرًا ليصيب كل ناكح يريد الجمع ما العدد كما تقول: اقتسموا هذا المال در همین در همين وثلاثة ثلاثة، ولو أفردت لم يكن له معنى؛ لأنّ الخطاب للجماعة، فصار المعنى لينكح جميعكم اثنين وثلاثة وأربعة، ولا معنى لذلك (السنبلي) فإنّه نص فيه: فإنّ العدد مقصود أصلي لهذا القول، سيق هذا القول له قصدًا أصالة، فصار هذا القول نصا في العدد.(القمر) لكنه أي لكن ما ثبت بنظم النص لغةً غير مقصود أي من النظم وهذا تعرّض لجانب المعنى يعني مقصود منه. (القمر) ولا سيق له أي لما ثبت بنظم النص لغة النص، وهذا تعرّض لجانب اللفظ أن لفظه غير مسوق لمعناه. (القمر) وليس أي ما ثبت بنظم النص لغة. أراد من أن معناه غير يعني شامل إلخ فإنّ في العبارة والإشارة كليهما عملاً بما ثبت بنظم النص. (القمر) يخرج به المقتضى على صيغة اسم المفعول، ثم فيه أنه يلزم حينئذ إخراج الخارج؛ لأن اقتضاء النص يخرج من قول المصنف يالله بنظمه؛ لأن المستدل إن لم يستدل بالنظم بل بالمعنى، فإن كان ذلك المعنى مفهوما منه لغةً فهو دلالة النص، وإلا فإن توقف عليه صحة النظم شرعًا أو عقلاً فهو اقتضاء النص على ما مرّ سابقا. (القمر)
As for inference by ishārat al-naṣṣ (indication of the text), it is acting upon what is established by its wording (naẓm) linguistically, but it is not intended, nor is the text directed to it, and it is not apparent (ẓāhir) from every aspect. His statement 'by its wording' includes both ʿibārat (expression) and ishārat (indication), but it excludes dalālat al-naṣṣ (implication of the text) because it is not established by the wording but by the meaning of the wording. His statement 'linguistically' excludes iqtiḍāʾ al-naṣṣ (requirement of the text) because it is not established linguistically but rather by sharʿ (law) or reason. His statement 'but it is not intended nor is the text directed to it' from the first: i.e., it is not primarily intended. This is broader than it not being intended at all or being intended but not primarily intended, according to the apparent wording. However, what is not intended at all is not ʿibārat al-naṣṣ, so one must divert from the apparent expression. It is said that the meaning of his statement 'first' or 'it is intended but not primarily' is that the sūq (directedness) is for another meaning essentially and the sūq for this meaning is accidentally, in that this meaning is intended by the wording for the purpose of completing another meaning. So 'if someone argues' etc. 'in it' means in the permissibility of marriage, because this statement is not directed essentially to this permissibility. (al-Qamar) 'But ẓāhir', etc.: because the permissibility of marriage is known from this very speech upon hearing it, so it becomes ẓāhir with respect to the absolute [permissibility], and explicit (naṣṣ) in explaining the number, i.e., two, two, three, three, four, four. The number is mentioned repeatedly in the verse so that every person who wants to combine [wives] may know the number, as you say: 'Divide this money one, one, two, two, three, three.' If you said it individually it would have no meaning because the address is to the group. So the meaning becomes: 'All of you marry two, three, and four', and that has no meaning. (al-Sanbalī) 'It is explicit (naṣṣ) for it': for the number is the primary intended meaning of this statement; the statement is directed to it essentially, so it becomes explicit (naṣṣ) for the number. (al-Qamar) 'But it' means that what is established by the wording of the text linguistically is not intended i.e., from the wording, and this is an exposition of the aspect of meaning, meaning it is not intended. (al-Qamar) 'Nor is the text directed to it' i.e., to what is established by the wording of the text linguistically, and this is an exposition of the aspect of wording, that its wording is not directed to its meaning. (al-Qamar) 'And it is not' i.e., what is established by the wording of the text linguistically. He intended that its meaning is not [apparent]. That is, 'includes' etc. (al-Qamar) 'It excludes' i.e., it excludes iqtiḍāʾ al-naṣṣ in the passive participle form. Then it is said that this would require excluding what is excluded, because iqtiḍāʾ al-naṣṣ is excluded from the author's statement 'by its wording', because the inferrer, if he does not infer by the wording but by the meaning, and if that meaning is understood linguistically from it, then it is dalālat al-naṣṣ; otherwise, if the validity of the wording depends on it by sharʿ or reason, then it is iqtiḍāʾ al-naṣṣ, as has passed earlier. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage defines ishārat al-naṣṣ (the indication of the text) as a type of textual inference. The author states that ishārat al-naṣṣ is acting upon a meaning that is established by the wording of the text from a linguistic perspective, but that meaning is not the intended meaning of the text, nor is the text directed to it, and it is not apparent (ẓāhir) from every angle. The marginal glosses by al-Qamar clarify the terms: 'by its wording' includes both ʿibārat al-naṣṣ and ishārat al-naṣṣ, but excludes dalālat al-naṣṣ (implication) because the latter is based on the meaning of the wording, not the wording itself. 'Linguistically' excludes iqtiḍāʾ al-naṣṣ (requirement of the text) because that is established by law or reason, not by language. 'Not intended' means not primarily intended; it could be unintended or intended but not primarily. The passage then discusses the example from the previous lesson: using the verse 'Marry what is lawful' for the permissibility of marriage. The permissibility is ʿibārat al-naṣṣ (as established earlier), but the number of wives is ishārat al-naṣṣ? Actually the text clarifies that the number is naṣṣ (explicit), not ishārat. The permissibility is ẓāhir, and the number is the primary intent. Then it further distinguishes between ishārat al-naṣṣ and dalālat al-naṣṣ and iqtiḍāʾ al-naṣṣ. The key distinction: ishārat al-naṣṣ is from the wording itself, linguistically, but not intended; dalālat al-naṣṣ is from the meaning inferred from the wording, while iqtiḍāʾ al-naṣṣ is a meaning necessary for the validity of the wording by law or reason.

Key Terms
إشارة النص (ishārat al-naṣṣ): Indication of the text: a meaning that is linguistically derived from the wording but not intended by the speaker.
اقتضاء النص (iqtiḍāʾ al-naṣṣ): Requirement of the text: a meaning that is not linguistically indicated but is necessary for the validity of the text by law or reason.
دلالة النص (dalālat al-naṣṣ): Implication of the text: a meaning inferred from the meaning of the wording, not the wording itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key distinction between ishārat al-naṣṣ and iqtiḍāʾ al-naṣṣ?
A) Ishārat is based on the wording, while iqtiḍāʾ is based on the meaning.
B) Ishārat is established linguistically, while iqtiḍāʾ is established by law or reason.
C) Ishārat can be overridden by iqtiḍāʾ.
D) There is no distinction; they are the same.

Key Takeaway
Ishārat al-naṣṣ is a Hanafi category of textual inference: a ruling derived from the wording linguistically but not as the primary intent, occupying a middle strength between explicit meaning and implication.

8 min
Indication of the Text (Isharat al-Nass) and Its Ruling
Defining Isharat al-Nass and comparing it with Ibarat al-Nass

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define Isharat al-Nass, distinguish it from Ibarat al-Nass, and apply the Hanafi rule that ibara overrides ishara when they conflict in deriving a ruling.

Core Idea
Isharat al-Nass is a definitive proof, but explicit wording overrides it in direct conflict.

This lesson introduces a key concept in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: Isharat al-Nass (indication of the text). Unlike explicit wording (Ibarat al-Nass), which directly states a ruling, ishara derives an implied meaning from the text's arrangement. Both are considered definitive proofs, but understanding how they interact is critical for correct istinbat. We will explore the definition, see an example from the Qur'an, and examine a famous dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i over the maximum menstruation period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of Isharat al-Nass
Read the commentary starting with 'Because the meaning...' to the end of the first paragraph. Note how the Qur'anic verse (2:233) uses the phrase 'al-mawludu lahu' (the one for whom the child is born) to indicate the father's responsibility, even though the primary purpose is to speak about maintenance for mothers. This secondary, understood meaning is the ishara.
The lam of specification (لام الاختصاص) is the key linguistic marker that generates the indication.
2
Understand the two rulings derived from the indication
From the indication, the author extracts two fiqh rulings: (1) the father may take from his child's property if in need, and (2) no one else shares the father's obligation to provide maintenance. Note how these are not explicit but flow from the implication of the wording.
These rulings are considered qat'i because they are necessarily understood from the nazm (arrangement).
3
Compare ibara and ishara in authority
The passage states: 'Both explicit wording and indication are definitive in indicating the intended meaning, but explicit wording is given preponderance over indication at the time of conflict.' That is the Hanafi position: both are qat'i al-dalalah, but ibara is superior when they directly contradict.
This is a crucial principle of tarjih: ibara > ishara.
4
Work through the conflict example (menstruation)
The hadith about women's deficiency is primarily about their religious deficiency, but it indicates (ishara) that maximum menstruation is 15 days because the word 'shatr' implies half a month. Imam al-Shafi'i took this as proof. However, the Hanafis cite another hadith that explicitly states the maximum is 10 days (ibara). Because the explicit wording is direct, it overrides the indication. Thus, the Hanafi ruling is 10 days.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is whether blood after 10 days is considered menstruation or istihadah.
5
Confirm that ishara can be general (amm)
The passage concludes that ishara can be general (amm) just like ibara, since both are established by the same nazm. This means that ishara can be specified (takhsis) by other evidence, just like an explicit general statement.
This shows that ishara is not always narrow; it has the same capacity for generality as explicit wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان إشارة النص: لأنّ المعنى وعلى الذي ولد الولد لأجله رزق الوالدات وكسوتهن، فالنسبة إليه بلام المولود له الاختصاص يعرف به أنّ الأب هو الذي اختص بهذه النسبة، بخلاف لفظ الولد والأب، فإنه لا يدلّ على هذا المعنى؛ إذ ليس فيه لام الاختصاص، وكذا يشير هذا إلى أنّ للأب حق التملك فى مال ولده عند الحاجة؛ لأنه مملوكه، وإلى أنّه لا يشارك الوالد أحدٌ في نفقة ولده كما لا يشاركه في هذه النسبة أحد، على ما فصلنا كل ذلك في "التفسير الأحمدي". وهما سواء في إيجاب الحكم إلا أنّ الأول أحق عند التعارض، يعني أن كلا من العبارة والإشارة قطعي الدلالة على المراد، لكن ترجّح العبارة على الإشارة وقت التعارض. مثاله: قوله عليه في حق النساء: "إنهن ناقصات عقل ودين" قلن: وما نقصان عقلنا وديننا؟ قال: "أليس شهادة النساء مثل نصف شهادة الرجال؟" قلن: بلى، قال: "فذلك من نقصان عقلها"، ثمّ قال: "تقعد إحداكن شطر دهرها في قعر بيتها، لا تصوم ولا تصلّي" قلن: بلى، قال: "فذلك من نقصان دينها"، فالحديث وإن كان مسوقا لنقصان دينهنّ، لكنه يفهم منه إشارةً أنّ أكثر الحيض خمسة عشر يوما؛ لأنّ لفظ الشطر موضوع للنصف في أصل اللغة، وبه تمسك الشافعي في أن أكثر الحيض خمسة عشر يوما، ولكنّه معارض بما روي أنه قال: "أقل الحيض للجارية البكر والتيب ثلاثة أيام ولياليهن، وأكثره عشرة أيام"؛ لأنه عبارة في هذا المعنى فرجحت على الإشارة. وللإشارة عموم كما للعبارة؛ لأنّ كلا منهما ثابت بنفس النظم، فيحتمل أن يكون كل منهما خاصا وأن يكون عاما مخصوص البعض وغيره.
Explanation of Indication of the Text: Because the meaning [of the verse] is: 'And upon the one to whom the child is born is the provision of the mothers and their clothing.' So the attribution to him with the lam of the one for whom the child is born indicates specification, by which it is known that the father is the one specified by this attribution, unlike the words 'child' and 'father,' for they do not indicate this meaning, since they do not have the lam of specification. Likewise, this indicates that the father has the right of ownership over his child's property in need, because the child is his possession, and that no one shares with the father in the maintenance of his child just as no one shares with him in this attribution, as we detailed all that in al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi. And they are equal in establishing the ruling, except that the first (explicit wording) has priority in case of conflict, meaning that both explicit wording and indication are definitive in indicating the intended meaning, but explicit wording is given preponderance over indication at the time of conflict. Example: His statement (peace be upon him) regarding women: 'They are deficient in intellect and religion.' They said: 'What is the deficiency of our intellect and our religion?' He said: 'Is not the testimony of women like half the testimony of men?' They said: 'Yes.' He said: 'That is from the deficiency of her intellect.' Then he said: 'One of you sits half (shatr) her life in the depths of her house, not fasting nor praying.' They said: 'Yes.' He said: 'That is from the deficiency of her religion.' So the hadith, although it is presented for the deficiency of their religion, is understood by indication that the maximum of menses is fifteen days, because the word 'shatr' in the original language means half. And al-Shafi'i adhered to this for the view that the maximum of menses is fifteen days, but it is contradicted by what was narrated that he said: 'The minimum of menses for the virgin and the non-virgin is three days and their nights, and the maximum is ten days'; because it is explicit wording in this meaning, so it is given preponderance over the indication. And indication has generality just as explicit wording has, because both are established by the same arrangement (nazm), so it is possible that each is specific or general, and the general is specified by some [evidence] etc.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage defines Isharat al-Nass (indication of the text) as a type of textual proof. It begins by explaining the meaning of the Qur'anic verse (2:233) concerning maintenance for mothers, showing that the phrase 'al-mawludu lahu' (the one for whom the child is born) indicates that the father is specifically responsible—this is an indication (ishara), not the explicit wording (ibara). The passage then lists two rulings derived from this indication: (1) the father has the right to take from his child's property when in need, because the child is his possession; (2) no one shares with the father in the obligation of maintenance, just as no one shares in the attribution. Both ibara and ishara are definitively indicative (qat'i al-dalalah) of the intended meaning, but when they conflict, explicit wording takes precedence over indication. The example provided is the hadith about women's deficiency in intellect and religion, which is explicitly about deficiency but also indicates that the maximum menstruation period is fifteen days because the word 'shatr' (half) refers to half a month. Imam al-Shafi'i used this as proof for a fifteen-day maximum, but the Hanafis counter with a hadith stating that the maximum is ten days, which is explicit wording (ibara) and therefore preponderates. The passage concludes by affirming that ishara can be general ('amm) just like ibara, since both are established by the same arrangement of words.

Key Terms
إشارة النص (Isharat al-Nass): An implied meaning derived from the arrangement of the text, not the primary intended meaning, but still considered a valid definitive proof.
عبارة النص (Ibarat al-Nass): The explicit, primary meaning of the text which is directly expressed by the wording.
التعارض (al-Ta'arud): Conflict between two proofs where both appear to give different rulings; here specifically between ibara and ishara.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul, what is the status of Isharat al-Nass (indication)?
A) It is a zanni (probable) proof and cannot establish a definitive ruling.
B) It is a qat'i (definitive) proof like Ibarat al-Nass, but Ibarat takes precedence in case of conflict.
C) It is not a valid proof because it is not explicit.
D) It is valid only when there is no explicit text.

Key Takeaway
Isharat al-Nass is a definitive proof in Hanafi usul, but it is overridden by explicit wording (Ibarat al-Nass) when they directly conflict.
