Differential between Textual Indication (Dalāla al-Naṣṣ) and Explicit Indication (ʿIbāra)
Defines and applies textual indication with three examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a case of dalāla al-naṣṣ, distinguish it from ʿibāra, and apply the Hanafi principle to extend a ruling to new cases sharing the same effective cause.

Core Idea
Dalāla al-naṣṣ extends a ruling to all cases sharing the text's effective cause, even beyond its explicit wording.

In Hanafi usul, understanding how a text indicates its ruling is key. The explicit wording (ʿibāra) gives the direct ruling, but the text also carries an implicit indication (dalāla) that reaches cases with the same underlying reason. This lesson unpacks that concept through three classical examples and the famous dispute over expiation (kaffāra) for breaking the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three examples
Locate the three cases the author uses to illustrate dalāla: stoning of Ma'iz, highway robbery assistants, and expiation for breaking the fast. Note the explicit text and the extended ruling in each.
The first two are brief; the third is detailed with a cross-madhhab dispute.
2
Extract the effective cause (ʿilla) from each
For each example, determine why the ruling was given. The text's underlying rationale is the ʿilla that allows extension to other cases.
E.g., for Ma'iz the ʿilla is being a married adulterer (muḥṣan); for the fasting case it is corrupting the fast (ifsād al-ṣawm).
3
Compare Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the kaffāra example
Shafi'i limits the ʿilla to the act of intercourse itself, rejecting rational extension to eating/drinking. The Hanafi response defends the rational ʿilla derived from the text's purpose.
This contrast shows how same text can yield different scope based on usul assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان دلالة النص بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع لأنّ ماعزًا لله إنّما رُحم؛ لأنه زان محصنٌ لا لأنه ماعز الله أو صحابي؛ فكل من كان رضي عنه كذلك يُرجم. ولكن ثبت الرّجم على كلّ زانٍ محصن بنص آخر أيضا. وإثبات حدّ قطع الطريق على من كان ردءًا لهم بدلالة قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَسْعَوْنَ فِي الْأَرْضِ فَسَادا﴾. (المائدة: 33) ومثال إثبات الكفّارات بالدلالة إثبات الكفّارة على امرأة وطئت عمدًا في نهار رمضان بدلالة نص ورد في الأعرابي حين جامع في رمضان عمدًا، وعلى كلّ من يفعل الجماع سواه؛ لأنه إنما وجبت عليه الكفّارة لفساد صومه، لا لأنه أعرابي مخصوص أو رجل. وإثبات الكفّارة على من أكل أو شرب عمدًا بدلالة هذا النص الوارد في الجماع؛ لأنه إنما وجبت عليه الكفارة لأجل أنّه إفساد للصوم لا لأنه جماع فقط؛ فكل ما فيه إفساد للصوم من الأكل والشرب والوطء تجب فيه الكفّارة غير مختص بالجماع والشافعي له أنكر هذه الدلالة ويقول: لا تجب الكفّارة إلّا بالجماع؛ فالعلة عنده ليس إفساد الصوم بل الجماع فقط؛ ولهذا قالوا: إنّ عَدّ أمثال هذه الأحكام في الدلالة لا يحسن؛ لأن الشافعي له لم يعرف .
Explanation of textual indication. Explanation of the sections of the fourth division: Because Ma'iz ibn Allah was only stoned because he was a married adulterer, not because he was Ma'iz ibn Allah or a Companion; so everyone similar to him is stoned. However, stoning is also established for every married adulterer by another text. And the proof of the hadd for highway robbery is for those who are assistants to them by the indication of His statement: 'and they strive in the land spreading corruption.' (Al-Ma'ida: 33). And an example of the proof of expiations by indication is the proof of expiation on a woman who had intercourse intentionally in Ramadan by the indication of the text that came regarding the Bedouin who had intercourse in Ramadan intentionally, and on everyone who does intercourse besides him; because expiation was obligatory upon him due to the corruption of his fast, not because he was a specific Bedouin or a man. And the proof of expiation on one who ate or drank intentionally by the indication of this text that came regarding intercourse; because expiation was obligatory upon him due to the fact that it is a corruption of the fast, not because it is only intercourse; so everything that involves corruption of the fast—eating, drinking, and intercourse—requires expiation, not limited to intercourse. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, denied this indication and said: Expiation is not obligatory except by intercourse; so the cause in his view is not the corruption of the fast but only intercourse; and therefore they said: counting such rulings under indication is not good because al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, did not recognize it.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work explaining the concept of 'dalala al-nass' (textual indication). The author begins by stating that this is the fourth division of textual indications. He then illustrates with three examples. First, the stoning of Ma'iz: the explicit text (ibara) is about Ma'iz, but the ruling (stoning for zina committed by a muhsan) applies to all such persons by indication, because the effective cause is being a married adulterer, not being Ma'iz. However, the author notes that stoning is also established by another independent text (the verse on stoning, now abrogated in recitation but not in ruling). The second example is the hadd for highway robbery: the Quran verse (5:33) mentions those who 'strive in the land spreading corruption,' which by explicit text applies to armed robbers. By indication, it includes those who assist them (ردء), because they share in the same meaning of causing corruption. The third and most detailed example is the expiation (kaffarah) for intentionally breaking the fast in Ramadan. The well-known hadith about the Bedouin who had intercourse and was told to free a slave, fast two months, or feed sixty poor people is the text. By indication, this ruling extends to a woman who forces intercourse on her husband (since she also corrupts her fast) and to a person who eats or drinks intentionally (since that also corrupts the fast). The effective cause (illa) is the corruption of the fast (إفساد الصوم), not the specific act of intercourse. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, limiting expiation to intercourse only, and the Hanafis respond that the cause is rationally understood and recognized customarily, as even permissible acts like intercourse with one's wife do not require expiation outside of Ramadan. The Shafi'i position is criticized by some Hanafi scholars as not understanding the indication, though the commentator from 'al-Tarjih' defends that it is a difference in ijtihad.

Key Terms
دلالة النص (dalāla al-naṣṣ): The indication of a text that extends the ruling beyond its explicit wording based on a shared effective cause (ʿilla), so that the ruling applies to all cases sharing that cause.
علة (ʿilla): The effective cause or reason behind a legal ruling, derived from the text by understanding its underlying rationale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the effective cause (ʿilla) for the expiation (kaffarah) for intentionally breaking the fast in Ramadan?
A) The act of sexual intercourse
B) The corruption of the fast (ifsad al-sawm) completely
C) The gender of the person (male)
D) The specific person being a Bedouin

Key Takeaway
Dalāla al-naṣṣ allows Hanafi scholars to extend a ruling to all cases that share the text's underlying cause, even if they are not explicitly mentioned, as seen in the expiation example where 'corruption of the fast' is the operative cause.

Dispute on Generality of the Implied Meaning (Muqtada)
Discusses the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on whether the muqtada can be general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the generality of muqtada and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument by identifying the thamarah al-khilaf in a concrete oath case.

Core Idea
The muqtada has no generality for Hanafis because it is a meaning, not a word — only words can be general or particular.

The concept of muqtada arises when a statement requires an implied element for its validity. A key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools is whether this implied meaning can be considered general (umum) like an explicit word. This passage presents both views, their proofs, and the practical consequence in oath cases. Understanding this disagreement sharpens your ability to analyze how legal inferences are drawn from textual implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core disagreement
Determine the exact point of contention between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the muqtada.
The dispute is about whether the muqtada (implied meaning) can be general or particular.
2
State each school's position and its basis
The Hanafi school holds that muqtada has no generality because it is a meaning (ma'na), not a word (lafz). The Shafi'i school holds that it does have generality because it is like an omitted word (mahzuf) that is assumed.
For Hanafis, only words can have linguistic properties like umum and khusus.
3
Analyze the Hanafi evidence
The Hanafi reasoning is that necessity (darurah) is satisfied with a single individual; there is no need to assert more than the minimum required for validity. Thus, the muqtada is only as much as needed.
This is based on the principle that the original state is absence (al-‘adam al-asli) unless there is an indication to the contrary.
4
Apply to the branch case (furu')
In the oath 'If I eat, my slave is free,' the swearer intends a specific food. The Hanafi school says this intention is invalid because the muqtada (the implied food) is not general and cannot be specified. The Shafi'i school allows it because they consider the muqtada general and therefore subject to specification (takhsis).
This is the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the two schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قيل: من أنّ مثاله لم يوجد في النصوص فإنّما هو من قلة التتبع. ولا عموم له عندنا؛ لأنّ العموم والخصوص من عوارض الألفاظ، والمقتضى معنى لا لفظ، وعند الشافعي الله يجري فيه العموم والخصوص؛ لأنه عنده كالمحذوف الذي يقدر. وهذا أصل كبير مختلف بيننا وبينه يتفرع عليه كثير من الأحكام. ولا يقال: إن يقتضي البيع وهو عام للعبيد كلهم؟ لأنا نقول: إنّه في معنى "بع
أي في العبارة
قوله: اعتق عبيدك عني عبيدك عني ثم كن وكيلي بإعتاقهم" ، فالعبيد مذكور صريح في العبارة؛ ولهذا يكون عاماً.
حتى إذا قال: "إن أكلتُ فعبدي حرّ" ونوى طعامًا ما دون طعام
وما قيل إلخ : قال في "الدائر": ومثال التعارض بين الثابت بالاقتضاء والثابت بالدلالة لم أجده. (القمر) ولا عموم له إلخ أي ليس للمقتضى اسم المفعول عموم يكون في الألفاظ العامة حتى يجري فروع العموم من التخصيص والاستثناء بأن يعتبر المقتضى عاما، ثم خصص بالمخصص أو يستثنى منه؛ لأن المقتضى يعتبر لتصحيح مدلول الكلام فلا يزيد ولا ينتقص بل يعتبر بقدر الضرورة. (القمر) لأن العموم إلخ ولأنّ الضرورة ترتفع بإثبات فرد فلا دلالة على إثبات ما ورائه فيبقى على عدمه الأصلي بمنزلة المسكوت عنه. ومعنى العموم المنفي في المقتضى، أنه إذا كان تحته أفراد يجب إثبات جميعها ، ونسب القول بعموم المقتضى إلى الشافعي ، قال في "التلويح" و"التحقيق": أن أنه له عنده أيضا بمعنى لا عموم لا يصح تقدير الجميع، بل يقدّر واحد بدليل. فعند الشافعي له: يجوز نية طعام دون طعام في قوله: "إن أكلتُ فعبدي "حرّ تخصيصا للعام أعني النكرة الواقعة في سياق الشرط؛ لأنّ المعنى لا اكل طعامًا، وعند أبي حنيفة بيه لا يجوز؛ لأنه ليس بعام فلا يقبل التخصيص ولا خلاف في شمول الحكم وشيوعه لكل طعام، بل الشيوع عند
أبي حنيفة له أو كد، لكنه مبني على وجود المحلوف عليه في كلّ صورة لا على عموم المقتضى. (السنبلي) لأن العموم والخصوص إلخ : أشار الشارح بزيادة لفظ الخصوص إلى أنّ الخلاف بيننا وبين الشافعي يله في جريان الخصوص في المقتضى كالخلاف بيننا وبينهم في جريان العموم، فنحن لا نقول بجريانهما فيه وهو يقول بجريانهما فيه. ولم يتعرّض المصنف له لذلك؛ لأنّ ذلك مبني على هذا، فإنّ الخصوص فرع العموم؛ إذ هو قصر العام على بعض مسمّياته بدليل مستقل موصول. لا لفظ أي لا حقيقة ولا تقديراً. (القمر) في معنى وإنما المقتضى هو البيع دون العبيد. (المحشي) حتى إذا قال إلخ تفريع لمسألة فرعية خلافية على أصل كلّي خلاقي، وهو عموم المقتضى عند الشافعي ، وعدمه عندنا. (القمر)
And what is said: that its example is not found in the texts is only from lack of investigation. And it has no generality for us, because generality and particularity are accidents of words, and the muqtada is a meaning, not a word. According to al-Shafi'i, generality and particularity apply to it because it is like an omitted word that is assumed. This is a great principle of disagreement between us and him, with many rulings branching from it. And do not say: the sale implies [the slaves] and it is general for all slaves? For we say: it is in the meaning of ... His statement: "Free your slaves for me, then be my agent in freeing them" — the slaves are explicitly mentioned in the wording, hence it is general. Until when he says: "If I eat, my slave is free" and intends a specific food other than food... And what is said etc.: He said in al-Da'ir: I did not find an example of conflict between the established by requirement and the established by indication. ...
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on whether the muqtada (implied meaning) can have generality (umum). The Hanafi position is that generality and particularity (umum and khusus) are properties of words (alfaz), not meanings. Since the muqtada is a meaning that is not explicitly expressed, it cannot be general; it is only as much as needed to validate the text. The Shafi'i view is that the muqtada is like an omitted word (mahzuf) that is assumed, so it can be general. The text gives two examples: 1) In the statement "a'tiq 'abidaka 'anni" (free your slaves for me), the word 'abid (slaves) is explicit, so it is general—but this is not a case of muqtada. 2) In an oath "in akaltu fa'abdi hurr" (if I eat, my slave is free), if the swearer intends a specific type of food, the Hanafi school says this intention is not valid because the muqtada (the implied food) has no generality to be specified; the Shafi'i school allows it because they consider the muqtada general (since the conditional implies 'any food') and thus can be specified by intention. The commentary clarifies that the disagreement leads to many subsidiary rulings.

Key Terms
مقتضى (muqtaḍā): The implied meaning that is required for the validity of the explicit text; a meaning, not a word.
عموم (ʿumūm): Generality: the property of a word that covers all individuals of its referent.
خصوص (khuṣūṣ): Particularity: the opposite of generality; the restriction of a general word to some of its individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can a person who swears an oath 'If I eat, my slave is free' validly intend a specific type of food (e.g., only bread)?
A) Yes, because the oath implies all food, and intention specifies it.
B) No, because the muqtada (the implied food) is not general and cannot be specified.
C) Yes, because intention is always effective in oaths.
D) No, because oaths require explicit words.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the muqtada (implied meaning) has no generality because it is a meaning, not a word — only words can be general or particular.

The Implied Element is Not General: Hanafi Usul Rule
Explaining the fourth division of text types: the implied element is not general

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the implied element (muqtaḍā) from general terms ('āmm) and apply the Hanafi rule that the implied element is not general, thereby rejecting specification (takhṣīṣ) by intention in oaths and divorce.

Core Idea
The implied element is not general; it is a linguistic necessity, not a deliberate general term.

This passage addresses a key rule in Hanafi usul: the implied element (muqtaḍā) that arises from linguistic necessity is not considered a general term. This has practical consequences for oaths and divorce: intention to specify is not accepted. The Hanafi position is contrasted with the Shafi'i view, which treats the implied element as general or as if explicitly uttered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
State the Hanafi rule: the implied element (muqtaḍā) is not general ('āmm). This rule is derived from linguistic necessity, not from a legal text.
The rule applies to elements that must be assumed for the utterance to be correct.
2
Apply to the Oath Example
In the phrase 'in akaltu ṭa'āman', the implied element is 'eating'. Since 'food' arises from the necessity of eating, it is not a general term. Therefore, the oath is broken by any food, and intention to specify (e.g., only wheat) is not accepted judicially or religiously.
Contrast with Shafi'i: they consider 'food' as general due to the conditional context.
3
Apply to the Divorce Example
For phrases like 'anti ṭāliq' or 'ṭallaqtuka', Hanafis classify them as khabar (news) about a past divorce. The present divorce is established only by shar'i iqtiḍā' (legal implication). Thus, only one divorce is implied, and intention of three or two is ineffective.
Shafi'is treat these as inshā' (performatives), so intention is effective.
4
Distinguish Types of Iqtiḍā'
The author notes that the implied element can be shar'i (legal) or 'aqlī (rational), while the deleted element is linguistic. This refines the understanding of iqtiḍā'.
This distinction explains why the divorce example involves shar'i iqtiḍā'.
5
Evaluate the Dispute
Compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions. Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) in both oaths and divorce. Recognize that the Hanafi position is based on a careful analysis of linguistic and legal implication.
The text strongly favors the Hanafi position as the correct view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان اقتضاء النص
بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع
لا يصدق عندنا لا ديانة ولا قضاء؛ لأنّ طعامًا إنّما ينشأ من اقتضاء الأكل؛ لأنه لا يكون بدون المأكول؛ فلا يكون عامًا، فلا يقبل التخصيص وأما حنثه بكل طعام؛ فإنّما هو لوجود ماهية الأكل، لا لأنّ الطعام عام وإن قال: "إن أكلتُ طعامًا، أولا أكل أكلا" يحنث بكلّ طعام ويصدّق في نية التخصيص ؛ لأنّه ملفوظ حينئذ، ولكن إيراد هذا المثال على قول من يشترط في المقتضى أن يكون شرعيًا مشكل؛ لأنه عقلي، والأولى أن يقال: إنّ المقتضى ما يكون شرعيًا أو عقليا، والمحذوف ما يكون لغويًّا. وكذا إذا قال: أنت طالق أو طلقتك ونوى ثلاثًا، لا يصح تفريع آخر على عدم كون المقتضى عاماً ؛ وذلك لأنّ قوله : " أنت طالق أو طلّقتك" خبر، وهو لا يصح إلا أن يسبق عليه طلاق من جانب الزوج ليكون هذا خبرًا عنه، ولم يسبق الطلاق المنه في الواقع، فلضرورة تصحيح الكلام وصدقه، قدرنا أنّ الزوج قد طلقها قبل ذلك، وهذا إخبار منه، فكأنه قال في الأوّل: "أنت طالق، لأني طلقتك قبل هذا والطلاق المفهوم بحسب اللغة في ضمن قوله : أنت طالق" هو الطلاق الذي هو وصف المرأة لا التطليق الذي هو فعل الزوج، فلا يكون هذا إلّا اقتضاء، فلا تصح فيه نية الثلاث والاثنين. وأما قوله: "طلقتك" فهو وإن كان دالاً على التطليق الذي هو فعل المتكلّم لكنه دال على مصدر ماض لا على مصدر حادث في الحال. فالمصدر الحادث لا يثبت إلّا اقتضاء من الشرع؛ فلم تصح فيه نية اثنين والثلاث وقال الشافعي محليه: يقع ما نوى من الثلاث أو الاثنين؛ لأنه يدل على الطلاق؛
Clarification of the implication of the text. Clarification of the divisions of the fourth division: It is not accepted with us, neither religiously nor judicially, because food only arises from the implication of eating; for it cannot exist without the eaten. Therefore it is not general, so it does not accept specification. As for his oath being broken by every food, that is only due to the existence of the essence of eating, not because food is general. And if he said: 'If I eat food' or 'I will not eat eating', he breaks his oath by every food and is believed in his intention of specification, because it is then explicitly uttered. But presenting this example according to the one who stipulates that the implied element must be shar'i is problematic, because it is rational. The better course is to say: The implied element is whatever is shar'i or rational, and the deleted element is linguistic. Likewise, if he said: 'You are divorced' or 'I divorce you' and intended three, another branch is not valid on the basis that the implied element is not general. That is because his statement 'You are divorced' or 'I divorce you' is news (khabar), and it is only valid if a divorce from the husband preceded it so that this would be news about it, but the intended divorce did not occur in reality. So for the necessity of correcting the speech and its truth, we assume that the husband had divorced her before this, and this is news from him. So it is as if he said in the first: 'You are divorced because I divorced you before this.' The divorce understood linguistically within his statement 'You are divorced' is the divorce that is a description of the woman, not the act of divorcing which is the husband's action. So this is nothing but implication, so intention of three or two is not valid. As for his statement 'I divorce you', although it indicates the act of divorcing which is the speaker's action, it indicates a past infinitive, not an infinitive occurring in the present. So the present infinitive is not established except by implication from the law; therefore intention of two or three is not valid. Al-Shafi'i and his followers said: What he intended of three or two occurs, because it indicates divorce.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the fourth division in the classification of textual implications (iqtiḍā' al-naṣṣ). The author, a Hanafi scholar, explains that the implied element (muqtaḍā) is not considered a general term ('āmm) and therefore cannot be specified (takhṣīṣ) by intention. For example, in an oath 'if I eat food', the implied element is 'eating', and since 'food' is not explicitly general, the oath is broken by any food due to the presence of the essence of eating, not because 'food' is general. The author contrasts this with the Shafi'i view, which treats 'food' as general because it is a indefinite noun in the context of a conditional, which is akin to a negative context. The discussion then moves to statements of divorce ('anti ṭāliq' or 'ṭallaqtuka'). The Hanafi position is that these are news (khabar) about a past divorce, and the present divorce is established only by implication (iqtiḍā') from the law. Therefore, the implied divorce is a single one, and intention of two or three does not affect the occurrence. The Shafi'is, treating these as performatives (inshā'), hold that intention is valid. The passage clarifies that the implied element is not general and does not carry the legal effects of generality.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtiḍā'): The necessary implication of a meaning that is not explicitly stated but required for the utterance to be correct linguistically or legally.
عام ('āmm): A term that according to Hanafi usul is qat'ī (definitive) in its generality when unqualified, but here the implied element is not 'āmm.
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ): The specification of a general term by a specific intention or evidence; not applicable to the implied element.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says 'I divorce you' intending two divorces, according to the Hanafi school, how many divorces occur?
A) Two
B) One
C) None
D) Three

Key Takeaway
The implied element (muqtaḍā) is not general ('āmm) and cannot be specified by intention, a key Hanafi usul rule affecting oaths and divorce.

Iqtida' al-Nass: How Textual Implication Limits Intention in Divorce Phrases
Analyzing textual implication in divorce phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi principle of iqtida' and apply it to distinguish when intention of multiple divorces is valid versus invalid in phrases like 'أنت طالق' and 'أنت بائن'.

Core Idea
The Lawgiver implies only the minimum necessary for truth – extra intention beyond that is ineffective.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the concept of iqtida' (textual implication) plays a crucial role in understanding how certain utterances produce legal effects. When a husband says 'أنت طالق' (you are divorced), the literal meaning is a declarative report that the woman is already divorced. For this report to be truthful, the Lawgiver implies that a prior divorce must have occurred. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi analysis of such implications, showing why only one divorce is effective in some phrases while intention of three is valid in others, and contrasts this with the Shafi'i approach.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of iqtida'
Recognize that iqtida' is the Lawgiver's implication of a necessary prior event to validate a declarative statement. Without this implication, the statement would be false or invalid.
Key: The implication is the minimum required for truthfulness.
2
Apply iqtida' to 'أنت طالق'
The phrase 'أنت طالق' describes the woman as divorced. For this to be true, a prior divorce must be assumed. The minimum implication is one divorce, so intending three does not add effect.
Contrast: The word 'طالق' is a description of the woman, not an act of divorcing.
3
Apply iqtida' to 'أنت بائن'
The phrase 'أنت بائن' reports irrevocability (baynuna). Since baynuna has two types (light and severe), intending the severe (three divorces) specifies one type of the implied genus, which is valid.
This is different from 'أنت طالق' because baynuna is a genus with types, not a simple action.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i treats these phrases as performative (insha'), not declarative, so no iqtida' is needed and intention directly determines the number of divorces.
This leads to different outcomes: in Hanafi, 'أنت طالق' with intent of three gives one divorce; in Shafi'i, three.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a Hanafi husband who utters 'أنت طالق' intending three divorces only effects one, allowing revocation; a Shafi'i husband effects three, making reconciliation impossible without a new marriage.
This difference stems from the usul principle of iqtida' vs. insha'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع بيان اقتضاء النص عليه طلاق من جانب الزوج ليكون هذا خبرًا عنه، الطلاق السابق ولم يسبق الطلاق المنه في الواقع، و فلضرورة تصحيح الكلام وصدقه، قدرنا أنّ الزوج قد طلقها قبل ذلك، وهذا إخبار منه، فكأنه قال في الأوّل: "أنت طالق، لأني طلقتك قبل هذا والطلاق المفهوم بحسب اللغة في ضمن قوله : أنت طالق" هو الطلاق الذي هو وصف المرأة لا التطليق الذي هو فعل الزوج، فلا يكون هذا إلّا اقتضاء، فلا تصح فيه نية الثلاث والاثنين. وأما قوله: "طلقتك" فهو وإن كان دالاً على التطليق الذي هو فعل المتكلّم لكنه دال على مصدر ماض لا على أي لغة لا اقتضاء مصدر حادث في الحال. فالمصدر الحادث لا يثبت إلّا اقتضاء من الشرع؛ فلم تصح فيه نية في الحال اثنين والثلاث وقال الشافعي محليه: يقع ما نوى من الثلاث أو الاثنين؛ لأنه يدل على الطلاق؛ = بحر نقلت شرعًا إلى الإنشائية؛ فيتحقق بها العقود والفسوخ ولا محكي عنه لها؛ فليس ههنا اقتضاء أصلاً، كذا قال العلوم مولانا عبد العلي الله وأما ما وقع في كلام الحنفية من أن هذه الصيغ إنشاءات شرعًا فليس معناه أنها نقلت من الخبرية إلى الإنشائية في الشرع، بل معناه أن صحة مدلولات هذه الألفاظ الخبرية تتوقف على ثبوت هذه الأمور من جهة المتكلّم، فلتصحيح هذه الصيغ يعتبر الشارع هذه الأمور من جهة المتكلم بطريق الاقتضاء؛ فهذه الأمور لما لم تكن ثابتة وقد ثبتت لتصحيح هذه الصيغ سميت هذه الصيغ إنشاءات لهذه الأمور، فتأمل. والطلاق المفهوم إلخ: دفع دخل مقدّر تقريره أن الطلاق مصدر الطالق، فالطالق يدلّ عليه لغةً لا اقتضاء.(القمر) فلا يكون هذا أي ثبوت التطليق من الزوج إلّا اقتضاء، فإن اتصاف المرأة بالطلاق يتوقف شرعًا على تطليق الزوج إياها. (القمر) فلا تصح إلخ : فإن التطليق الواحد يكفي لتصحيح الكلام، والزائد فضل فلا يعتبر في المقتضى. وقال بحر العلوم مولانا عبد العلي له إني لا أفهمه؛ لأنّ القائل لما نوى الطلقات الثلاث فصار هذا القول خبرا عن إيقاع الطلقات الثلاث فلتصيح هذا القول لابد أن يعتبر الطلقات الثلاث، فكأنه أوقع الطلقات الثلاث أولا، وعقد القلب بها ثم حكي عنها بهذا القول. وأما قوله طلقتك إلخ: دفع سؤال، تقريره: أن المفهوم من الطلاق التعليق الذي هو فعل الزوج؛ لأن قوله: " طلقتك" هناك مقدّر ؛ فلا حاجة إلى الاقتضاء؛ لأنّ الدلالة عليه من حيث اللغة لا من حيث الشرع؟ فأجاب بأنه لا يستقيم؛ لأن قوله: "طلقتك" وإن دلّ على المصدر أي التطليق لكنه ماض ولا يكفي؛ فإنّ الضروري ههنا المصدر الحادث وهو لا يثبت إلا اقتضاء لا يثبت إلا اقتضاء من الشرع، الشرع، فافهم. (السنبلي) إلّا اقتضاء لئلا يلغو هذا الكلام. (القمر) بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع بيان اقتضاء النص فتعل نيته فيه، بخلاف قوله: "طلقي نفسك وأنت "بائن" على اختلاف التخريج، يعني تخريج "طلقي نفسك" في صحة الثلاث على حدة، وتخريج "أنت بائن" فيها على حدة. أمّا صحة نية الثلاث تخريج "طلقي نفسك" فهو أنه أمر يدلّ على المصدر لغةً، وهو لفظ فرد يقع على الواحد، أي لا اقتضاء أي المصدر ويحتمل الثلاث عند النية فهو ليس بمقتضى حتى لم يجر فيه العموم، وأما تخريج "أنت بائن" فهو أنّ البينونة نوعان: غليظة وخفيفة، فإذا نوى الغليظة وهو الثلاث فقد نوى أحد محتمليه فتصح ولا يكون هذا من العموم في شيء، ولا يتصوّر مثل هذا في "طلقي نفسك"؛ لأنّ الطلاق إنّما يشتمل على الأفراد من الواحد والاثنين والثلاثة، لا على نوعي في صحة الثلاث أي في صحة نيّة الثلاث (القمر) أمر أي للتفويض وليس بخبر. ويحتمل إلخ: فإنّ الثلاث كل الجنس فهو واحد حكمي (القمر) حتى لم يجر فيه العموم إلخ : تفريع على المنفي، أي لو كان هذا مقتضى لم يجر فيه العموم بل الدلالة فيه على المصدر لغة فيجري فيه العموم. (السنبلي) شرعي وأما تخريج إلخ: هذا دفع دخل مقدّر تقريره : أنّكم قلتم: إنّ المصدر الذي يثبت من المتكلم إنشاء أمر شرعي لا لغوي، فيكون ثابتا اقتضاء؛ فلا تصلح فيه نية الثلاث؛ فكذلك ثبوت البينونة من المتكلم بقوله: "أنت بائن" أمر أيضًا؛ فينبغي أن لا تصح فيه نية الثلاث أيضًا؟ وخلاصة الردّ: إنا سلّمنا أنّ البينونة ثابتة بطريق الاقتضاء، لكن البينونة من حيث هي مشتركة بين الخفيفة وهي التي يمكن رفعها والغليظة هي التي لا يمكن رفعها، وهي الثلاث؛ إذ هي جنس بالنسبة إليهما ونية أحد المحتملين صحيحة في المقتضى، وكذلك نية أحد النوعين. (السنبلي) أن قوله: "أنت بائن" خبر عن البينونة؛ فلابد له من المحكي عنه سابقا، فإذا نوى البينونة فهو أن البينونة إلخ: يعني الغليظة وتتوقف على الطلقات الثلاث كان هذا الكلام خبرًا وحكاية عنها؛ فيقع الطلقات الثلاث. (القمر) نوعان: هذا إذا كان لفظ البينونة موضوعًا للمعنى العام الذي هو الجنس، وأما إذا كان لفظ البينونة موضوعًا لكلّ من البينونتين على حدة كان مشتركا، فعلى كل تقدير ليس نية البينونة الغليظة من قبيل عموم المقتضى، بل هو من قبيل تعين أحد نوعي الجنس أو أحد معنى المشترك وهذا جائز . (القمر) غليظة وهو ما لا يمكن رفعه. (القمر) وخفيفة وهو ما يمكن رفعه (القمر) مثل هذا أي مثل هذا التخريج الذي في "أنت بائن". (القمر) إنما يشتمل إلخ أي لا اختلاف في الطلاق إلّا بالعدد (القمر) لا على نوعي إلخ فإنّه لا يمكن أن يقال: إن الطلاق يتنوع على ما يمكن رفعه وعلى ما لا يمكن رفعه؛ فإن الطلاق لا يمكن رفعه أصلا، كذا في "التوضيح". وههنا بحث؛ فإنّه يمكن تنويع الطلاق إلى ما يوجب الخفّة وإلى ما يوجب الغلظ، وحينئذ يمكن إرادة أحد نوعي الجنس، فتأمل.(القمر)
Statement of the divisions of the fourth division: Statement of the implication of the text. Upon it, divorce from the husband's side so that this becomes a report about him, the prior divorce, and the divorce intended did not occur in reality. And for the necessity of correcting the speech and its truthfulness, we assumed that the husband had divorced her before that, and this is a report from him. It is as if he said initially: 'You are divorced, because I divorced you before this.' And the divorce understood linguistically within his statement 'You are divorced' is the divorce that is a description of the woman, not the act of divorcing which is the husband's action. So this is nothing but implication (iqtida'), and the intention of three or two is not valid in it. As for his statement: 'I divorced you,' although it indicates the act of divorcing which is the speaker's action, it indicates a past infinitive, not an infinitive occurring in the present. The infinitive occurring in the present is only established by implication from the law; so the intention of two or three is not valid in the present. And al-Shafi'i said, may mercy be upon him: What he intended of three or two occurs, because it indicates divorce. = Bahar: It was transferred legally to performative speech; contracts and dissolutions are realized by it, and there is no reported thing for it; so there is no implication at all here, as said by 'Allamah Mawlana 'Abd al-'Ali. And as for what occurred in the Hanafi statements that these formulas are performative in the law, its meaning is not that they were transferred from declarative to performative in the law. Rather, its meaning is that the validity of the meanings of these declarative words depends on the establishment of these matters from the speaker's side. So to validate these formulas, the Lawgiver considers these matters from the speaker's side by way of implication; so these matters, since they were not established and were established to validate these formulas, these formulas are called performatives for these matters. So reflect. And the understood divorce etc.: Repelling an anticipated objection. Its explanation: That divorce is the infinitive of 'the one who divorces', so 'the one who divorces' indicates it linguistically, not by implication (al-Qamar). So this, i.e., the establishment of the act of divorcing from the husband, is nothing but implication. For the woman's being described with divorce depends legally on the husband's divorcing her (al-Qamar). So it is not valid etc.: For a single act of divorcing is sufficient to validate the speech, and the extra is surplus, so it is not considered in the implied thing. And Bahar al-'Ulum Mawlana 'Abd al-'Ali said: I do not understand it, because the speaker, when he intended the three divorces, then this statement becomes a report about the occurrence of the three divorces; so for the validation of this statement, the three divorces must be considered. It is as if he caused the three divorces first, concluded the heart with them, then reported about them with this statement. And as for his statement 'I divorced you' etc.: Repelling a question. Its explanation: That the understood from divorce is the act of divorcing which is the husband's action, because his statement 'I divorced you' is implied there; so there is no need for implication, because the indication of it is from language, not from the law? He answered that it is not correct, because his statement 'I divorced you', although it indicates the infinitive, i.e., the act of divorcing, it is past and insufficient. For what is necessary here is the infinitive occurring in the present, and it is only established by implication from the law. So understand (al-Sanabali). Except implication, lest this speech be vain (al-Qamar). Statement of the divisions of the fourth division: Statement of the implication of the text. So his intention operates in it, unlike his statement: 'Divorce yourself' and 'You are irrevocably divorced' according to the difference in derivation. Meaning: the derivation of 'Divorce yourself' in the validity of three is separate, and the derivation of 'You are irrevocably divorced' in it is separate. As for the validity of the intention of three: The derivation of 'Divorce yourself' is that it is a command that indicates the infinitive linguistically, and it is a singular word that applies to the single, i.e., no implication of the infinitive, and it can possibly mean three upon intention, so it is not an implied thing such that generality would apply in it. And as for the derivation of 'You are irrevocably divorced', it is that irrevocability (baynuna) is of two types: severe and light. So if he intends the severe, which is three, then he intended one of its possibilities, so it is valid, and this is not from generality at all. And such is not conceivable in 'Divorce yourself', because divorce comprises only individuals of one, two, and three, not two types. In the validity of three, i.e., in the validity of the intention of three (al-Qamar). 'Command' means for entrustment, not a report. 'And it can possibly mean three' etc.: For three is the whole of the genus, so it is a virtual single (al-Qamar). 'Such that generality would not apply in it' etc.: A consequence of the negation, i.e., if this were an implied thing, generality would not apply in it, but the indication in it of the infinitive is linguistic, so generality applies in it (al-Sanabali). Legal. And as for the derivation etc.: This is repelling an anticipated objection. Its explanation: You said that the infinitive that is established from the speaker is a performative matter, legal not linguistic, so it is established by implication; therefore the intention of three is not valid in it. Similarly, the establishment of irrevocability from the speaker by his statement 'You are irrevocably divorced' is also a matter; so it should be that the intention of three is not valid in it either? The summary of the response: We concede that irrevocability is established by way of implication, but irrevocability in itself is common between the light, which can be lifted, and the severe, which cannot be lifted, and it is three. For it is a genus with respect to them, and the intention of one of the probabilities is valid in the implied thing, and likewise the intention of one of the two types (al-Sanabali). That his statement 'You are irrevocably divorced' is a report about irrevocability; so it must have a previous reported thing. So if he intends irrevocability—meaning the severe, and it depends on three divorces—then this statement is a report and a narration about it; thus the three divorces occur (al-Qamar). Two types: This is if the word 'irrevocability' is placed for the general meaning which is the genus. And if the word 'irrevocability' is placed for each of the two irrevocabilities separately, it is a homonym. In any case, the intention of severe irrevocability is not from the generality of the implied thing; rather it is from the specification of one of the two types of the genus or one of the meanings of the homonym, and this is permissible (al-Qamar). Severe is what cannot be lifted (al-Qamar). And light is what can be lifted (al-Qamar). 'Such' i.e., such derivation that is in 'You are irrevocably divorced' (al-Qamar). 'Only comprises' etc., i.e., no difference in divorce except by number (al-Qamar). 'Not two types' etc., for it cannot be said that divorce varies into what can be lifted and what cannot be lifted, because divorce cannot be lifted at all. This is in al-Tawdih. And here there is a discussion: for it is possible to vary divorce into what causes lightness and what causes severity, and then it is possible to intend one of the two types of the genus. So reflect (al-Qamar).
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage from Noor al-Anwar is a commentary on the topic of 'iqtida' al-nass' (textual implication) as applied to divorce formulations. The author explains the Hanafi position on when the intention of multiple divorces is valid. The key concept is 'iqtida'' – an implied meaning necessary for the speech to be truthful or valid according to the Lawgiver. In the phrase 'أنت طالق' (you are divorced), the literal meaning is that the woman is already described as divorced. For the statement to be truthful, the husband must have divorced her before. Thus, the Lawgiver implies a prior divorce from the husband to validate the statement (iqtida'). However, this implied divorce is only one, because one is sufficient for the speech to be true; any additional divorces are surplus and not part of the necessary implication. Therefore, if the husband intends three or two divorces, his intention is not effective – only one divorce occurs. In contrast, the phrase 'أنت بائن' (you are irrevocably divorced) also involves iqtida', but here the implied irrevocability (baynuna) has two types: light (which allows revocation) and severe (which does not, i.e., three divorces). Since the word 'بائن' is general or homonymous, when the husband intends the severe type (three divorces), he is merely specifying one of the possible meanings of the implied irrevocability. This is valid in the case of iqtida', so three divorces occur. The Shafi'i position, also mentioned, is that all such phrases are performative (insha') and not declarative, so no iqtida' is needed, making the intention of any number valid. The Hanafi response clarifies that these phrases are legally considered 'performative' only in the sense that the Lawgiver validates them by implying the necessary prior events, not that they have been linguistically transformed. The passage thus illustrates the Hanafi approach to iqtida' and its limitations on intention.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida'): The implication by the Lawgiver of a meaning necessary for the validity or truthfulness of a statement, without which the speech would be false or invalid.
الإنشاء (al-insha'): Performative speech that creates a legal effect immediately, as opposed to declarative speech (khabar) that reports a fact.
الخبر (al-khabar): Declarative speech that reports a past or present fact, susceptible to truth or falsehood.
البينونة (al-baynuna): Irrevocability in divorce; it is of two types: light (baynuna sughra, revocable before iddah ends) and severe (baynuna kubra, requiring three divorces to remarry).
التخريج (al-takhrīj): Derivation or explanation of how a ruling applies to specific cases, here showing why intention of three is valid in 'أنت بائن' but not in 'طلقي نفسك'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife, 'أنت بائن' intending the severe type of irrevocability (three divorces). According to the Hanafi position on iqtida' al-nass, how many divorces occur?
A) One divorce occurs, because iqtida' always implies only one.
B) Three divorces occur, because baynuna is a genus with two types and intending the severe is valid.
C) No divorce occurs, because the statement is invalid without a prior divorce.
D) Two divorces occur, as a compromise.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of iqtida' limits the effect of intention in declarative divorce phrases to the minimum implication; however, when the implied matter is a genus with types, specifying a type is valid.

Refuting Mafhum al-Laqab as Invalid Inference
Refuting the concept of Mafhum al-Laqab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the concept of mafhūm al-laqab as an invalid inference in Hanafi usul and explain why the mention of a name does not negate the ruling from all other things.

Core Idea
A name does not create an exclusive rule; absence of mention does not prove negation.

In this lesson, we examine the first of the 'invalid faces' (al-wujūh al-fāsida) mentioned in the text: the concept of mafhūm al-laqab. Some scholars claim that if a ruling is attached to a specific name or common noun, it implies the ruling is exclusive to that named entity. The Hanafi school rejects this reasoning as invalid, and we will explore why. By the end, you will understand the fundamental distinction between explicit and implicit meaning and how Hanafi usul guards against erroneous inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept being defined
Mafhum al-laqab is the inference that mentioning a specific name (proper or common) implies that the ruling is exclusive to that named thing and negated from all others. It is a claim made by some Ash'aris and Hanbalis.
This is not accepted in Hanafi usul.
2
Understand the Hanafi rejection
The passage labels this as one of the 'invalid faces'. The Hanafi position is that the explicit meaning (mantuq) does not automatically create an implicit negation (mafhum) unless there is evidence. Simply naming something does not exclude others.
The principle: 'Absence of mention does not imply absence of ruling'.
3
Distinguish explicit and implicit meaning
The passage introduces that what is understood from wording can be explicit (ṣarīḥ) or implicit (ghayr ṣarīḥ). Mafhum al-laqab is not explicit; it is a claimed implicit meaning that is unsupported.
The marginal notes expand on types of mantuq.
4
Recognize the scope of 'al-ʿalam'
The commentator clarifies that 'al-ʿalam' here includes both proper names and common nouns (ism jins), broader than the grammatical definition. For example, 'Water is purifying' uses a common noun.
This avoids confusion about what constitutes a 'name'.
5
Analyze the practical consequence
If accepted, mafhum al-laqab would restrict rulings to only the named entities. For instance, 'Water is purifying' would mean only water purifies, limiting legal flexibility.
The Hanafi view preserves the general applicability of many texts.

Chapter on mentioning the invalid faces: Specifying a thing by its proper noun indicates specificity according to some. This is the first of the invalid faces, i.e., ruling on a proper noun indicates negation of it from others according to some. What is meant by proper noun here is the word that indicates the essence without the attribute, whether it is a proper name or a common noun. And 'some' refers to some Ash'aris and Hanbalis. This is called the concept of the proper name (mafhūm al-laqab) in their terminology. The original is the wording, which is the spoken word first, and it is that which is understood from the wording either explicitly or implicitly.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section on invalid methods of inference. The first invalid face is the 'concept of the proper name' (mafhūm al-laqab). According to some Ash'aris and Hanbalis, specifying a thing by its proper name (e.g., 'Zaid' or 'water') implies that the ruling is exclusive to that thing and negated from others. For example, if the Prophet said 'Water is purifying,' they argue it means only water is purifying, not other liquids. The Hanafi position rejects this, considering it a false inference. The commentator clarifies that the term 'proper noun' here includes both proper names and common nouns. The marginal notes explain that this is not the same as the definition of 'specific' (khāṣṣ) in uṣūl, which designates a single meaning. The author then begins to analyze the distinction between explicit and implicit understanding from wording.

Key Terms
مفهوم اللقب (mafhūm al-laqab): The inferred meaning that the mention of a specific name or common noun indicates that the ruling applies only to that named entity and is negated from all others.
العلم (al-ʿalam): In this context, a word that indicates the essence without the attribute, including proper names (like Zaid) and common nouns (like water).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Mafhūm al-laqab

D) ʿUmūm al-balwā

Key Takeaway
Mafhum al-laqab is an invalid inference in Hanafi usul because the mention of a name does not automatically negate the ruling from all other things.

Invalid Ways: Concept of Title and Conditions of Mafhum al-Mukhalafa
Definitions of mafhum types and Hanafi rejection of concept of title

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mafhum al-muwafaqa and mafhum al-mukhalafa, identify the conditions for valid mafhum al-mukhalafa, and explain why Hanafis reject mafhum al-laqab as a valid proof.

Core Idea
Mentioning a noun does not automatically negate the ruling from others; exclusivity requires separate evidence.

This passage introduces the concept of 'mafhum' (understood meaning) and its two types: mafhum al-muwafaqa (agreement) and mafhum al-mukhalafa (opposition). It then focuses on one disputed subtype: mafhum al-laqab (concept of title), which some scholars accept but Hanafis consider invalid. The passage also lists conditions that must be met for mafhum al-mukhalafa to be a valid proof. As a Hanafi usul student, you need to grasp why the school restricts the use of mafhum al-mukhalafa and reject the inference from mere nouns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of mafhum
Locate the definitions of mafhum al-muwafaqa and mafhum al-mukhalafa. Note that the former agrees with the explicit ruling, while the latter gives the opposite ruling for the unmentioned case.
Mafhum al-muwafaqa is like analogy; mafhum al-mukhalafa relies on a qualifier.
2
Understand the dispute over mafhum al-laqab
Recognize that some Ash'aris and Hanbalis argue that naming something with a noun (proper or common) indicates the ruling is exclusive to that entity. The Hanafi position rejects this as one of the 'invalid ways' (wujuh fasida).
The term 'noun' here includes both proper nouns and common nouns when used as labels.
3
State the Hanafi response
Explain the Hanafi reasoning: the noun simply identifies the subject; it does not inherently negate the ruling from others. Additional evidence is needed for exclusivity.
The burden of proof lies on those claiming exclusivity.
4
List the conditions for valid mafhum al-mukhalafa
Extract the five conditions: the unmentioned case must not be more appropriate or equal; the expression must not be customary; it must not be in response to a question/event; it must not be for clarification/praise/blame; it must not serve another benefit.
These conditions are stipulated by those who accept mafhum al-mukhalafa (Hanafis accept some types but apply these conditions too).
5
Apply to the hadith example
Consider the hadith 'Water is from water' used by proponents. Determine if it meets the conditions for valid mafhum al-mukhalafa according to Hanafi standards.
Hanafis would likely find that the hadith is customary or serves another purpose, thus invalidating the inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة
التنصيص على الشيء باسمه العلم يدل على الخصوص عند البعض. هذا وجه أول من الوجوه الفاسدة، أي الحكم على العلم يدلّ على نفيه عن غيره عند البعض. والمراد بالعلم ههنا: هو اللفظ الدال على الذات دون الصفة سواء كان علمًا أو اسم جنس، وبالبعض هو بعض الأشعرية والحنابلة. ويسمى هذا مفهوم اللقب عندهم.
والمفهوم نوعان: مفهوم موافقة وهو أن يفهم من اللفظ حال المسكوت عنه على وفق المنطوق. ومفهوم مخالفة: وهو أن يفهم منه حاله خلاف ما فهم من المنطوق، وهو إن فهم من اسم العلم سمّي مفهوم اللقب، وإن فهم من الشرط أو الوصف سمي مفهوم الشرط أو الوصف على ما سيأتي، ولكنهم اشترطوا أن لا تظهر أولوية المسكوت عنه أو مساواته على المنطوق للمنطوق، ولا يخرج مخرج العادة، ولا يكون لسؤال أو حادثة ولا لكشف أو مدح أو ذمّ، ولا يفيد فائدة أخرى، فحينئذٍ يتعيّن النفي عمّا عداه كقوله عليه السلام: "الماء من الماء".
Section on mentioning the invalid ways.
Explicitly naming a thing by its proper noun indicates specificity according to some. This is the first invalid way: that ruling on a noun indicates its negation from others according to some. What is meant by 'noun' here is the word that indicates the essence without the attribute, whether it is a proper noun or a common noun. And 'some' refers to some Ash'aris and Hanbalis. This is called 'concept of the title' (mafhum al-laqab) with them.
And the understood meaning (mafhum) is of two types: Mafhum muwafaqa (concept of agreement) - that the understood meaning of the unmentioned case follows the manifest meaning. And mafhum mukhalafa (concept of opposition) - that the understood meaning of the unmentioned case is contrary to what is understood from the manifest meaning. If it is understood from a proper noun, it is called 'concept of the title'. If understood from a condition or description, it is called 'concept of condition' or 'concept of description' as will come. But they stipulated that the unmentioned case should not be clearly more appropriate or equal to the manifest case, nor should it be spoken as a customary matter, nor be in response to a question or an event, nor for clarification, praise or blame, nor should it convey another benefit. Only then is negation of the ruling from others established, like his saying (peace be upon him): 'Water is from water.'
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a section from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary discussing invalid ways of deriving rulings. The commentator explains that some scholars (Ash'aris and Hanbalis) claim that explicitly naming something with a proper noun indicates specificity, meaning the ruling is limited to that named thing and negated from others. This is called 'concept of the title' (mafhum al-laqab) and the Hanafis consider it invalid. The passage then defines the two types of mafhum: mafhum muwafaqa (agreement) where the unmentioned case follows the ruling of the mentioned case by a clear ratio (like parents being included in the prohibition of saying 'uff' based on the verse about children), and mafhum mukhalafa (opposition) where the unmentioned case takes the opposite ruling. Mafhum mukhalafa includes subtypes based on the qualifier: from a proper noun (title), from a condition, from a description, etc. The commentator then lists several conditions for mafhum mukhalafa to be considered valid according to those who accept it: the unmentioned case should not be more appropriate or equal in ruling; the statement should not be customary; it should not be a response to a question or event; it should not be for clarification, praise, or blame; and it should not serve another purpose. The example given is the hadith 'Water is from water' (i.e., only water necessitates ablution after sexual dream) - but note: the Hanafis themselves do not consider this hadith as establishing a valid mafhum mukhalafa; this is given as an example used by those who accept such concepts. Throughout, the Hanafi position is that such inferences (especially concept of title) are invalid (wajuh fasida) because they lack proper textual support and lead to arbitrary restrictions.

Key Terms
مفهوم اللقب (mafhum al-laqab): A type of mafhum al-mukhalafa where the opposite ruling is inferred from a proper noun or common noun used as a label.
مفهوم الموافقة (mafhum al-muwafaqa): The unmentioned case follows the ruling due to a shared or stronger cause.
مفهوم المخالفة (mafhum al-mukhalafa): The unmentioned case takes the opposite ruling due to a qualifier (condition, description, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what is the ruling on using 'concept of title' (mafhum al-laqab) to infer exclusivity of a ruling?
A) It is a valid proof and used extensively.
B) It is invalid and considered among the 'invalid ways'.
C) It is valid only if it is a common noun.
D) It is valid only for proper nouns.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafis reject the concept of title (mafhum al-laqab) as an invalid inference for exclusivity; only a condition or description can yield valid mafhum al-mukhalafa under strict conditions.

Hanafi Rejection of Mafhum al-Mukhalafa
Explains and rejects mafhum al-mukhalafa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi rejection of mafhum al-mukhalafa, apply the distinction between mantuq and mafhum, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the example of marrying slave-girls.

Core Idea
The uttered text (mantuq) is stronger than implied meaning; mafhum al-mukhalafa is rejected in Hanafi usul.

This passage from a Hanafi usul text confronts a key methodological difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: the validity of mafhum al-mukhalafa (opposite implication). The author first illustrates how ghusl is made obligatory by the meeting of circumcised parts as a substitute for actual water emission, then pivots to the core dispute. He explains that attaching a ruling to a specific description or condition does not, in the Hanafi view, automatically imply the opposite ruling when that description or condition is absent. The Quranic verses on marriage provide the test case: the general command in 4:3, according to Hanafis, permits marrying slave-girls even when free believing women are available, despite the condition in 4:25.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the Hanafi position (mafhum al-mukhalafa is not a valid proof) and the Shafi'i position (mafhum al-mukhalafa is a valid proof).
The Shafi'i position is presented as relying on the implication of a condition in Quran 4:25.
2
Examine the Shafi'i proof
Analyze how al-Shafi'i derives the ruling from Quran 4:25: the condition 'if you cannot afford free believing women' implies prohibition when you can afford them.
The Shafi'i uses this to forbid marrying a slave-girl when a free woman is available.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi argument: the general command in 4:3 ('marry what is good for you of women') is an uttered text (mantuq) that cannot be specified by a mere implication (mafhum). The uttered text is stronger.
Hanafis argue that specifying a general uttered text by an implied opposite meaning is 'unreasonable' (غير معقول).
4
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference leads to opposite rulings: Hanafis permit marrying a slave-girl even when a free believing woman is available; Shafi'is forbid it.
This is a concrete example of how usul differences affect fiqh.
5
Apply to a new example
Consider a similar text: 'If you are ill, do not fast.' Does this imply that fasting is obligatory when healthy? Hanafis would say no; the general obligation of fasting for the capable is explicit in other verses.
The principle: general explicit commands are not overridden by implied opposites.

Source Text

فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة
بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع الغسل الذي يتعلّق بالشهوة منحصر في الماء، فلا يضر خروج الغسل بالحيض والنفاس؛ لأنّ وجوبه لا يتعلّق بالشهوة، ولكنّ الماء على نوعين مرّةً يكون عيانا بأن ينزل في نفس الأمر في النّوم أو اليقظة بالوطء أو بغيره، ومرّةً يكون دلالة بأن يقام دليله وهو التقاء الختانين مقامه؛ لأنه سبب نزول الماء، ونفسُه تغيب عن بصره، ولعله لم يشعر به لقلّته، فأقمنا السبب مقام المسبب، وأوجبنا الغسل عليه بمجرد الالتقاء احتياطاً.
والحكم إذا أضيف إلى مسمّى هذا ابتداء وجه ثان من الوجوه الفاسدة، وهو يتضمن مفهوم الوصف والشرط، يعني أنّ الحكم إذا أسند إلى شيء موصوف بوصف خاص أو علق بشرط كان دليلاً على نفيه، أي كان كلّ من الوصف والتعليق دالا على نفي الحكم عند عدم الوصف أو الشرط.
عند الشافعي له حتى لم يجوّز نكاح الأمة عند طول الحرّة، ونكاح الأمة الكتابية؛ أي القدرة لفوات الشرط والوصف المذكورين في النص، وهو قوله تعالى: {وَمَنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ مِنْكُمْ طَوْلاً أَنْ يَنْكِحَ الْمُحْصَنَاتِ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ فَمِنْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ مِنْ فَتَيَاتِكُمُ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ} (النساء:25) يعني لم يستطع منكم زيادة وقدرة أن ينكح الحرائر المؤمنات؛ لأجل زيادة مهرهنّ ونفقتهنّ في معاشهنّ، فلينكح مملوكة من مملوكات أيمانكم، أي أيمان إخوانكم؛ إذ لا يجوز نكاح أمة أصلاً من إمائكم المؤمنات، فالله تعالى قد نص على أنه إن لم يستطع الحرّة فلينكح أمة هذا عند الشافعي: وعندنا لا يدلّ فيها. ونحن نقول: إن هذا تخصيص لعموم منطوق قوله تعالى: {فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُمْ مِنَ النِّسَاءِ} (النساء:3) الآية فإنه ينادي بأعلى نداء على أن نكاح الأمة مع طول الحرة، ونكاح الأمة الكتابية جائز، ولا مرية في أن تخصيص العام المنطوق بمفهوم المخالفة غير معقول؛ لأن المنطوق أقوى، فالحق عندنا أنه لا دلالة للمنطوق على المسكوت، فالدليل الخارجي إذا كان يحكم فيه بحكم موافق أو مخالف للمنطوق يحكم هنالك بذلك الحكم، وإلّا يبقى على أصله.
Chapter on mentioning the invalid aspects. Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division: the ghusl that is connected to desire is confined to water, so the emission of ghusl due to menses or postpartum does not harm because its obligation is not connected to desire. But water is of two types: sometimes it is actual, such as when it descends in reality during sleep or wakefulness by intercourse or otherwise; and sometimes it is indication, such as when its evidence is established, which is the meeting of the two circumcised parts, in its place because it is a cause of water descending, and the water itself is hidden from his sight, and perhaps he does not perceive it due to its scarcity, so we established the cause in place of the effect, and made ghusl obligatory upon him merely by the meeting as a precaution. And when a ruling is attributed to a named thing, this is the beginning of a second invalid aspect, and it includes the concept of the description and condition, meaning that when a ruling is based on something described with a specific description or attached to a condition, it is evidence for its negation, i.e., each of the description and condition indicate the negation of the ruling when the description or condition is absent. According to al-Shafi'i, he held that it is not permissible to marry a slave-girl when a free woman is available, nor a Kitabi slave-girl; i.e., due to the loss of the condition and description mentioned in the text, which is His saying: {And whoever among you does not have the means to marry free believing women, then from what your right hands possess of your believing slave-girls} meaning whoever among you does not have extra wealth and ability to marry free believing women due to the increase of their dowries and expenses, then let him marry a bondmaid from the bondmaids of your right hands, i.e., the right hands of your brothers; for it is not permissible to marry one's own slave-girl at all, from your believing slave-girls. So Allah has stated that if he cannot afford a free woman, then let him marry a slave-girl; this is according to al-Shafi'i: but according to us, it does not indicate that. And we say: This is a specification of the generality of the uttered text of His saying: {Then marry what is lawful to you of women} (4:3) which proclaims loudly that marrying a slave-girl even with the availability of a free woman, and marrying a Kitabi slave-girl, is permissible. And there is no doubt that specifying the general uttered text by implication of the opposite is unreasonable because the uttered text is stronger. So the truth according to us is that the uttered text does not indicate the unspoken. Thus, if an external evidence rules a ruling either agreeing or opposing the uttered text, that ruling is applied there; otherwise, it remains on its original principle.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul work discussing 'invalid aspects' (وجوه فاسدة) of legal reasoning. The author first gives an example from fiqh: ghusl due to sexual desire is caused by water emission. Since actual water emission may go unnoticed, the jurists established the meeting of the circumcised parts (penis and vagina) as a substitute cause (سبب) for the effect (مسبب), making ghusl obligatory upon mere contact. This is a form of qiyas (analogy) based on custom and precaution. Then the author introduces the second invalid aspect: when a ruling is attached to a specific description (وصف خاص) or condition (شرط), some scholars (like al-Shafi'i) argue that it implies the negation of the ruling when that description or condition is absent. This is called 'mafhum al-mukhalafa' (opposite implication). The Hanafi position, presented here, rejects this implication. The proof is the verse about marrying slave-girls (4:25) which al-Shafi'i interprets as forbidding marrying a slave-girl when a free believing woman is available, because the description 'when you cannot afford' is taken to imply that if you can afford, you may not marry a slave-girl. The Hanafi response is that this would be an unreasonable specification of the general command to marry what is good (4:3), which explicitly permits marrying slave-girls even when free women are available. Since the uttered text (منطوق) is stronger than the implied meaning (مفهوم), the opposite implication cannot override the general command. Thus, in Hanafi usul, mafhum al-mukhalafa is not a valid source of law unless supported by external evidence. The author concludes that the ruling stands on its original principle (i.e., permissibility) unless there is another proof.

Key Terms
مفهوم المخالفة (mafhum al-mukhalafa): The legal implication from a text that when a ruling is attached to a description or condition, the opposite ruling applies when that description or condition is absent.
منطوق (mantuq): The explicit, uttered meaning of a text.
وصف خاص (wasf khas): A specific description that restricts a general ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, what is the ruling on marrying a Kitabi (non-Muslim) slave-girl when a free believing woman is available?
A) It is not permissible because the verse (4:25) implies permission only for believing slave-girls.
B) It is permissible because the general command (4:3) allows marrying any woman who is lawful.
C) It is permissible only if the free woman is unavailable.
D) It is not permissible because of a hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects mafhum al-mukhalafa because the explicit text (mantuq) cannot be overridden by an implied opposite meaning. The Quranic example shows that a general command remains general until a valid external evidence specifies it.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
15 min
Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Mafhum al-Mukhalafa: The Case of Marrying Slave Women
Dispute over legal implication of description and condition in Quranic texts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that mantuq (articulated text) overrides mafhum al-mukhalafa, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument in the context of Quran 4:25.

Core Idea
The articulated general text trumps implied negation: mafhum al-mukhalafa cannot restrict a clear command.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute: does a condition or description in a Quranic verse automatically negate the ruling when absent? The Shafi'i school says yes (mafhum al-mukhalafa); the Hanafi school says no when it conflicts with a stronger general text. The example is the permissibility of marrying slave women, where Quran 4:25 ('believing slave women') is pitted against Quran 4:3 ('marry what seems good to you of women').

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position and its basis
Identify the Shafi'i ruling that marrying a slave woman is prohibited when a free woman is available, and marrying a Kitabi slave woman is prohibited. Note that they derive this from the condition (absence of ability to marry free women) and the description (believing) in Quran 4:25, using mafhum al-mukhalafa.
The Shafi'i also equates description with condition in legal effect.
2
Identify the Hanafi counter-proof
The Hanafis cite Quran 4:3 ('Marry what seems good to you of women') as a general articulated text (mantuq) that includes all women, overrding any implied negation from a specific verse.
This is the core of the dispute: general vs. specific, mantuq vs. mafhum.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i supporting arguments
The Shafi'i uses custom (e.g., 'the tall human does not fly' implying non-tall fly) and the principle of maximizing benefit. Understand these arguments.
These are linguistic and rational proofs.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to each argument
The Hanafis respond that the general text is stronger; the custom analogy fails because one can say 'both tall and non-tall do not fly' without ugliness. They also argue that if description negated, it would be a preference without a preponderator.
The Hanafi rejection is not total; they accept mafhum when no stronger text contradicts.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The practical ruling: according to Shafi'i, marrying a slave woman when a free woman is available is prohibited; according to Hanafi, it is permissible (though may be discouraged). Also, Kitabi slave women are permissible in Hanafi but not in Shafi'i.
This shows the real-life impact of the usul principle.
