Degrees of Descriptive Phrases and the Rule of Absolute and Restricted
Classifying descriptive phrases and explaining the principle of carrying absolute to restricted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify descriptive phrases (awsaaf) as coincidental, conditional, or causal, and apply the Hanafi principle of carrying the absolute (mutlaq) to the restricted (muqayyad).

Core Idea
Only conditional and causal descriptive phrases restrict the ruling; coincidental phrases do not.

Descriptive phrases in revelation are not all equal. Some are mere custom, some function as conditions, and some are the very cause of the ruling. Understanding this hierarchy is key to correctly interpreting commands. Beyond that, when an absolute term and a restricted term appear on the same issue, the Hanafi school rules that the absolute is always interpreted in light of the restricted—a principle that shapes many fiqh derivations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three degrees of wasf
Extract from the text the three categories: coincidental (ittifaqi), conditional (shart), and causal ('illa). Note the Quranic example for each.
The examples are: step-daughters (coincidental), believing slave-girls (conditional), thief/fornicator (causal).
2
Determine the legal effect of each degree
For each degree, ask: does the absence of the description negate the ruling? The text says for coincidental, it does not; for conditional and causal, it does (though for causal, another cause may still apply).
The phrase 'fama dunahu awla' means that lower degrees (coincidental, conditional) are even less likely to negate the ruling upon absence.
3
Define absolute (mutlaq) and restricted (muqayyad)
The text defines mutlaq as a term referring to the essence without attributes (e.g., 'raqaba'), and muqayyad as one with a qualifying attribute (e.g., 'raqaba mu'mina'). Understand that the absolute is 'silent' (sakit) and the restricted is 'speaking' (natiq).
The absolute is not inherently ambiguous; it indicates the ruling but is silent on the attribute.
4
Apply the principle: absolute is carried to restricted
When both absolute and restricted terms appear in a shari'ah issue, the absolute is interpreted as the restricted. The text gives the example: 'free a neck' is understood as 'free a believing neck' when the verse on believing slave-girls is present.
This is the Hanafi position; the Shafi'i view differs when the texts are in different incidents.
5
Recognize the scholarly difference (Shafi'i)
The text mentions that according to al-Shafi'i, if the absolute and restricted occur in two different incidents, the absolute is not necessarily carried to the restricted. The Hanafi view is that they must be in the same 'haadithah' (incident) for the carry to apply.
The commentary elaborates on various combinations of ruling and incident.

Section on mentioning the corrupt aspects: And it is that the descriptive phrase (wasf) has three degrees: the lowest is that it is coincidental (ittifaqi) like His statement: '…and your step-daughters who are in your bosoms…' (4:23); the middle is that it is in the meaning of a condition (shart) like His statement: '…and from your believing slave-girls…' (4:25); and the highest is that it is in the meaning of a cause ('illa) like His statement: 'the thief, male and female' and 'the fornicator, male and female' (q.v.). And there is no effect of the negation of the cause on the negation of the ruling, so what is below it (i.e., lower degrees) is even more so. And the absolute (mutlaq) is carried upon the restricted (muqayyad). This is a third type from the corrupt aspects. And the absolute is that which refers to the essence (dhat) like 'a neck' (raqaba) without any affirmative or negative attribute; and the restricted is that which refers to the essence along with a qualifying attribute, like 'a believing neck'. So when both occur in a shari'i issue, the absolute is carried upon the restricted, i.e., what is intended is the restricted. And if they occur in two different incidents according to al-Shafi'i, and it is known from this that if they occur in one incident and the two rulings are different... (then commentary follows)...
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul work discusses the classification of descriptive phrases (awsaaf) found in Quranic and prophetic texts. There are three degrees: (1) Coincidental (ittifaqi), where the description is merely according to custom and does not affect the ruling. Example: 'your step-daughters who are in your bosoms' – the phrase 'in your bosoms' is not a condition for prohibition; it describes the usual state. (2) Conditional (shart), where the description functions like a condition: 'your believing slave-girls' – belief is considered a condition for permissibility of marriage to a slave-girl. (3) Causal (illa), where the description is the effective cause of the ruling, e.g., 'the thief' and 'the fornicator' – the act of theft or fornication is the cause of the prescribed punishment. The higher the degree, the more the description implies negation when absent; but for causal descriptions, even if the cause is absent, the ruling may still apply due to another cause. Then the text introduces the principle: the absolute (mutlaq) is carried upon the restricted (muqayyad). That is, if an absolute term is used in one text and a restricted term in another on the same issue, the absolute is interpreted as the restricted. For example, 'free a neck' (mutlaq) is understood as 'free a believing neck' (muqayyad) due to the verse about believing slave-girls. The commentary discusses whether this applies when the texts are in one incident or different incidents, and the Shafi'i view is that they can apply to different incidents. The Hanafi view is that they apply only when the rulings and events are unified. This is a key usul principle.

Key Terms
وصف (wasf): A descriptive phrase (adjectival or relative clause) that qualifies a subject; it can be coincidental, conditional, or causal in its relation to the ruling.
اتفاقي (ittifaqi): Coincidental description; the attribute is mentioned merely as a customary occurrence, not as a condition or cause for the ruling.
علة (illa): The effective cause of a ruling; when a descriptive phrase is causal, it indicates that the described attribute is the reason for the legal effect.
مطلق (mutlaq): A term that refers to the essence (dhat) without any qualifying attribute, e.g., 'neck' (raqaba).
مقيد (muqayyad): A term that refers to the essence along with a qualifying attribute, e.g., 'believing neck' (raqaba mu'mina).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the highest degree of descriptive phrase (wasf)?
A) Coincidental (ittifaqi)
B) Conditional (shart)
C) Causal ('illa)
D) Ambiguous (mujmal)

Key Takeaway
Descriptive phrases in sacred texts must be classified: only conditional and causal ones affect the ruling; and the Hanafi principle that the absolute is carried upon the restricted ensures coherence in divine legislation.

Carrying Absolute to Restricted: The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Five Scenarios
Classification of mutlaq-muqayyad application across five scenarios with Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the five scenarios for mutlaq-muqayyad interaction and apply the Hanafi principle to determine when carrying is obligatory.

Core Idea
Hanafis carry mutlaq to muqayyad only when both ruling and incident are identical—causes and conditions are independent.

This passage from the commentary on Noor al-Anwar presents the Hanafi-Shafi'i disagreement on when an absolute text (mutlaq) must be restricted by a restricted text (muqayyad). Through five analytical scenarios, the author defends the Hanafi position that carrying is obligatory by consensus only when both the ruling (hukm) and the incident (haditha) are the same. In intermediate cases—same ruling but different incidents, or different rulings but same incident—the Hanafis do not carry, while Shafi'is do. The key Hanafi argument is that texts addressing causes or conditions do not create a contradiction requiring restriction, and that descriptive clauses do not imply legal conditionality (rejecting mafhum al-mukhalafa).

Guided Path
1
Identify the five scenarios
List the five possible ways absolute and restricted texts can relate: (1) same ruling & same incident, (2) different ruling & different incident, (3) same ruling & different incidents, (4) different rulings & same incident, (5) concerning causes/conditions (non-ruling). Understand the consensus and dispute for each.
Scenario 5 is actually a subset of non-ruling texts, but the passage treats it separately under the zakat al-fitr example.
2
Analyze the zakat al-fitr example
The objection: one hadith says 'for every free and slave' (absolute) and another says 'from the Muslims' (restricted). The incident is one (zakat al-fitr) and ruling one (giving sa'). Why not carry? The Hanafi response: these texts concern the cause (sabab) of obligation (the head), not the ruling itself. No conflict when causes differ; each text operates on its own cause.
The absolute makes zakat on every head; the restricted adds condition of Islam. Both are applied separately—hence non-Muslim slaves are exempted.
3
Understand the Hanafi-Shafi'i disagreement in intermediate scenarios
For scenarios 3 and 4 (same ruling/different incidents, different ruling/same incident), Hanafis do not carry; Shafi'is do. The example given is expiation for killing (free a believing slave) vs. expiation for zihar (feed sixty poor). Hanafis treat each independently because the incidents differ.
The Shafi'i position relies on the principle that if a restriction appears in one context, it should apply to all similar contexts unless there is a reason not to.
4
Examine the Hanafi response to the conditionality argument
Shafi'is argue that a descriptive clause (wasf) implies conditionality, so the absence of the description negates the ruling (mafhum al-mukhalafa). The Hanafi rebuttal: descriptions can be incidental, causal, or for clarification/praise/blame; even if conditional, negating the description does not negate the ruling because Hanafis do not accept mafhum al-mukhalafa as a proof.
The text lists examples: 'your stepdaughters under your guardianship' (incidental), 'the thief and the adulterer' (causal), 'the tall body' (clarification).
5
Synthesize the five-scenario framework
Consolidate the five divisions: (1) same ruling & same incident → carry by consensus; (2) different ruling & different incident → no carry by consensus; (3) same ruling & different incidents → Hanafi no carry, Shafi'i carry; (4) different rulings & same incident → Hanafi no carry, Shafi'i carry; (5) texts concerning cause/condition (non-ruling) → Hanafi no carry, Shafi'i carry (according to some). The thamarah al-khilaf appears in expiation rules and similar cases.
The passage notes that detailed discussion exists in longer works; here the author focuses on the core principles.

ولا تأثير لنفيه في نفي الحكم؛ لأن نفي الحكم نفي أصلي لا شرعي على ما قدمنا .
يجب الحمل إلخ : أي حمل المطلق على المقيد. (القمر) وفي تعدّدهما اختلاف الحكم واختلاف الحادثة. (المحشي) وتحقيق ذلك إلخ: توضيح المقام على ما في "التوضيح" وغيره أنّ النص المطلق والمقيد إما أن يردا في غير الحكم كالسبب، وإما في الحكم الواحد في حادثة واحدة أو في حادثتين، وإما في الحكمين المختلفين في حادثة واحدة أو في حادثتين فهذه خمسة أقسام فعلى الأوّل لا يحمل المطلق على المقيّد عندنا ويحمل عليه عند الشافعي ليه، وقد مرّ مثاله في الشرح وأشار إليه المصنف يحلله بقوله : وفي الفطر إلخ. وعلى الثاني يحمل المطلق على المقيد بالاتفاق بيننا وبين الشافعي لله، وقد مرّ مثاله في الشرح، وأشار إليه المصنف بقوله: إلّا أن يكونا في حكم واحد. وعلى الثالث يجب حمل المطلق على المقيّد عند الشافعي لله وليس الحمل عندنا، وقد أشار إليه وإلى مثاله المصنف الله بقوله : وإن كانا في حادثتين عند الشافعي له مثل كفّارة القتل إلخ. وعلى الرابع يحمل المطلق على المقيد عند الشافعي له لا عندنا وأشار إليه وإلى مثاله الشارح لله بقوله : ويعلم منه أنهما إن كانا في حادثة واحدة إلخ. وعلى الخامس لا يحمل المطلق على المقيد بالاتفاق بيننا وبين الشافعي لله، ومثاله تقييد الصيام بالتتابع في كفّارة القتل، وإطلاق الإطعام في كفّارة الظهار. في الخامس اتفاق على عدم الحمل، وفي الثاني اتفاق على الحمل. وفي الأقسام الباقية .خلاف وههنا تفصيل وبحث كثير مذكور في المطوّلات. (القمر) في جواب: أي جواب عن حمل المطلق على المقيّد في كفارة الظهار على القتل. (المحشي) قد يكون اتفاقيا كما مر من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَبَائِبُكُمُ اللَّاتِي فِي حُجُورِكُمْ (النساء: ۲۳) (القمر) بمعنى العلة: نحو: السارق والزاني.(القمر) للكشف: نحو الجسم الطويل العريض العميق. (القمر) أو للمدح نحو: الله الرحمن الرحيم. (القمر)
أو الدّم نحو الشيطان الرجيم (القمر) ولئن كان أي القيد بمعنى الشرط. (القمر) النفي أي نفي الحكم عند انتفاء الشرط. (القمر) لا شرعي لأنا لا نقول بمفهوم المخالفة فكيف يتصوّر القياس، فإنّه لابد في القياس من أن يكون المعدّى حكمًا شرعيًا. (القمر)
Explanation of the divisions of the fourth classification. Section on corrupt aspects regarding non-mutawatir recitations, whether mashhura or ahad. The agreed-upon example is his statement (peace be upon him) to a Bedouin who intentionally had intercourse with his wife during Ramadan: 'Fast two months.' And in one narration: 'Fast two consecutive months.' * Then it is objected to us: Since you acknowledge that carrying (the absolute to the restricted) is necessary in a single incident and a single ruling, then in his statement: 'Give for every free person and slave' * and his statement: 'Give for every free and slave among the Muslims' the absolute should be carried to the restricted because the incident is one—zakat al-fitr—and the ruling is one—giving a sa' or half. So he replied: In zakat al-fitr, the two texts came concerning the cause, and there is no conflict in causes, so it is necessary to combine them. Meaning that what we said—that the absolute is carried to the restricted in a single incident and a single ruling—only applies if they come concerning the ruling because of contradiction. But if they come concerning causes or conditions, there is no difficulty or contradiction. (al-Qamar) 'It is necessary' etc. even though according to you, O Hanafis, there is no carrying, for it would obligate [zakat] on the disbelieving slave. (al-Qamar) 'No conflict' etc. It is permissible for one thing to have multiple causes. (al-Qamar) 'It is necessary to combine' etc. i.e., it is necessary to act on each one separately without invalidating the description of absoluteness and restriction. (al-Qamar) 'When they come' i.e., the absolute and restricted texts. (al-Qamar) 'Or conditions' e.g., 'No marriage except with witnesses' and 'No marriage except with a guardian and two just witnesses'; these are two hadiths, as it is said, absolute and restricted, concerning the condition of marriage i.e., witnesses. (al-Qamar) 'No difficulty' etc. The cause of the obligation of zakat al-fitr is the head, and it is absolute in one hadith and restricted by Islam in another; so the two texts concern the cause. Since the absolute is a cause, every individual of it is a cause, so the restricted also becomes a cause, and there is no contradiction or harm. (al-Qamar) Its takhrij has been mentioned earlier. Al-Daraqutni narrated in his Sunan ... Ibn Umar said: The Messenger of Allah prescribed zakat al-fitr as one sa' of dates or one sa' of barley or half a sa' of wheat for every young and old, male and female, free and slave from the Muslims. Section on corrupt aspects. Explanation of the divisions of the fourth classification: So the absolute can be a cause by its absoluteness and the restricted a cause by its restriction. The conclusion is: if the ruling and the incident are the same, carrying is obligatory by consensus; if they differ, carrying is not obligatory by consensus; in cases other than these, there is disagreement. The verification of this is in al-Tawdih. Then he began the response to al-Shafi'i: We do not concede that the restriction implies conditionality, because a descriptive clause may be incidental, or may indicate the cause, or may be for clarification, praise, or blame. Even if it [implies conditionality], we do not concede that it necessitates negation, because the contested point is the syntactic condition that is entered by conditional tools. The negation [of the description] has no effect on negating the ruling, because the negation of the ruling is original negation, not legal, as we have established. (al-Qamar) 'Carrying is obligatory' etc. i.e., carrying the absolute to the restricted. (al-Qamar) 'If they differ' i.e., the ruling and the incident. (al-Muhashshi) 'Verification' etc. Clarification of the status based on al-Tawdih and others: The absolute and restricted texts either occur regarding other than the ruling, such as the cause; or regarding the same ruling in one incident or in two incidents; or regarding two different rulings in one incident or in two incidents. These are five divisions. In the first, we do not carry the absolute to the restricted, while al-Shafi'i does; its example has been given in the commentary. In the second, carrying the absolute to the restricted is obligatory by consensus between us and al-Shafi'i; its example was given. In the third, carrying is obligatory for al-Shafi'i but not for us; example: expiation for killing. In the fourth, carrying is done by al-Shafi'i but not by us; example: when they are in one incident. In the fifth, there is consensus that carrying is not done; example: restriction of fasting consecutively in expiation for killing and absoluteness of feeding in expiation for zihar. So in the fifth there is agreement on no carrying, in the second agreement on carrying, and in the remaining divisions there is disagreement. There is much detail and discussion in the longer works. (al-Qamar) 'In response' i.e., response to carrying the absolute to the restricted in the expiation for zihar to that for killing. (al-Muhashshi) 'It may be incidental' as in the verse: 'your stepdaughters under your guardianship' (4:23). (al-Qamar) 'Indicating the cause' e.g., the thief and the adulterer. (al-Qamar) 'For clarification' e.g., the tall, wide, deep body. (al-Qamar) 'For praise' e.g., Allah, the Most Merciful, the Most Compassionate. (al-Qamar) 'For blame' e.g., the accursed devil. (al-Qamar) 'Even if it is' i.e., the restriction implies conditionality. (al-Qamar) 'Negation' i.e., negation of the ruling upon absence of the condition. (al-Qamar) 'Not legal' because we do not accept the concept of opposition, so how can qiyas be conceived? For qiyas requires that the rule being extended be a legal rule.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Noor al-Anwar discusses the usul principle of carrying the absolute (mutlaq) to the restricted (muqayyad) and the Hanafi-Shafi'i disagreement over its application. The author begins by presenting an example that is agreed upon: the hadith commanding an expiatory fast of two months for intercourse during Ramadan, where one narration is absolute and another is restricted to consecutive months. This is used to raise an objection: if carrying is necessary when ruling and incident are identical, then in the case of zakat al-fitr, where one hadith says 'for every free and slave' (absolute) and another says 'from the Muslims' (restricted), why not carry the absolute to the restricted? The Hanafi response is that the two texts on zakat al-fitr concern the cause (sabab) of obligation, not the ruling itself. When multiple texts address different causes, there is no conflict, so each is applied separately. The absolute text makes zakat obligatory on every head, and the restricted text adds the condition of Islam, thus non-Muslim slaves are exempted—both are operative without contradiction. The passage then outlines five scenarios for the occurrence of absolute and restricted texts: (1) both on the same ruling and same incident—carrying by consensus; (2) both on different rulings and different incidents—no carrying by consensus; (3) both on the same ruling but different incidents; (4) different rulings but same incident; (5) different rulings and different incidents—no carrying by consensus. In scenarios 3 and 4, the Hanafis do not carry while the Shafi'is do, except for specific exceptions mentioned. Finally, the author refutes the Shafi'i argument that a descriptive clause (wasf) implies conditionality, explaining that adjectives can be incidental, causal, or for clarification/praise/blame, and that even if it were conditional, negating it does not negate the ruling because the Hanafis reject the concept of opposition (mafhum al-mukhalafa) as a proof.

Key Terms
مطلق (mutlaq): A text that does not contain a qualifying restriction (qayd), leaving the ruling unqualified.
مقيد (muqayyad): A text that includes a qualifying restriction (qayd) limiting the ruling to a specific subset.
حمل (haml): The interpretation process of taking the absolute text and making it apply within the confines of the restricted text.
سبب (sabab): The cause or occasion for a ruling, such as having a head (for zakat al-fitr) which triggers the obligation.
شرط (shart): A condition that affects the ruling, like the presence of witnesses for marriage; syntactic condition (shart nahwi) is distinguished from legal condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, in which of the following five scenarios is carrying the absolute to the restricted obligatory by consensus?
A) When the texts concern two different rulings and two different incidents.
B) When the texts concern the same ruling and the same incident.
C) When the texts concern the same ruling but different incidents.
D) When the texts concern different rulings but the same incident.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle for mutlaq-muqayyad: carry only when ruling and incident are identical; texts on causes or conditions are independent and do not require restriction.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Five Cases of Mutlaq and Muqayyad and the Dispute with Shafi'i
Classification of absolute/restricted scenarios and rebuttal of Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for carrying the absolute onto the restricted by identifying the five cases and distinguishing when analogy is valid.

Core Idea
Carrying the absolute onto the restricted is only obligatory when the ruling and incident are identical.

This passage dives into one of the most nuanced discussions in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: when does a restriction in one text bind another absolute text? The author presents a five-case classification based on whether the ruling and cause (incident) are the same or different. The Hanafi position is strict: only when both match does the restriction transfer. The Shafi'i school extends it further. Understanding this distinction is key to correctly deriving rulings from the Qur'an and Sunnah without overgeneralizing.

Guided Path
1
Understand the five-case classification
The text divides scenarios based on whether the ruling and incident (cause) are united or separate. Identify all five combinations: (1) neither ruling nor incident is a ruling (just cause), (2) both same, (3) same ruling, different incident, (4) same incident, different ruling, (5) both different.
Refer to the breakdown in the commentary for clarity.
2
Identify consensus cases
Case 2 (both same) has consensus on carrying; case 5 (both different) has consensus on not carrying. Recognize these as undisputed.
3
Analyze the disputed cases (1,3,4)
In cases 1, 3, and 4, the Hanafi school does not carry the absolute onto the restricted, while the Shafi'i school does. Understand the reasoning behind each position.
The key is the difference in ruling or incident that breaks the analogy.
4
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi argument has two prongs: (a) a restriction may not be a true condition; (b) even if it is, analogy requires full similarity (mumathala), which is absent due to differing severity of offenses and distribution of expiations.
The example of expiation for killing (grave sin) vs. zihar and yamin (minor sins) illustrates the point.
5
Apply to a practical example
Take the absolute text on zihar (free a slave) and the restricted text on killing (free a believing slave). According to Hanafi, since rulings and incidents differ, we do not carry the condition of faith onto zihar. Thus, a slave without faith suffices for zihar.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in fatwa.

فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة
بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع فيمكن أن يكون المطلق سببًا بإطلاقه والمقيد سببًا بتقييده. فالحاصل أن في اتحاد الحكم والحادثة يجب الحمل بالاتفاق، وفي تعدّدهما لا يجب الحمل بالاتفاق، وفيما سواهما اختلاف. وتحقيق ذلك في "التوضيح".
ثم شرع في جواب الشافعي له فقال : ولا نسلّم أنّ القيد بمعنى الشرط؛ لأنّ الوصف قد يكون اتفاقيًا، وقد يكون بمعنى العلة، وقد يكون للكشف أو للمدح أو الذم، ولئن كان فلا نسلّم أنه يوجب النفي؛ لأنّ المتنازع فيه هو الشرط النحوي الذي تدخل عليه الأدوات، الشرط ولا تأثير لنفيه في نفي الحكم؛ لأن نفي الحكم نفي أصلي لا شرعي على ما قدمنا.

(المحشي) قد يكون اتفاقيا كما مر من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَبَائِبُكُمُ اللَّاتِي فِي حُجُورِكُمْ (النساء: ۲۳) (القمر) بمعنى العلة: نحو: السارق والزاني.(القمر) للكشف: نحو الجسم الطويل العريض العميق. فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة
بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع ولئن كان فإنّما يصح الاستدلال به على غيره إن صحت المماثلة وليس كذلك؛ فإن القتل من أعظم الكبائر ، يعني لو سلّمنا نفي الحكم في الأصل المنصوص، لكن لا نسلم المساواة بينه وبين المسكوت حتى يحمل عليه؛ فإن القتل من أعظم الكبائر فيمكن أن تشترط فيه الرقبة أي المسكوت المؤمنة، بخلاف الظهار واليمين؛ فإنّهما صغيرتان يمكن جبرهما بالرقبة المطلقة، أعم من أن تكون كافرة أو مؤمنة، وأيضا توزيع كلّ منهما مختلف؛ فإنّ في القتل حكم أولاً بالتحرير، ثمّ بالصيام في شهرين، وفي الظهار حكم أولاً بالتحرير، ثمّ بالصيام في شهرين، ثم بإطعام ستين مسكينًا، وفي اليمين خيار بين إطعام عشرة أو كسوتهم أو تحرير رقبة، ثمّ إن لم يتيسر هولاء فصيام ثلاثة أيام، فالله تعالى العالم بمصالح العباد وحكمتهم قد حكم بما شاء في كلّ جناية على حالها؛ فلا ينبغي لنا أن نتعرّض لشيء منها أو نحمل نص أحد منها على الآخر بالإطلاق والتقييد؛ فإن فيه تضييع الأسرار التي أودعها فيه فأما قيد الإسامة والعدالة فلم النفي. جواب عما يرد علينا من النقضين وهو أنكم قلتم: إذا أورد الإطلاق والقيد يوجب السبب لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر، وههنا ورد قوله علي : " في خمس من الإبل شاة"،
ولئن كان أي ولئن أوجب النفي ويصح تعديته فإنما إلخ. (القمر) الاستدلال به: أي بالمقيد وهو رقبة كفّارة القتل مثلاً على غيره وهو المطلق وهو رقبة كفارة الظهار واليمين مثلاً (القمر) المماثلة أي بين الجنايات القتل واليمين والظهار. (القمر) نفي الحكم: أي عند نفي القيد وهو الإيمان. (القمر) في الأصل المنصوص: أي كفّارة القتل. وبين المسكوت أي كفّارة الظهار واليمين (القمر) من أعظم الكبائر فيه ما قيل: من أنّ الكفّارة إنّما هي في القتل خطاً لا في العمد، والقتل خطاً ليس من الكبائر. اللّهم إلّا أن يقال: إنّ الكفارة تجب في القتل عمدا أيضا عند الخصم وهو من أعظم الكبائر. (القمر) فيمكن أن تشترط إلخ فإنّ تغليظ الكفّارة بقدر غلظ الجناية.(القمر) فإنهما صغيرتان فيه أنه ليس في القتل خطاً إلا جناية عدم التثبت وعدم الاحتياط، والظهار قول منكر و زور فهو أقوى من القتل خطأ فتأمل. (القمر)
أخرجه الترمذي رقم: ٦٢١ باب ما جاء في زكاة الإبل والغنم، وأبو داود رقم: ١٥٦٨، باب في زكاة السائمة، عن سالم عن أبيه قال الترمذي: حديث حسن.
Chapter on mentioning the invalid aspects. Explanation of the divisions of the fourth classification: The absolute may be a cause by its absoluteness, and the restricted a cause by its restriction. The conclusion: When the ruling and incident are united, carrying (the absolute onto the restricted) is obligatory by consensus. When both differ, carrying is not obligatory by consensus. In other cases, there is disagreement. The verification of this is in 'al-Tawdih'. Then he began replying to al-Shafi'i, saying: We do not concede that the restriction is in the sense of a condition, because a description may be coincidental, or in the sense of a cause, or for clarification, praise, or blame. Even if it were, we do not concede that it necessitates negation, because the point of contention is the grammatical condition that involves particles, and its negation has no effect on negating the ruling, because negating the ruling is an original negation, not a legal one, as we have previously stated. (al-Qamar) 'Carrying is obligatory' etc.: meaning carrying the absolute onto the restricted. (al-Muhashi) 'When both differ': difference in ruling and difference in incident. (al-Muhashi) 'The verification of this' etc.: Clarifying the matter according to 'al-Tawdih' and others: The absolute and restricted texts either pertain to something other than a ruling, like a cause, or pertain to a single ruling in a single incident or in two incidents, or pertain to two different rulings in a single incident or in two incidents. These are five divisions. In the first, we do not carry the absolute onto the restricted, but al-Shafi'i does. Its example was given in the commentary, and the author indicated it with his statement: 'Concerning breaking the fast' etc. In the second, carrying the absolute onto the restricted is obligatory by consensus between us and al-Shafi'i. Its example was given in the commentary, and the author indicated it with his statement: 'Unless they are in a single ruling.' In the third, carrying the absolute onto the restricted is obligatory according to al-Shafi'i, but we do not carry. The author indicated it and its example with his statement: 'If they are in two incidents, according to al-Shafi'i, like the expiation for killing' etc. In the fourth, carrying the absolute onto the restricted is obligatory according to al-Shafi'i, but not according to us. The commentator indicated it and its example with his statement: 'It is understood from this that if they are in a single incident' etc. In the fifth, carrying the absolute onto the restricted is not obligatory by consensus between us and al-Shafi'i. Its example is restricting fasting to consecutiveness in the expiation for killing, and leaving feeding unrestricted in the expiation for zihar. In the fifth there is consensus on not carrying, and in the second consensus on carrying. In the remaining divisions there is disagreement. There is much detail and research mentioned in the lengthy works. (al-Qamar) 'In reply': meaning reply concerning carrying the absolute onto the restricted in the expiation for zihar onto that for killing. (al-Muhashi) 'It may be coincidental' as in His statement: 'And your stepdaughters who are under your guardianship' (4:23). (al-Qamar) 'In the sense of a cause': like 'the thief' and 'the adulterer'. (al-Qamar) 'For clarification': like 'the tall, broad, deep body'. (al-Qamar) 'Or for praise': like 'Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful'. (al-Qamar) 'Or for blame': like 'the accursed devil'. (al-Qamar) 'Even if it were': meaning the restriction in the sense of a condition. (al-Qamar) 'Negation': meaning negation of the ruling when the condition is absent. (al-Qamar) 'Not legal': because we do not accept the concept of opposition; how then can analogy be conceived? For in analogy, the transmitted ruling must be legal. (al-Qamar) Chapter on mentioning the invalid aspects. Explanation of the divisions of the fourth classification: And even if it were, it would only be valid to use it as evidence for another case if similarity is sound, and that is not the case. Because killing is among the gravest sins. That is, if we concede the negation of the ruling in the textual original, we do not concede equality between it and the unstated case such that it is carried onto it. For killing is among the gravest sins, so it is possible that a believing slave is required, unlike zihar and a vow (yamin). For they are minor sins that can be compensated by an unrestricted slave, whether the slave is a believer or not. Also, the distribution of each differs: in killing, the ruling first is freeing a slave, then fasting two months; in zihar, first freeing a slave, then fasting two months, then feeding sixty poor persons; in a vow, there is a choice between feeding ten, clothing them, or freeing a slave, and if these are not available, then fasting three days. Allah, who knows the interests of His servants and their wisdom, has decreed as He willed for each offense according to its circumstance. So it is not appropriate for us to interfere with any of them or to carry one text onto another by absoluteness or restriction, for that would waste the secrets He has deposited in them. As for the restriction of pasturage and justice, it is for negation. A reply to the objection raised against us: You said: When the absolute and restricted are mentioned with the cause, one is not carried onto the other. But here the statement of 'Ali came: 'For every five camels, one sheep.' And even if it were, i.e., even if it obligated negation and it is valid to extend it, then it is etc. (al-Qamar) 'Using it as evidence': i.e., using the restricted, like the slave in expiation for killing, for another, i.e., the absolute, like the slave in expiation for zihar and a vow. (al-Qamar) 'Similarity': i.e., between the offenses: killing, vow, and zihar. (al-Qamar) 'Negation of the ruling': i.e., when the restriction (faith) is negated. (al-Qamar) 'In the textual original': i.e., expiation for killing. And 'the unstated': i.e., expiation for zihar and a vow. (al-Qamar) 'Among the gravest sins' — there is what is said: that expiation is only for accidental killing, not intentional; and accidental killing is not one of the gravest sins. Unless it is said: expiation is obligatory for intentional killing as well according to the opponent, and it is among the gravest sins. (al-Qamar) 'So it is possible that it is required' etc., because the severity of expiation is proportional to the severity of the offense. (al-Qamar) 'For they are minor sins' — it is noted that accidental killing is only an offense of lacking certainty and caution, while zihar is an abominable and false statement, so it is stronger than accidental killing; reflect. (al-Qamar) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi no. 621, chapter on zakat of camels and sheep, and Abu Dawud no. 1568, chapter on zakat of pasturing animals, from Salim from his father; al-Tirmidhi said: hasan hadith.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul analysis of the rules governing the relationship between absolute (mutlaq) and restricted (muqayyad) texts. The author classifies the possible scenarios into five cases based on whether the ruling and incident (cause) are the same or different. The Hanafi position is that carrying the absolute onto the restricted is obligatory only when the ruling and incident are both the same (case 2). When they differ (case 5), it is not obligatory by consensus. In the remaining three cases, there is disagreement with Shafi'i who extends carrying to cases where either the ruling or the incident differs. The commentary rebuts Shafi'i by arguing that a descriptive restriction may not be a true condition, and even if it were, it does not necessitate negation of the ruling in other contexts because analogy requires similarity between the cases. The example of expiation for killing (requiring a believing slave) versus zihar and vows (allowing any slave) illustrates that the severity of the offense justifies different restrictions. The overall lesson is that absolute and restricted texts are treated independently unless the legal cause (sabab) is identical.

Key Terms
المطلق (al-mutlaq): A text that is unrestricted, not qualified by any condition or description.
المقيد (al-muqayyad): A text that is restricted by a condition or description.
حمل المطلق على المقيد (haml al-mutlaq ala al-muqayyad): Interpreting an absolute text in light of a restricted text so that the restriction applies to both.
المماثلة (al-mumathala): Complete similarity between two cases required for valid analogy (qiyas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, in which of the following cases is it obligatory to carry the absolute (mutlaq) onto the restricted (muqayyad)?
A) When the ruling is the same but the incident differs.
B) When both the ruling and the incident are the same.
C) When the ruling differs but the incident is the same.
D) When both the ruling and the incident differ.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school carries the absolute onto the restricted only when both the ruling and the incident are identical, preserving the independence of each text's legislative purpose.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Objection and Response on Carrying Muqayyad to Mutlaq When Causes Differ
Refutation of an objection to the Hanafi rule on carrying muqayyad to mutlaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle regarding the extension of a restricted ruling (muqayyad) to an absolute ruling (mutlaq) only when the cause is the same or supported by additional evidence, and apply it to distinguish the zakat and testimony cases from the kaffarah cases.

Core Idea
Carrying muqayyad to mutlaq requires common cause or separate proof; differences in gravity block automatic extension.

The Hanafi school holds that a restriction in one text does not automatically apply to another text on the same subject unless the underlying cause (sabab) is identical or a third independent proof (dalil) supports the restriction. In this passage, the author defends this rule against an objection: why do Hanafis themselves carry the restriction 'pasturing' to the absolute zakat hadith, and 'just' to the absolute testimony verse, but refuse to carry 'believing' from the expiation for killing to the expiations for zihar and yamin? The response hinges on the difference in the gravity of the crimes and the presence of independent texts supporting the restrictions in zakat and testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi rule on mutlaq and muqayyad
State the general Hanafi principle: when an absolute text and a restricted text exist on the same subject, they are not automatically merged unless the cause (sabab) is the same or there is a third proof indicating the restriction applies to the absolute.
This rule is based on the idea that each legislative ruling has its own rationale.
2
Recognize the objection (naqd) against the Hanafis
The opponent argues: you Hanafis carry the absolute 'on five camels, one sheep' to the restricted 'on five pasturing camels, one sheep' and carry 'two witnesses' (absolute) to 'two just witnesses' (restricted). Yet you do not carry 'believing slave' from kaffarat al-qatl to the kaffarah for zihar and yamin. This appears inconsistent.
The objection is a comparison between two pairs: zakat and testimony where carrying was done, and kaffarah where it was not.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: the role of additional evidence
The Hanafi scholar responds that in the zakat case, the restriction to pasturing camels is not derived from the restricted hadith alone; rather, a third independent Sunnah (the letter of the Prophet to Yemen) explicitly negates zakat on non-pasturing camels. In the testimony case, the condition of uprightness is already present in another verse (mimman tardawna min al-shuhada'). These are separate proofs, not mere extensions.
Thus, the carrying in those cases is not an automatic application of the rule but based on external evidence.
4
Examine why the kaffarah cases are different
The passage emphasizes that the crimes differ in severity: killing is a major sin, while zihar and yamin are minor. The expiation structures also differ (killing: only two options; zihar: three; yamin: options with choice). Therefore, the restriction to a believing slave in killing is based on the gravity of the crime and is not applicable to the lesser crimes.
The principle: without common cause or additional proof, the restriction stays within its own text.
5
Apply the principle to a new hypothetical case
Consider a hadith: 'On forty sheep, one sheep' (mutlaq) and another: 'On forty pasturing sheep, one sheep' (muqayyad). If there is a third Sunnah that exempts non-pasturing sheep, the restriction applies; otherwise, the absolute is applied to all sheep. This reinforces the need for additional evidence.
This example mirrors the zakat discussion in the text.

And even if it were, inference from it to others is only valid if similarity is established, but it is not so; for killing (qatl) is among the greatest major sins. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'On every five pasturing camels, one sheep' indicates the causes; because camels are the cause of zakat, and the first is absolute and the second is restricted by pasturing, and you have carried the absolute here onto the restricted until you said: zakat is not obligatory on non-pasturing camels. And you also said: if the incident is different, the absolute is not carried onto the restricted, yet you carried His saying 'And call to witness two witnesses from among your men' onto His saying 'And bring to witness two just men from among you' until you made uprightness a condition in testimony absolutely, even though the former is in the incident of debt (al-Baqarah 2:282) and the latter in the chapter of taking back in divorce (al-Talaq 65:2)? So he answered: the restriction of pasturing in the first issue and the restriction of uprightness in the second issue did not necessitate negation of what is other than them as you understood. [Zakat of working and pregnant animals] but the well-known Sunnah in negating zakat from working and pregnant animals necessitated abrogation of the absoluteness, meaning we only acted in the first issue by the third Sunnah indicating negation of zakat from other than pasturing animals. 'Pasturing' etc. are camels that graze and are not fed in the pen; they are called sawam, sa'imah, and sawa'im. It is derived from sam yasumu sawman and sawaman. It is said: the seller sam the merchandise, i.e., he displayed it and mentioned its price to the buyer; i.e., he sought to sell it. It is said: he sam with the merchandise such and such, and also istama; and the cattle grazed and went out to the pasture; and he saddled someone with the affair, i.e., tasked him with it. It is mostly used in punishment and evil. (al-Sanbali: with pasturing (al-isamah) with a kasra on the hamza from the pattern of if'al, its past tense is asama. It is said: asama al-ibila, he let them graze; and it is said: he brought them out to the pasture; and al-hadrah upon the knee means he attended him; and he turned his eyes to him, meaning he threw him with them). (al-Sanbali: other than pasturing animals owned in 'Rasa'il al-Arkan'; then pasturing according to us is what suffices by grazing most of the year, and from it milk or offspring is intended, establishing the majority in place of the whole). (al-Qamar: 'And call to witness two witnesses...' he said in the marginal note: but it is objected against that this text is also restricted by His saying 'from those whom you approve as witnesses' (al-Baqarah 2:282), and the approved witness is the just one). (al-Qamar: testimony, and similarly His saying 'And bring to witness when you transact' (al-Baqarah 2:282)) (al-Muhashi: in the incident of debt, i.e., a transaction where one lends money to another, and al-mudayanah is a transaction on credit whether giving or taking). (al-Qamar: negation means negation of the ruling from working animals etc.; al-‘awamil is plural of ‘amila, i.e., those prepared for work like tilling the earth; al-hawamil is plural of hamila, i.e., those prepared for carrying loads; and al-‘alufah is what is given fodder, which is the opposite of pasturing). (al-Qamar: by the third Sunnah, i.e., besides the two texts, the absolute and the restricted). (al-Qamar: narrated by al-Hakim in his Mustadrak 1/553, in a long letter sent by the Prophet (peace be upon him) to the people of Yemen with ‘Amr ibn Hazm. In the absence of this restriction). (al-Qamar).
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of an objection raised against the Hanafi principle regarding carrying a restricted (muqayyad) ruling to an unrestricted (mutlaq) one. The objection is: in some cases, such as the hadith 'On every five camels, one sheep' (which is absolute) and 'On every five pasturing camels, one sheep' (restricted), the Hanafis themselves carry the absolute to the restricted, and they also do so with the verses on testimony (absolute in debt, restricted in divorce requiring two just witnesses). So why do they not do the same in the case of expiation (kaffarah)? The passage responds that the restriction in the zakat and testimony cases does not negate the ruling for other categories because there is a separate Sunnah (the third Sunnah) that specifically negates zakat on non-pasturing camels, and similarly the condition of uprightness ('adalah) is supported by another verse (mimman tardawna min al-shuhada'). In the expiation case, the crimes are different in gravity (killing is a major sin, zihar and yamin are minor) and the expiation rulings are structured differently, so the conditioned ruling (believing slave) cannot be imposed on the others. The commentator (al-Qamar) provides detailed marginal notes clarifying terms and addressing sub-issues, such as whether unintentional killing is a major sin. The hadith is cited from al-Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud, and the discussion shows that the Hanafi rule is not applied mechanically; it is subject to additional evidence (dalil) from Sunnah and textual context.

Key Terms
مطلق (mutlaq): An unqualified or absolute expression that does not contain a restriction (qayd).
مقيد (muqayyad): A qualified or restricted expression that includes a condition or specification.
السائمة (al-sa'imah): Pasturing livestock that graze freely in the wilderness and are not fed by the owner.
العدالة (al-'adalah): Uprightness or moral integrity, a condition for witness testimony.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation for certain sins or violations, often involving freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding the poor.
المماثلة (al-mumathalah): Similarity or equality between cases, a condition for transferring a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the restriction of 'believing slave' in the expiation for killing not applied to the expiation for zihar?
A) Because killing is a major sin while zihar is a minor sin, and the expiation structures differ.
B) Because the hadith on zihar is weaker in transmission.
C) Because the Hanafis never carry muqayyad to mutlaq under any circumstances.
D) Because the verse on zihar explicitly says 'slave' without any condition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule on mutlaq and muqayyad requires either identical cause or independent textual evidence to carry a restriction; differences in the gravity and structure of rulings block automatic extension.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Zakat on Camels: Sunnah Overrides the Absolute-Restricted Principle
Refutation of the first invalid objection concerning zakat on camels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a separate Sunnah text overrides the principle of haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad and apply it to the case of zakat on non-sa'ima camels.

Core Idea
The Hanafi exclusion of non-grazing camels from zakat is based on a specific hadith, not on carrying absolute to restricted.

In this passage, the commentator refutes the objection that the Hanafis inconsistently apply the principle of carrying absolute to restricted (haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad). The objectors note that Hanafis restrict the absolute 'camel' to 'grazing camel' (sa'ima) for zakat, while in other cases they do not carry absolute to restricted. The Hanafi response clarifies that the restriction in the camel case is not from the principle but from a separate prophetic hadith that explicitly exempts working, pregnant, and stabled camels. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objectors' argument
The objectors claim the Hanafis carry the absolute 'camel' to the restricted 'grazing camel' using the principle of haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad, and then inconsistently refuse to apply the same principle elsewhere.
The objectors point to the same cause (sabab) argument.
2
Recognize the Hanafi counter: the role of a separate Sunnah
The Hanafis respond that the restriction on non-grazing camels is derived from a different dalil: the hadith 'لا زكاة في العوامل والحوامل والعلوفة' (No zakat on working, pregnant, and stabled camels). This hadith abrogates the absolute ruling for those categories.
This shows that a specific text can override the general implication of the absolute.
3
Analyze the hadith as a dalil
The hadith is classified as a Sunnah (zanni al-thubut but qati' al-dalala in its negation of zakat on those types). It provides the specific exclusion that the objectors thought came from the absolute-restricted principle.
The Hanafis cite this hadith as the primary proof, not the principle.
4
Distinguish the two usul operations
The objectors confused two operations: (a) carrying absolute to restricted when cause is same, and (b) using an independent Sunnah to specify the general. The Hanafis did (b), not (a).
This distinction is crucial for correct usul application.

Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division: Chapter on mentioning the invalid aspects. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'In five grazing camels, one sheep' ... regarding the causes; because the camel is the cause of zakat, and the first is absolute and the second is restricted by grazing, and you have carried the absolute here to the restricted until you said: Zakat is not obligatory on non-grazing [animals]. And also you said: When the incident is different, the absolute is not carried to the restricted, and you have carried the verse 'And call to witness two witnesses from among your men' to the verse 'And call to witness two just men from among you' until you conditioned justice in witnessing absolutely, even though the first is about the incident of debt and the second about the chapter of revocation in divorce? So he answered: That the restriction of grazing in the first issue and the restriction of justice in the second issue did not necessitate negation of what is beyond them as you understood. But the well-known Sunnah concerning the negation of zakat on working and pregnant animals necessitated abrogation of the absolute, meaning the absolute of grazing camels etc. The grazing camels that are not fed in the enclosure are called sawam, sa'ima, and sawa'im... [continues translation of definitions and hadith references] ... And it is said: The conjunction in the wording is a fourth invalid aspect, held by Malik (may Allah have mercy on him), which is that joining two statements with the letter waw necessitates conjunction in the ruling, i.e., sharing in it; because observance of harmony between sentences is a condition. So zakat is not obligatory on a minor because of its conjunction with prayer in the verse 'Establish prayer and give zakat', for they are two complete sentences conjoined by waw; so it necessitates equality between them, so no zakat on working animals etc. And in al-Hidayah: 'There is no sadaqah on working and pregnant animals, contrary to Malik; he has the apparent of the texts, and we have his statement (peace be upon him): There is no sadaqah on working, pregnant, and stabled animals.' They are non-grazing, so zakat falls from non-grazing; hence we restricted camels to grazing, not because the absolute is carried to the restricted in the other narration... [continues]
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the 'invalid aspects' (wujuh faseda) raised against the Hanafi application of the principle that the absolute is carried to the restricted (haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad). The author, likely a Hanafi commentator, addresses two main objections and then a fourth one (attributed to Malik). The first objection concerns zakat on camels: opponents argue that Hanafis carry the absolute term 'camel' to the restricted 'grazing camel' (saa'ima) based on a hadith, and then use the same method inconsistently. The author replies that the Hanafis do not rely on that principle for the negation of zakat on non-grazing camels; rather, they rely on a separate Sunnah (the hadith about working and pregnant camels) which abrogates the absolute meaning. The second objection is about witness testimony: opponents argue that Hanafis carry the absolute witness statement to the restricted just witness condition from another verse. The reply is that they rely on the third text (Quran 49:6) which requires verification of a fasiq's report, implying that a witness must be 'adl (just) in all cases. The fourth invalid aspect is from Imam Malik, who argued that because zakat is conjoined with prayer in the Quran by waw, the rulings must be equal, so if prayer is not obligatory on minors, zakat is not. The Hanafis reject this, citing the hadith specifically exempting working and pregnant animals from zakat, indicating that conjunction does not necessitate identical rulings in all circumstances. The passage concludes with a hadith reference from al-Hidayah supporting the Hanafi position. Throughout, the commentator emphasizes that the Hanafi school uses additional texts (sunnah or Quran) to specify the ruling, not merely the absolute-restricted principle.

Key Terms
السائمة (al-sāʾima): Grazing livestock that spend most of the year grazing freely, intended for milk or offspring.
العوامل (al-ʿawāmil): Draft animals used for work such as ploughing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul scholars in the passage, why is zakat not obligatory on working camels (al-‘awāmil)?
A) Because the absolute term 'camel' is carried to the restricted 'sa'ima'.
B) Because a separate hadith explicitly negates zakat on working and pregnant camels.
C) Because working camels are not owned as wealth.
D) Because the conjunction of zakat with prayer in the Quran implies no obligation on minors.

Key Takeaway
A specific Sunnah can abrogate the general implication of an absolute text, and this is the basis for the Hanafi exclusion of non-sa'ima camels from zakat.

The Condition of Justice in Witnesses: Deriving from the Quran via Usul
Refutation of the second invalid objection concerning witness justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the Hanafi derivation of the condition of justice in witnesses from the Quranic verse on verifying the report of a fasiq.

Core Idea
The requirement of justice for witnesses is derived from the command to verify a fasiq's report, not from carrying absolute to restricted.

The second invalid argument raised against the Hanafis is about their conditioning of justice in witnesses. The objectors claim that the Hanafis arbitrarily carry the absolute in one verse (استشهدوا شهيدين) to the restricted in another (أشهدوا ذوي عدل) despite different contexts. The Hanafi reply reveals that the condition of justice comes from a third text: the verse on verifying news from a fasiq (Quran 49:6). This verse implies that a report from an unreliable person requires caution, and analogically, a witness must be reliable ('adl).

Guided Path
1
Understand the objectors' argument
The objectors say: 'You (Hanafis) carry the absolute 'witness' in the debt verse (2:282) to the restricted 'just witness' in the divorce verse (65:2), even though the contexts differ.' They argue this is inconsistent with the rule that mutlaq is only carried to muqayyad when the cause/incident is the same.
The objectors assume the Hanafis rely solely on the absolute-restricted principle.
2
Identify the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: The condition of justice is not derived from carrying the absolute to the restricted between those two verses. Instead, it is derived from a third text: Quran 49:6, 'If a fasiq comes to you with news, verify it.'
This text commands caution when receiving news from an untrustworthy person.
3
Analyze the istinbat (derivation)
From the command to verify a fasiq's report, we infer that an untrustworthy person's statement is not reliable. Since a witness provides testimony (a form of news), a witness must be trustworthy. This leads to the requirement of 'adalah (justice) in witnesses.
This is a form of qiyas or istidlal: the ruling for witnesses is derived from the general principle of caution.
4
Classify the textual operation
This is not haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad but rather a derivation from a separate text that imposes a condition (shart).
The Hanafis show that each issue has its own proof; there is no inconsistency.

Source Text

(القمر) بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع 458 فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة وهي قوله علية : "لا زكاة في العوامل والحوامل والعلوفة؛ لأن هذه الثلاثة كلّها غير السائمة، وما عملنا بحمل المطلق على المقيد. والأمر بالتثبت في نبأ الفاسق أوجب نسخ الإطلاق، يعني هكذا إنما عملنا في المسألة أي إطلاق الشاهد الثانية بالنص الثالث الوارد في باب التثبت في نبأ الفاسق، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِنْ جَاءَكُمْ فَاسِقٌ بِنَبَإٍ فَتَبَيَّنُوا﴾ فلما كان خبر الفاسق واجب التوقف فلا جَرَم تُشترط العدالة في المُخبِر، وما عملنا بحمل المطلق على المقيد. وقيل: إن القران في النظم هذا وجه رابع من الوجوه الفاسدة، ذهب إليه مالك حليم وهو أن الجمع بين الكلامين بحرف الواو يوجب القران في الحكم، أي الاشتراك فيه؛ لأن رعاية المناسبة بين الجُمل شرط. فلا تجب الزكاة على الصبي لاقترانها بالصلاة في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلاةَ وَآتُوا الزَّكَاةَ﴾، فهما جملتان كاملتان عُطفت إحداهما على الأخرى بالواو ؛ فيقتضي التسوية بينهما، لا زكاة في العوامل إلخ وفي "الهداية : وليس في العوامل والحوامل صدقة خلافا لمالك له، له ظواهر النصوص، ولنا قوله علية : ليس في العوامل والحوامل والعلوفة صدقة. غير السائمة فسقط الزكاة عن غير السائمة؛ فلذا قيّدنا الإبل بالسائمة لا لأن المطلق محمول على المقيد في الرواية الأخرى. (القمر) بالنص الثالث أي وراء النصين اللذين كلامنا فيهما. (القمر) إن جاءكم فاسق بنبا: أي خبر فتبينوا، أي فتعرفوا وتفصحوا وقرئ فتثبتوا أي فتوقفوا إلى أن يتبين لكم الحال.(القمر) إن القرآن: بكسر القاف بمعنى الاتصال (المحشي الجمع بين الكلامين إيماء إلى أنه ليس المراد القران في النظم بين أي لفظين كانا وإن كانا مفردين بل المراد القران بين الكلامين. (القمر) فيقتضي التسوية بينهما ولا صلاة على الصبي؛ فلا يكون الزكاة عليه أيضًا. (القمر) غريب بهذا اللفظ، و روى أبو داود والدار قطني عن علي الله قال: "ليس في البقر العوامل صدقة" و رجح وقفه، وفي معناه أحاديث كثيرة مروية في سنن أبي داود وغيره قال العلي القاري له: هذا الحديث وإن لم يرو بهذا المحدثين فقد روته الفقهاء واحتجوا به؛ فلا يضرهم عدم اطلاع غيرهم عليه. [إشراق الأبصار: 15]
Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division: Chapter on mentioning the invalid aspects. And his statement (peace be upon him): 'In five grazing camels, one sheep' ... regarding the causes; because the camel is the cause of zakat, and the first is absolute and the second is restricted by grazing, and you have carried the absolute here to the restricted until you said: Zakat is not obligatory on non-grazing [animals]. And also you said: When the incident is different, the absolute is not carried to the restricted, and you have carried the verse 'And call to witness two witnesses from among your men' to the verse 'And call to witness two just men from among you' until you conditioned justice in witnessing absolutely, even though the first is about the incident of debt and the second about the chapter of revocation in divorce? So he answered: That the restriction of grazing in the first issue and the restriction of justice in the second issue did not necessitate negation of what is beyond them as you understood. But the well-known Sunnah concerning the negation of zakat on working and pregnant animals necessitated abrogation of the absolute, meaning the absolute of grazing camels etc. The grazing camels that are not fed in the enclosure are called sawam, sa'ima, and sawa'im... [continues translation of definitions and hadith references] ... And it is said: The conjunction in the wording is a fourth invalid aspect, held by Malik (may Allah have mercy on him), which is that joining two statements with the letter waw necessitates conjunction in the ruling, i.e., sharing in it; because observance of harmony between sentences is a condition. So zakat is not obligatory on a minor because of its conjunction with prayer in the verse 'Establish prayer and give zakat', for they are two complete sentences conjoined by waw; so it necessitates equality between them, so no zakat on working animals etc. And in al-Hidayah: 'There is no sadaqah on working and pregnant animals, contrary to Malik; he has the apparent of the texts, and we have his statement (peace be upon him): There is no sadaqah on working, pregnant, and stabled animals.' They are non-grazing, so zakat falls from non-grazing; hence we restricted camels to grazing, not because the absolute is carried to the restricted in the other narration... [continues]
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the 'invalid aspects' (wujuh faseda) raised against the Hanafi application of the principle that the absolute is carried to the restricted (haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad). The author, likely a Hanafi commentator, addresses two main objections and then a fourth one (attributed to Malik). The first objection concerns zakat on camels: opponents argue that Hanafis carry the absolute term 'camel' to the restricted 'grazing camel' (saa'ima) based on a hadith, and then use the same method inconsistently. The author replies that the Hanafis do not rely on that principle for the negation of zakat on non-grazing camels; rather, they rely on a separate Sunnah (the hadith about working and pregnant camels) which abrogates the absolute meaning. The second objection is about witness testimony: opponents argue that Hanafis carry the absolute witness statement to the restricted just witness condition from another verse. The reply is that they rely on the third text (Quran 49:6) which requires verification of a fasiq's report, implying that a witness must be 'adl (just) in all cases. The fourth invalid aspect is from Imam Malik, who argued that because zakat is conjoined with prayer in the Quran by waw, the rulings must be equal, so if prayer is not obligatory on minors, zakat is not. The Hanafis reject this, citing the hadith specifically exempting working and pregnant animals from zakat, indicating that conjunction does not necessitate identical rulings in all circumstances. The passage concludes with a hadith reference from al-Hidayah supporting the Hanafi position. Throughout, the commentator emphasizes that the Hanafi school uses additional texts (sunnah or Quran) to specify the ruling, not merely the absolute-restricted principle.

Key Terms
العدالة (al-ʿadāla): Justice; moral probity, uprightness in religious observance and social dealings.
الفاسق (al-fāsiq): A sinner; someone who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, lacking moral integrity.

The Hanafi school derives the condition of witness justice from Quran 49:6, not from the absolute-restricted principle between other verses.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Conjunction (Qiran) in the Quran: Does It Imply Equal Ruling? Hanafi vs. Maliki
Refutation of the fourth invalid argument attributed to Imam Malik on conjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the argument that conjunction (qiran) by waw necessitates equal rulings and apply the Hanafi counter-argument using a specific hadith.

Core Idea
Conjunction by waw does not require identical rulings; specific textual proofs can override the apparent implication of equality.

The third invalid argument presented in the passage is attributed to Imam Malik. He argues that because zakat is conjoined with prayer in the Quran by the letter waw (وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ وَآتُوا الزَّكَاةَ), the rulings of the two should be identical. Since prayer is not obligatory on minors, zakat should not be obligatory on minors either. The Hanafi school rejects this argument by pointing to a specific hadith that clearly exempts certain types of camels from zakat. This shows that conjunction does not force identical rulings in all aspects.

Guided Path
1
Understand Malik's argument from qiran
Malik argues that the conjunction (waw) between 'establish prayer' and 'give zakat' in Quran 2:43 and similar verses creates a connection that implies shared rulings. Since prayer is not wajib on a minor (due to lack of taklif), zakat should also not be wajib on a minor.
This is an argument based on the implication of the conjunction.
2
Identify the Hanafi response
The Hanafis reject this by citing a specific prophetic hadith that explicitly exempts working, pregnant, and stabled camels from zakat. This hadith is evidence that the ruling on zakat has its own specific conditions and is not simply a mirror of the ruling on prayer.
The hadith overrides the general implication of the conjunction.
3
Analyze the usul principle at stake
The principle: Qiran (conjunction) does not necessitate equal rulings (qiran fi al-nazm la yufid al-qiran fi al-hukm). The waw is a conjunction for the wording, not necessarily for the legal effect.
This is a well-established Hanafi usul rule.
4
Apply to the specific case of minors
The hadith about non-sa'ima camels shows that zakat has an independent scope. The obligation of zakat on a minor's wealth (if they own sa'ima camels) is established by other proofs. The conjunction with prayer does not change that.
Thus, Malik's argument is invalid.

(السنبلي) بالإسامة إلخ بكسر الهمزة من باب الإفعال ماضيه أسامَ يقال أسأم الإبل أرعاها، وقيل: أخرجها إلى المرعى والحضرة على الركبة حضرها، وإليه ببصره رماه .به (السنبلي) في غير السائمة من الحيوانات المملوكات في "رسائل الأركان ثم السائمة عندنا ما يكتفي في أكثر الحول بالرعي، ويقصد منه الدر أو النسل إقامة للأكثر مقام الكل (القمر) وَاسْتَشْهِدُوا شَهِيدَيْنِ إلخ قال في الحاشية: ولكن يرد عليه أن هذا النص أيضًا مقيّد بقوله: ﴿ مِمَّنْ تَرْضَوْنَ مِنَ الشُّهَدَاءِ﴾ والشاهد المرضي هو العدل.(القمر) الإشهاد وكذا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَشْهِدُوا إِذَا تَبَايَعْتُمْ﴾ (المحشي) في حادثة الدين: أي معاملة ما داين البعض بعضا، والمداينة المعاملة نسيئةً معطيًا أو آخذا. Key Terms
القران (al-qirān): Conjunction of two sentences with waw, argued by some to imply equal ruling.

According to the Hanafi school as presented in the passage, which of the following best describes the effect of grammatical conjunction (qiran) by waw on legal rulings?
A) It necessitates that the rulings of conjoined items are identical in all aspects.
B) It is a strong indication but can be overridden by a specific Sunnah.

The Invalid Argument of Haml al-Mutlaq 'ala al-Muqayyad
Enumeration and rebuttal of the first invalid usul argument regarding zakat on working animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi critique of applying haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad in the case of zakat on working animals, and explain why they instead rely on the command of investigation for a transgressor.

Core Idea
Haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad is not applied when a separate text abrogates the absolute.

In this passage, the commentator discusses one of the 'invalid faces' (al-wujuh al-fasidah) in usul al-fiqh: the claim that the hadith exempting working animals from zakat should be used to restrict the absolute command of zakat on all livestock. The Hanafis respond that they do not apply haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad here; rather, the command to verify news from a transgressor (Quran 49:6) shows that the absolute command regarding acceptance of news is abrogated or overridden. This lesson unpacks the methodological error and the correct Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalid argument
The opponent claims that the hadith exempting working animals from zakat should restrict the general zakat command, but the Hanafis refuse to apply haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad here.
2
Locate the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis cite Quran 49:6, which commands verification of a transgressor's news, indicating that the absolute acceptance of news is abrogated.
3
Understand the reasoning
The Hanafis argue that the command to verify implies that a reporter must be upright, thus the absolute (general acceptance) is overridden by the specific command, not simply restricted.
4
Note the distinction
This shows that haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad is not automatic; it is conditional on the absence of a text that abrogates or overrides.
5
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: For the opponent, the hadith restricts the general zakat rule; for Hanafis, the hadith is not the primary proof; instead, the ruling on zakat for working animals is derived from other texts.

فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة
وهي قوله عليه : "لا زكاة في العوامل والحوامل والعلوفة؛ لأن هذه الثلاثة كلّها غير السائمة، وما عملنا بحمل المطلق على المقيد.
والأمر بالتثبت في نبأ الفاسق أوجب نسخ الإطلاق، يعني هكذا إنما عملنا في المسألة أي إطلاق الشاهد الثانية بالنص الثالث الوارد في باب التثبت في نبأ الفاسق، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِنْ جَاءَكُمْ فَاسِقٌ بِنَيَا فَتَبَيَّنُوا﴾ (الحجرات: ٦) فلما كان خبر الفاسق واجب التوقف فلا جَرَم تُشترط العدالة في المُخبِر، وما عملنا بحمل المطلق على المقيد.
وقيل: إن القران في النظم هذا وجه رابع من الوجوه الفاسدة، ذهب إليه مالك حليم وهو أن الجمع بين الكلامين بحرف الواو يوجب القران في الحكم، أي الاشتراك فيه؛ لأن رعاية المناسبة بين الجُمل شرط.
فلا تجب الزكاة على الصبي لاقترانها بالصلاة في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلاةَ وَآتُوا الزَّكَاةَ﴾ (البقرة: ٤٣) فهما جملتان كاملتان عُطفت إحداهما على الأخرى بالواو؛ فيقتضي التسوية بينهما، لا زكاة في العوامل إلخ وفي "الهداية: وليس في العوامل والحوامل صدقة خلافا لمالك له، له ظواهر النصوص، ولنا قوله عليه: ليس في العوامل والحوامل والعلوفة صدقة. غير السائمة فسقط الزكاة عن غير السائمة؛ فلذا قيّدنا الإبل بالسائمة لا لأن المطلق محمول على المقيد في الرواية الأخرى.
(القمر) بالنص الثالث أي وراء النصين اللذين كلامنا فيهما. (القمر) إن جاءكم فاسق بنبا: أي خبر فتبينوا، أي فتعرفوا وتفصحوا وقرئ فتثبتوا أي فتوقفوا إلى أن يتبين لكم الحال.(القمر)
إن القرآن: بكسر القاف بمعنى الاتصال (المحشي الجمع بين الكلامين إيماء إلى أنه ليس المراد القران في النظم بين أي لفظين كانا وإن كانا مفردين بل المراد القران بين الكلامين. (القمر)
فيقتضي التسوية بينهما ولا صلاة على الصبي؛ فلا يكون الزكاة عليه أيضًا. (القمر)
غريب بهذا اللفظ، و روى أبو داود والدار قطني عن علي الله قال: "ليس في البقر العوامل صدقة" و رجح وقفه، وفي معناه أحاديث كثيرة مروية في سنن أبي داود وغيره قال العلي القاري له: هذا الحديث وإن لم يُرو بهذا المحدثين فقد روته الفقهاء واحتجوا به؛ فلا يضرهم عدم اطلاع غيرهم عليه. [إشراق الأبصار: ١٥]
اللفظ
عن

وعندنا أيضًا لا تجب الزكاة على الصبي،
"لا زكاة في مال الصبي
لكن لا لأجل العطف، بل لقوله عليه:
واعتبروا بالجملة الناقصة أي قاس هؤلاء القائلون الجملة الكاملة المعطوفة على الكاملة مثل قوله زينب طالق وهند "طالق" بالجملة الناقصة المعطوفة على الكاملة مثل قوله: زينب" طالق وهند"، فإنهما يشتركان في الخبر لا محالة، فكذا الأوليان.
وقلنا: إن عطف الجملة على الجملة لا يوجب الشركة؛ لأن الشركة إنما وجبت في الجملة الناقصة لافتقارها إلى ما تتمّ به وهو الخبر، فإن هندًا كان محتاجًا إلى طالق؛ فلهذا الجملة الناقصة جاءت الشركة، بخلاف الكاملة المعطوفة؛ فإنها تامة.
أي بين المعطوف والمعطوف عليه
فإذا تمت بنفسها لا تجب الشركة إلّا فيما تفتقر إليه كالتعليق في قوله: "إن دخلت الدار أي الجملة التامة أي بالشرط فأنتِ طالق وعبدي حرّ ؛ فإن الجملة الأخيرة وإن كانت تامة إيقاعا، لكنّها ناقصة تعليقًا؛
وعندنا أيضًا إلخ هذا جواب لسؤال مقدّر يرد علينا تقريره أنكم أيها الحنفية أيضا استعملتم هذا الوجه الفاسد؛ فقلتم بعدم وجوب الزكاة على الصبي؛ لأن الصلاة غير واجبة عليه، وحكم الزكاة حكم الصلاة بقرانها بها في الآية الكريمة؛ فالزكاة أيضًا غير واجبة عليه؟ وتقرير الجواب لا يخفى. (السنبلي) لا لأجل العطف أي لا لأجل قران الجملتين في العطف . (القمر) بالجملة الناقصة : المراد بالجملة الناقصة مفرد إذا انضم إلى ما قبله أو إلى شيء آخر يكون جملة تامة (القمر) في الجملة الناقصة أي في عطف الجملة الناقصة على الكاملة. وهو الخبر أقول: لعلّ المراد هو الخبر مثلاً؛ لأن نقصان الجملة لا يلزم أن يكون بعدم ذكر الخبر، بل قد يكون النقصان بغيره كعدم ذكر المبتدأ. (القمر) لا تجب الشركة إلخ والواو لمطلق الشركة في ثبوت مضمون الجملتين في الواقع، فقياسُهم الجملة التامة على الجملة الناقصة قياس مع الفارق وهو تحقق الضرورة. (القمر) إيقاعا إلخ : يعني لو قال القائل: "عبدي حرّ" فقط لما احتاج في إيقاع الحرّية إلى غيره من الكلمات. (السنبلي) ناقصة تعليقا لأنه عرف بدلالة الحال أنّ غرضه التعليق لا التنجيز. (القمر)
هذا اللفظ مشهور في كتب الفقه و روی محمد بن الحسن الله في "كتاب "الآثار" رقم: ۲۹۷ باب زكاة الذهب والفضة ومال اليتيم عن ابن مسعود الله أنه قال ليس في مال اليتيم زكاة.
Section on mentioning the invalid aspects. They are: His saying (peace be upon him): 'No zakah on working animals, pregnant animals, and animals fed at home; because these three are all not grazing (sa'imah), and we do not act by carrying the absolute to the restricted.' And the command to investigate in the news of the transgressor necessitated abrogation of the absolute, meaning thus we act in the issue, i.e., the absolute of the second witness by the third text occurring in the chapter of investigation in the news of the transgressor, which is His saying: 'O you who believe, if a transgressor comes to you with news, then investigate' (al-Hujurat:6). So when the news of the transgressor requires suspension, there is no doubt that the condition of integrity is required in the informant, and we do not act by carrying the absolute to the restricted. And it is said: The joining in the wording is a fourth invalid aspect, held by Malik (or graceful?), which is that joining two statements with the letter waw necessitates joining in the ruling, i.e., sharing in it; because upholding the appropriateness between the sentences is a condition. So zakah is not obligatory on a minor due to its association with prayer in His saying: 'Establish prayer and give zakah' (al-Baqarah:43). They are two complete sentences, one coordinated with the other by waw; thus it requires equality between them. [Then the text continues with glosses and commentary including hadith and responses.] And according to us also, zakah is not obligatory on a minor, but not because of the conjunction, but due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'No zakah on the wealth of a minor.' And they analogized by the incomplete sentence, i.e., those who say that the complete sentence coordinated with the complete, like saying 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind is divorced', by the incomplete sentence coordinated with the complete, like saying 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind', for they share in the predicate inevitably, so likewise the first two. And we said: The conjunction of a sentence to a sentence does not necessitate sharing; because sharing is only necessitated in the incomplete sentence due to its need for completion, which is the predicate. For Hind was in need of 'divorced'; therefore sharing occurred in the incomplete sentence, unlike the complete coordinated one, for it is complete. [...]
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on usul al-fiqh discussing invalid arguments (al-wujuh al-fasidah) that opponents use. It presents two main invalid approaches. First, the argument that the hadith about working, pregnant, and stall-fed animals being exempt from zakah is used to restrict the general command of zakah on grazing livestock. The commentator explains that the Hanafis do not apply haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad here; rather, the verse about investigating the news of a transgressor (49:6) shows that the absolute is abrogated or restricted by that command. Second, a more detailed discussion: the claim that joining two sentences with 'waw' implies sharing in the ruling. For example, the conjunction of prayer and zakah in the Quran is used by some (like Imam Malik, according to the text) to argue that if prayer is not obligatory on a minor, then zakah is also not obligatory. The Hanafi response is multi-fold: they agree that zakah is not obligatory on a minor, but not because of the conjunction; rather, there is a specific hadith ('No zakah on the wealth of a minor'). Moreover, they reject the analogy that complete sentences joined by 'waw' necessitate sharing in all aspects. In sentence grammar, incomplete sentences (like 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind') require sharing because the predicate is missing; but complete sentences (like 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind is divorced') are independent. The exception is when the context indicates a conditional link, such as in a suspended divorce. The Hanafis thus argue that the opponent's qiyas is flawed (qiyas ma'a al-fariq). The text also includes glosses clarifying terms and the opinion of Imam Muhammad and Ibn Mas'ud regarding zakat on the wealth of orphans.

Key Terms
الوجوه الفاسدة (al-wujuh al-fasidah): Invalid arguments or methods in usul al-fiqh that are rejected by the Hanafis
القران في النظم (al-qiran fi al-nazm): The claim that joining two expressions in a single textual structure (by waw) necessitates a shared ruling
حمل المطلق على المقيد (haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad): Carrying an absolute (mutlaq) text to a restricted (muqayyad) one, a principle of interpretation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why do they not apply haml al-mutlaq to the hadith about working animals?
A) Because the hadith is not authentic
B) Because the absolute command about news has been abrogated by Quran 49:6
C) Because the hadith is about working animals, which are not subject to zakat
D) Because they prefer qiyas

Key Takeaway
Haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad is not automatically applied; it depends on the presence of overriding texts, such as abrogation.
