Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
The Invalid Argument of Qiran (Conjunction) in Rulings
Enumeration and rebuttal of the invalid 'qiran' argument regarding prayer and zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete and incomplete sentences in usul al-fiqh, and explain why the conjunction of complete sentences with 'waw' does not necessitate a shared ruling.

Core Idea
Conjunction of two complete sentences with 'waw' does not imply equality in ruling; each sentence is independent.

This part of the passage addresses another invalid argument: the claim that when the Quran joins 'Establish prayer' and 'Give zakah' with the waw, the two rulings must be identical, so that if prayer is not obligatory on a minor, then zakah is also not obligatory. The Hanafis rebut this by distinguishing between complete and incomplete sentences, and by citing a specific hadith about zakah on a minor's wealth. This lesson will dissect the reasoning and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
State the invalid argument (qiran)
The opponent (Imam Malik) claims that joining two sentences with 'waw' implies sharing in ruling, using the example of prayer and zakah.
2
Identify the Hanafi response
The Hanafis agree that zakah is not obligatory on a minor, but not because of conjunction; rather because of a specific hadith: 'No zakah on the wealth of a minor.'
3
Analyze the grammatical distinction
The Hanafis differentiate between complete and incomplete sentences. Incomplete sentences (e.g., 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind') require sharing the predicate because the second part is missing. Complete sentences (e.g., 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind is divorced') are independent.
4
Consider the exception
The exception is when context indicates a conditional link, e.g., 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and my slave is free.' The second sentence is complete in content but incomplete in suspending (ta'liq).
5
Apply the distinction to the case
The verse 'Establish prayer and give zakah' consists of two independent complete sentences; therefore, they do not share rulings. The obligation of zakah on minors is determined by its own evidence.

فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة
وهي قوله عليه : "لا زكاة في العوامل والحوامل والعلوفة؛ لأن هذه الثلاثة كلّها غير السائمة، وما عملنا بحمل المطلق على المقيد.
والأمر بالتثبت في نبأ الفاسق أوجب نسخ الإطلاق، يعني هكذا إنما عملنا في المسألة أي إطلاق الشاهد الثانية بالنص الثالث الوارد في باب التثبت في نبأ الفاسق، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِنْ جَاءَكُمْ فَاسِقٌ بِنَيَا فَتَبَيَّنُوا﴾ (الحجرات: ٦) فلما كان خبر الفاسق واجب التوقف فلا جَرَم تُشترط العدالة في المُخبِر، وما عملنا بحمل المطلق على المقيد.
وقيل: إن القران في النظم هذا وجه رابع من الوجوه الفاسدة، ذهب إليه مالك حليم وهو أن الجمع بين الكلامين بحرف الواو يوجب القران في الحكم، أي الاشتراك فيه؛ لأن رعاية المناسبة بين الجُمل شرط.
فلا تجب الزكاة على الصبي لاقترانها بالصلاة في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلاةَ وَآتُوا الزَّكَاةَ﴾ (البقرة: ٤٣) فهما جملتان كاملتان عُطفت إحداهما على الأخرى بالواو؛ فيقتضي التسوية بينهما، لا زكاة في العوامل إلخ وفي "الهداية: وليس في العوامل والحوامل صدقة خلافا لمالك له، له ظواهر النصوص، ولنا قوله عليه: ليس في العوامل والحوامل والعلوفة صدقة. غير السائمة فسقط الزكاة عن غير السائمة؛ فلذا قيّدنا الإبل بالسائمة لا لأن المطلق محمول على المقيد في الرواية الأخرى.
(القمر) بالنص الثالث أي وراء النصين اللذين كلامنا فيهما. (القمر) إن جاءكم فاسق بنبا: أي خبر فتبينوا، أي فتعرفوا وتفصحوا وقرئ فتثبتوا أي فتوقفوا إلى أن يتبين لكم الحال.(القمر)
إن القرآن: بكسر القاف بمعنى الاتصال (المحشي الجمع بين الكلامين إيماء إلى أنه ليس المراد القران في النظم بين أي لفظين كانا وإن كانا مفردين بل المراد القران بين الكلامين. (القمر)
فيقتضي التسوية بينهما ولا صلاة على الصبي؛ فلا يكون الزكاة عليه أيضًا. (القمر)
غريب بهذا اللفظ، و روى أبو داود والدار قطني عن علي الله قال: "ليس في البقر العوامل صدقة" و رجح وقفه، وفي معناه أحاديث كثيرة مروية في سنن أبي داود وغيره قال العلي القاري له: هذا الحديث وإن لم يُرو بهذا المحدثين فقد روته الفقهاء واحتجوا به؛ فلا يضرهم عدم اطلاع غيرهم عليه. [إشراق الأبصار: ١٥]

عن

وعندنا أيضًا لا تجب الزكاة على الصبي،
"لا زكاة في مال الصبي
لكن لا لأجل العطف، بل لقوله عليه:
واعتبروا بالجملة الناقصة أي قاس هؤلاء القائلون الجملة الكاملة المعطوفة على الكاملة مثل قوله زينب طالق وهند "طالق" بالجملة الناقصة المعطوفة على الكاملة مثل قوله: زينب" طالق وهند"، فإنهما يشتركان في الخبر لا محالة، فكذا الأوليان.
وقلنا: إن عطف الجملة على الجملة لا يوجب الشركة؛ لأن الشركة إنما وجبت في الجملة الناقصة لافتقارها إلى ما تتمّ به وهو الخبر، فإن هندًا كان محتاجًا إلى طالق؛ فلهذا الجملة الناقصة جاءت الشركة، بخلاف الكاملة المعطوفة؛ فإنها تامة.
أي بين المعطوف والمعطوف عليه
فإذا تمت بنفسها لا تجب الشركة إلّا فيما تفتقر إليه كالتعليق في قوله: "إن دخلت الدار أي الجملة التامة أي بالشرط فأنتِ طالق وعبدي حرّ ؛ فإن الجملة الأخيرة وإن كانت تامة إيقاعا، لكنّها ناقصة تعليقًا؛
وعندنا أيضًا إلخ هذا جواب لسؤال مقدّر يرد علينا تقريره أنكم أيها الحنفية أيضا استعملتم هذا الوجه الفاسد؛ فقلتم بعدم وجوب الزكاة على الصبي؛ لأن الصلاة غير واجبة عليه، وحكم الزكاة حكم الصلاة بقرانها بها في الآية الكريمة؛ فالزكاة أيضًا غير واجبة عليه؟ وتقرير الجواب لا يخفى. (السنبلي) لا لأجل العطف أي لا لأجل قران الجملتين في العطف . (القمر) بالجملة الناقصة : المراد بالجملة الناقصة مفرد إذا انضم إلى ما قبله أو إلى شيء آخر يكون جملة تامة (القمر) في الجملة الناقصة أي في عطف الجملة الناقصة على الكاملة. وهو الخبر أقول: لعلّ المراد هو الخبر مثلاً؛ لأن نقصان الجملة لا يلزم أن يكون بعدم ذكر الخبر، بل قد يكون النقصان بغيره كعدم ذكر المبتدأ. (القمر) لا تجب الشركة إلخ والواو لمطلق الشركة في ثبوت مضمون الجملتين في الواقع، فقياسُهم الجملة التامة على الجملة الناقصة قياس مع الفارق وهو تحقق الضرورة. (القمر) إيقاعا إلخ : يعني لو قال القائل: "عبدي حرّ" فقط لما احتاج في إيقاع الحرّية إلى غيره من الكلمات. (السنبلي) ناقصة تعليقا لأنه عرف بدلالة الحال أنّ غرضه التعليق لا التنجيز. (القمر)
هذا اللفظ مشهور في كتب الفقه و روی محمد بن الحسن الله في "كتاب "الآثار" رقم: ۲۹۷ باب زكاة الذهب والفضة ومال اليتيم عن ابن مسعود الله أنه قال ليس في مال اليتيم زكاة.
Section on mentioning the invalid aspects. They are: His saying (peace be upon him): 'No zakah on working animals, pregnant animals, and animals fed at home; because these three are all not grazing (sa'imah), and we do not act by carrying the absolute to the restricted.' And the command to investigate in the news of the transgressor necessitated abrogation of the absolute, meaning thus we act in the issue, i.e., the absolute of the second witness by the third text occurring in the chapter of investigation in the news of the transgressor, which is His saying: 'O you who believe, if a transgressor comes to you with news, then investigate' (al-Hujurat:6). So when the news of the transgressor requires suspension, there is no doubt that the condition of integrity is required in the informant, and we do not act by carrying the absolute to the restricted. And it is said: The joining in the wording is a fourth invalid aspect, held by Malik (or graceful?), which is that joining two statements with the letter waw necessitates joining in the ruling, i.e., sharing in it; because upholding the appropriateness between the sentences is a condition. So zakah is not obligatory on a minor due to its association with prayer in His saying: 'Establish prayer and give zakah' (al-Baqarah:43). They are two complete sentences, one coordinated with the other by waw; thus it requires equality between them. [Then the text continues with glosses and commentary including hadith and responses.] And according to us also, zakah is not obligatory on a minor, but not because of the conjunction, but due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'No zakah on the wealth of a minor.' And they analogized by the incomplete sentence, i.e., those who say that the complete sentence coordinated with the complete, like saying 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind is divorced', by the incomplete sentence coordinated with the complete, like saying 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind', for they share in the predicate inevitably, so likewise the first two. And we said: The conjunction of a sentence to a sentence does not necessitate sharing; because sharing is only necessitated in the incomplete sentence due to its need for completion, which is the predicate. For Hind was in need of 'divorced'; therefore sharing occurred in the incomplete sentence, unlike the complete coordinated one, for it is complete. [...]

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on usul al-fiqh discussing invalid arguments (al-wujuh al-fasidah) that opponents use. It presents two main invalid approaches. First, the argument that the hadith about working, pregnant, and stall-fed animals being exempt from zakah is used to restrict the general command of zakah on grazing livestock. The commentator explains that the Hanafis do not apply haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad here; rather, the verse about investigating the news of a transgressor (49:6) shows that the absolute is abrogated or restricted by that command. Second, a more detailed discussion: the claim that joining two sentences with 'waw' implies sharing in the ruling. For example, the conjunction of prayer and zakah in the Quran is used by some (like Imam Malik, according to the text) to argue that if prayer is not obligatory on a minor, then zakah is also not obligatory. The Hanafi response is multi-fold: they agree that zakah is not obligatory on a minor, but not because of the conjunction; rather, there is a specific hadith ('No zakah on the wealth of a minor'). Moreover, they reject the analogy that complete sentences joined by 'waw' necessitate sharing in all aspects. In sentence grammar, incomplete sentences (like 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind') require sharing because the predicate is missing; but complete sentences (like 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind is divorced') are independent. The exception is when the context indicates a conditional link, such as in a suspended divorce. The Hanafis thus argue that the opponent's qiyas is flawed (qiyas ma'a al-fariq). The text also includes glosses clarifying terms and the opinion of Imam Muhammad and Ibn Mas'ud regarding zakat on the wealth of orphans.

Key Terms
الوجوه الفاسدة (al-wujuh al-fasidah): Invalid arguments or methods in usul al-fiqh that are rejected by the Hanafis
القران في النظم (al-qiran fi al-nazm): The claim that joining two expressions in a single textual structure (by waw) necessitates a shared ruling
حمل المطلق على المقيد (haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad): Carrying an absolute (mutlaq) text to a restricted (muqayyad) one, a principle of interpretation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is zakah not obligatory on a minor?
A) Because the conjunction of prayer and zakah implies they have the same ruling
B) Because of the hadith: 'No zakah on the wealth of a minor'
C) Because minors are not required to perform prayer
D) Because the absolute is restricted by the specific

Key Takeaway
Conjunction of complete sentences with 'waw' does not necessitate shared ruling; each sentence is independent unless context indicates otherwise.

General Expressions in Conditional and Responsive Contexts
Explaining the fifth invalid aspect regarding general expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a general expression based on its context (independent, dependent response, or conditional) and apply the Hanafi rule to determine whether it is restricted to its specific cause.

Core Idea
The generality of wording prevails unless the expression is entirely dependent on its context.

In usul al-fiqh, one critical skill is determining when a general expression ('amm) retains its general application and when it is restricted to a specific cause (sabab). The Hanafi school maintains the principle 'al-ibrah li-umum al-lafz' but makes an exception for expressions that are entirely dependent on context, such as a conditional clause or a direct response. In this lesson, we examine a passage from Noor al-Anwar that details four scenarios—three agreed-upon and one disputed—to sharpen your ability to identify when a general expression is truly general and when it is restricted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios of general expression context
Read the passage carefully to find the four categories: (1) independent statement (mubtada'), (2) conditional clause without addition, (3) response without addition, (4) response with addition. The first three are agreed upon; the fourth is disputed.
The first scenario is independent statement — no dispute that it is general. The second and third are dependent contexts where the expression is restricted to its cause by consensus.
2
Apply the rule for agreed-upon scenarios
For scenarios 2 (conditional clause like 'if you enter, you are divorced' based on a specific event) and 3 (response that exactly matches the question, e.g., 'bala' or 'na'am'), the general expression is restricted to the specific cause. Understand the reasoning: the expression is structurally dependent on the preceding context.
Example from text: 'stoned' after the Ma'iz case is a conditional clause and applies only to that stoning, not all stonings.
3
Analyze the disputed scenario: response with addition
Focus on the fourth scenario: a response that includes extra wording beyond the answer, e.g., 'If I eat lunch today, my slave is free' when invited. According to the Hanafi school, the addition (ziyada) makes the statement independent, so it is general and not restricted to the cause.
The opposing view (al-ba'd, possibly Zufar) holds that the response context still restricts it. The Hanafi argue that the addition cannot be rendered void, so it shows the speaker intended an independent statement.
4
Trace the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Consider the real-world difference: if the invitee eats lunch elsewhere (not the invited meal), the Hanafi view says the slave is freed because the condition is general; the opposing view says no, because it is restricted to the invitation.
This illustrates why the classification matters for fatwa and legal reasoning.
5
Integrate the Hanafi principle on general expressions
The overarching Hanafi rule is that consideration is for the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the cause. The exceptions (scenarios 2 and 3) are narrow: only when the expression is entirely dependent and lacks independence. Any addition tips the scale back to generality.
If the speaker claims he intended the response context despite the addition, he is believed inwardly (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an), because the apparent meaning (zahir) is independence.

Chapter on mentioning the invalid aspects. It became shared with it in suspension, unlike his statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and Zaynab the first is divorced', for it does not suspend Zaynab's divorce; because if his intent was suspension he would have said 'and Zaynab' without mentioning the predicate, since the predicate of both sentences is one, so when he repeats it, it is known that his intent is immediate effect. And the general [expression] when it comes out in the form of a conditional clause. This is a fifth invalid aspect that he mentioned contrary to the previous pattern where he presented his own school as primary and the invalid school as subsidiary. Its detail: that the general formula when applied concerning a specific person in a text or saying of the Companions, if it is an independent statement, there is no dispute that it is general for all its individuals and is not restricted to a specific cause in which it was revealed. But if it is not so, rather it comes out in the form of a conditional clause as narrated that Ma'iz committed adultery and was stoned, or the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forgot and prostrated. For his statement 'stoned' and 'prostrated' is a general expression valid in itself for every stoning and every prostration, but it occurred in the position of the conditional clause. ... Alternatively, coming out as a response without adding anything, such as one who is invited to lunch saying: 'If I eat lunch, my slave is free.' For it occurs in the position of the response and does not exceed it. ... And if he exceeds the extent of the response, such as the invitee saying: 'If I eat lunch today, my slave is free.' This is the fourth category under dispute. According to us, it is not restricted to the cause and becomes an independent statement, so that the addition is not rendered void, contrary to some others.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage from Noor al-Anwar is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Usul. It discusses the fifth 'invalid aspect' (wajh fasad) regarding the treatment of general expressions ('amm) that appear in the context of a condition (jaza') or a response (jawab). The author first distinguishes between a general statement that is independent (mubtada')—unanimously general—and one that is dependent, like a conditional clause. The example of the stoning of Ma'iz and the prostration of forgetfulness by the Prophet (peace be upon him) illustrates that even though the words 'stoned' and 'prostrated' are general in themselves, they occurred as penalties/effects of specific events, thus they are restricted to those specific acts, not applied generally to all stonings or prostrations. The text then divides three scenarios: (1) a response that is not independent itself (like 'bala' or 'na'am'), (2) a response that exactly matches the question without addition, and (3) a response that adds extra wording. For the first two, there is consensus that the general expression is restricted to its cause. For the third scenario (e.g., adding 'today' to the conditional response), there is a dispute: the Hanafi position (as per the text) holds that the addition makes the statement independent, thus the general expression is not restricted to the cause; the 'some' (al-ba'd) counter that it remains restricted. This dispute is a key usul issue about the effect of context on general expressions. The Hanafi principle 'al-ibrah li-umum al-lafz la li-khusus al-sabab' (consideration is for the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the cause) is cited, but with the qualification that if the wording is exactly like a response, it is restricted.

Key Terms
وجوه فاسدة (wujuh fasidah): Invalid aspects or flawed arguments in usul reasoning that the author refutes.
عام ('amm): A general expression that applies to all individuals of its category.
سبب (sabab): The specific occasion or cause that prompted the legal text.
جزاء (jaza'): The conditional clause (the result part of a conditional sentence).
جواب (jawab): A response to a question or statement.
مبتدأ (mubtada'): An independent statement not tied to a condition or prior context.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: a general expression in a response context is restricted only if it exactly matches the answer; any addition makes it independent and general.

The Generality of Praise/Blame Expressions: A Hanafi Defense
Rejecting the argument that general expressions lose generality in praise/blame contexts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that general words in praise or blame retain their generality, explain the opposing Shafi'i argument, and identify the practical difference regarding zakat on women's jewelry.

Core Idea
A general word in praise or blame remains general—context does not restrict it.

This passage tackles a fundamental usul dispute: do general expressions used for praise or blame lose their generality? Some scholars argue they apply only to the specific individuals for whom the verse was revealed. The Hanafi school, however, insists that the word's general indication remains, and the context of praise or blame does not act as a restrictor. This principle has direct fiqh consequences, such as whether the verse about hoarding gold and silver (Q 9:34) applies to women's jewelry, making zakat obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rejected claim
First, recognize that some scholars (e.g., certain Shafi'is) assert that general words in praise or blame have no generality. They argue that such verses apply only to the specific individuals mentioned in the occasion of revelation.
This is called the 'sixth invalid aspect' in the text.
2
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi position is that the word's indication of generality is primary and is not overridden by the context of praise or blame. The word is general by linguistic convention (wad‘), and no specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is present.
The text emphasizes that the explicit wording (ṣarīḥ) takes precedence over hidden context (bāṭin).
3
Apply the principle to the example
Apply this to Q 9:34: the masculine plural 'alladhīna' is general. Even though the verse was revealed about specific hoarders, it includes all who hoard, including women, by the principle of taghlīb (predominant usage).
Taghlīb allows the masculine form to include females when the context does not exclude them.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute directly affects fiqh: Hanafis rule that zakat is obligatory on women's jewelry (silver/gold ornaments), while Shafi'is do not, as they restrict the verse's generality.
This shows that usul principles have real-world legal impact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: الكلام المذكور للمدح أو الذم لا عموم له وإن كان اللفظ عاما، وهذا هو الوجه السادس من الوجوه الفاسدة، فلا يكون عندهم قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الْأَبْرَارَ لَفِي نَعِيمٍ وَإِنَّ الْفُجَّارَ لَفِي جَحِيم﴾ مما يستدل به على حال كل بَرّ وفاجر، بل على من نزل في حقهم فقط، والباقي يقاس عليهم أو يُثبت بنص آخر. وعندنا هذا فاسد؛ لأن اللفظ دال على العموم؛ فلا يُنافيه دلالته على المدح والذم أيضًا؛ فحينئذ يجوز أن يتمسك بعموم قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يَكْتِرُونَ الذَّهَبَ وَالْفِضَّةَ﴾ الآيةَ على وجوب الزكاة في حليّ النساء وإن كان واردا في قوم مخصوص كنزوا الذهب والفضة، و يكون إطلاق صيغة المذكر أعني "الذين" عليهن تغليبا كما حررته في "التفسير الأحمدي".
And it is said: Speech used for praise or blame has no generality, even if the word is general, and this is the sixth invalid aspect. So according to them, the verse of the Most High: 'Indeed the righteous will be in pleasure, and indeed the wicked will in hellfire' is not used to infer the state of every righteous and wicked person, but only those about whom it was revealed; the rest are analogically deduced from them or established by another text. And according to us, this is invalid because the word indicates generality, and its indication of praise and blame does not negate that. Therefore, it is permissible to rely on the generality of the verse of the Most High: 'And those who hoard gold and silver' for the obligation of zakah on women's jewelry, even if it was revealed concerning a specific people who hoarded gold and silver, and the masculine form 'those who' is applied to them by way of predominant usage (taghlib), as I have explained in al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage discusses the sixth invalid aspect (wajh fāsid) concerning general expressions used for praise or blame. Some scholars (e.g., certain followers of al-Shāfi‘ī, as reported by Imām al-Ḥaramayn) argue that general words in contexts of praise or blame lose their generality and apply only to the specific individuals or cases for which they were revealed. For example, the verse about the righteous and the wicked (Q 82:13-14) would only refer to those specific righteous and wicked persons, and other instances must be established by analogy or another text. The Hanafī response, presented here as the sound view, holds that this position is invalid. The word itself indicates generality (established by linguistic convention, wad‘), and the fact that it is used for praise or blame does not act as a deterrent (ṣārif) from that general meaning. Therefore, the general meaning must be upheld until a valid specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is found. As an application, the Hanafīs use the generality of the verse about hoarding gold and silver (Q 9:34) to argue that zakah is obligatory on women's jewelry (ḥily al-nisā'), even though the verse was revealed concerning a specific group of people. The masculine plural pronoun 'alladhīna' is applied to women by the principle of taghlīb (predominant usage). The passage refutes the opposing view by insisting on the priority of the explicit general wording over implied contextual cues.

Key Terms
عام ('āmm): A word that by its linguistic form encompasses all individuals of its category without restriction.
مدح (madḥ): Praise; a speech act that commends someone.
تغليب (taghlīb): Predominant usage: applying a masculine plural form to include females due to the dominance of the masculine in Arabic grammar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī position in this passage, why does the generality of the verse about hoarding gold and silver (Q 9:34) apply to women's jewelry?
A) Because the verse explicitly mentions women
B) Because the masculine plural 'alladhīna' includes women by taghlīb when the context is blame
C) Because women are analogically similar to men in hoarding
D) Because the verse was revealed about women

Key Takeaway
General words in praise or blame remain general; context does not restrict them unless a valid specifier is found.

Do General Terms Lose Generality in Praise/Blame Contexts?
Refutation of the sixth invalid view: general terms in praise/blame contexts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that general terms in praise/blame contexts retain their generality and apply this to the ruling on zakat on women's jewelry.

Core Idea
Praise or blame does not strip a general term of its generality; only an explicit specifier can restrict it.

Some scholars argue that when the Quran uses a general term in a context of praising or blaming specific individuals, that term only applies to those individuals. The Hanafis reject this, maintaining that the linguistic indication of generality is primary and cannot be overridden by context alone. This lesson examines the Hanafi argument and its practical consequence for the obligation of zakat on women's jewelry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested claim
Some Shafi'is hold that general terms in praise/blame contexts lose generality and apply only to the specific individuals mentioned.
This is the sixth invalid view being refuted.
2
State the Hanafi counterclaim
The Hanafis assert that generality remains; the wording's indication of generality is not negated by praise or blame.
Consideration is for the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the cause.
3
Examine the proof text
The verse on hoarding gold and silver (9:34) is used as a test case: although revealed about specific people, the Hanafis apply it generally to rule that zakat is obligatory on women's jewelry.
The verse's general wording 'those who hoard' includes all forms of gold and silver.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis counter the hyperbole argument: hyperbole without exaggeration is possible even when generality is intended, and the Lawgiver's speech is immune from exaggeration that would undermine trust in promises and threats.
No valid specifier (mukhassis) exists here; hence the general meaning holds.

Chapter on mentioning the invalid aspects. It is intended that 'general' here means 'absolute' as is the opinion of al-Shafi'i, not the technical term; so reflect. And it is said: The speech mentioned for praise or blame has no generality, even if the wording is general. This is the sixth invalid aspect. Thus, in their view, the verse of the Almighty: 'Indeed the righteous will be in pleasure and indeed the wicked will be in blaze' is not used as evidence for the state of every righteous and wicked person, but only for those in whose context it was revealed; the rest are analogized to them or proven by another text. In our view, this is invalid because the wording indicates generality; its indication of praise and blame does not negate that. Therefore, it is permissible to hold to the generality of the Almighty's verse: 'And those who hoard gold and silver' for the obligation of zakat on women's jewelry, even if it came down regarding a specific people who buried gold and silver, and the use of the masculine form 'those who' for them is predominant, as I explained in 'al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi'. (al-Qamar) 'Not the technical term': i.e., the one whose definition passed earlier. (al-Qamar) 'So reflect': perhaps it indicates a third answer: that what is meant by 'general' here is that which is not specific to a particular entity, whether it is absolute like a verb or technical general. (al-Qamar) 'And it is said': the speaker is some of the Shafi'is. (al-Qamar) 'Has no generality': because the customary usage in praise or blame is hyperbole, i.e., in obedience or in deterrence from sin, and it involves mentioning a general term without intending generality. We say: hyperbole in this manner is exaggeration, and that is far from the speech of the Lawgiver; how so, if exaggeration were permissible, trust in the reports of promise and threat would be removed due to the possibility of exaggeration. As for hyperbole without exaggeration, it is achieved even if generality is intended. (al-Qamar) 'Because the wording indicates generality': i.e., by convention, and there is no preventing factor from the convention; acting upon the literal meaning is obligatory as long as no preventing factor exists. (al-Qamar) 'Upon generality' and the meaning of the wording is not abandoned as long as possible. (al-Qamar) 'Therefore, etc.': i.e., when the speech mentioned for praise or blame is general, it is permissible to hold to it, etc., then it becomes a proof against al-Shafi'i regarding his view that zakat is not obligatory on jewelry, as in 'al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi'. (al-Qamar) 'The verse': complete verse: 'And those who hoard gold and silver and spend them not in the way of Allah, give them tidings of a painful torment.' (al-Tawba 34) Hoarding means burying, but that is not intended here; rather what is intended is not giving zakat, indicated by the Almighty's saying 'and spend them not in the way of Allah', because what is meant by obligatory spending is zakat, and the threat is not for one who buried wealth, but rather for one who did not pay zakat, whether he buried the wealth or not, as in 'al-Tafsir al-Ahmadi'. (al-Qamar) 'On women's jewelry': i.e., made of gold and silver. (al-Qamar) 'And it is etc.': repelling a hypothetical objection: that the form 'those who' in the verse is a masculine form, so how does it include women?
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage discusses the sixth invalid view in usul al-fiqh: that general terms used specifically for praise or blame lose their generality. According to some Shafi'is, when the Quran praises or blames specific individuals, the general term (e.g., 'the righteous', 'the wicked') does not apply to all individuals of that category; others are included by analogy or other evidence. The Hanafi reply is that this is invalid because the wording itself indicates generality by linguistic convention, and the context of praise or blame does not override that. The Hanafis support their position by citing the verse about hoarding gold and silver (Quran 9:34), which was revealed about specific people but is used to derive a general ruling—the obligation of zakat on women's jewelry. The commentary (labeled al-Qamar) clarifies definitions, refutes the opposing claim of hyperbole, and explains that the masculine form includes women by predominant usage (taghlib). The passage demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to the literal generality of general terms unless there is a clear specifier.

Key Terms
عام (ʿāmm): In this context, some Shafi'is take it as 'absolute' (mutlaq), not the technical general; the Hanafis uphold the technical general meaning that includes all individuals.
مدح/ذم (madḥ/dhamm): Praise and blame contexts that some argue restrict the scope of general terms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the text, what is the ruling on zakat for women's jewelry based on the verse 'And those who hoard gold and silver'?
A) Zakat is obligatory because the verse is general.
B) Zakat is not obligatory because the verse was revealed about specific people.
C) Zakat is obligatory only if the jewelry is buried.
D) Zakat is recommended but not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
General terms in the Quran retain their generality even in praise/blame contexts; only an explicit specifier can restrict them.

The Hanafi Position on Plural-to-Plural Comparison: Muqābalat al-Āḥād bil-Āḥād
Analyzing and refuting the view that plural-construct comparison requires each-to-each distribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the Hanafi principle of muqābalat al-āḥād bil-āḥād and apply it to distinguish between the Hanafi and Shafi'i interpretations of plural-construct comparisons.

Core Idea
When a plural is compared to another plural, the default is one-to-one correspondence, not full distribution.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar presents the seventh invalid view in usul al-fiqh: the claim that a plural in the construct form attached to a plural pronoun (e.g., 'their wealth') when compared to another plural implies that each individual of the first plural is paired with every individual of the second. The Hanafis refute this, arguing that linguistic usage and scholarly consensus support a one-to-one correspondence (muqābalat al-āḥād bil-āḥād). Understanding this debate is crucial for correctly deriving rulings from Quranic verses and everyday speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing view
The Shafi'i majority holds that when a plural in construct is compared to another plural, it necessitates full distribution: each individual of the first plural must be paired with each individual of the second. For example, in 'Take from their wealth a charity,' they argue it requires taking charity from every single wealth item for every rich person.
2
Examine the Hanafi counter-evidence
The Hanafis provide two proofs: linguistic induction (e.g., 'they rode their mounts' means each rode his own mount, not all mounts) and consensus (ijma') that zakat is not due on every dirham and dinar, even though they are wealth. This shows the default is one-to-one correspondence.
The Hanafi position avoids absurd or impractical obligations.
3
Apply the principle to a concrete example
Consider a man saying to his two wives: 'If you both give birth to two children, you are divorced.' Hanafis rule that if each gives birth to one child (total two), divorce occurs. This is because the plural 'two children' is distributed one-to-one to each wife. The opposing view would require each wife to give birth to two children (total four).
4
Recognize exceptions
If external evidence (dalil khariji) indicates full distribution is intended, the default can be overridden. An example is 'Guard the prayers,' which requires guarding all prayers collectively, not just one per person.
The Hanafi principle is a default, not an absolute rule.

Source Text

فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع :وقيل: الجمع المضاف إلى الجماعة، هذا وجه سابع من الوجوه الفاسدة؛ فإن عندهم إذا وقعت مقابلة الجمع بالجمع. حكمه حكم حقيقة الجماعة في حق كل واحد أي لا بدّ لكل فرد من أفراد الجمع الأول من كل فرد من أفراد الثاني؛ ففي قوله تعالى: ﴿خُذْ مِنْ أَمْوَالِهِمْ صَدَقَةً﴾ لا بد في كل مال من السوائم والنقود والعروض لكل أحد من الأغنياء أن تحب الصدقة، ونحن نقول: لا تجب الصدقة في كل درهم ودينار بالإجماع مع أنهما من أفراد الأموال؛ فلا تجب في كل أنواعها أيضًا على ما ذكر في "العضدي".

(القمر) من كل فرد من أفراد الثاني إلخ: مثاله ما إذا قال لنسائه: "إن ولدتن أولادًا فأنتن طالقات" فلا بد لكل امرأة أن تلد ثلاثة أولاد (السنبلي) لا بد في كل مال إلخ لأن لفظ "الأموال" جمع وقد أضيف إلى ضمير الجمع، فيعمل بحقيقة الجماعة في كل واحد من أفراد كلا الجمعين، فلا بد في كل مال إلخ. (القمر) لا تجب الصدقة إلخ: توضيحه: أنه ظاهر أنه لا تجب الصدقة في كل دينار ودرهم بالإجماع مع أنه مال، فلا يصح أن يكون معنى أنه مال، فلا يصح أن يكون معنى الآية خُذ من كل فرد من أموال كل منهم صدقة؛ فلا يجب الصدقة بهذه الآية في كل نوع من أنواع أموالهم أيضا، كذا قال العضدي في شرح أصول ابن الحاجب". (القمر)
Chapter on mentioning the invalid aspects, explanation of the divisions of the fourth division: And it is said: The plural added to a group is a seventh invalid aspect. For in their view, when a plural is compared to a plural, its ruling is the ruling of the reality of the group regarding each one, i.e., it is necessary for each individual of the first plural to be paired with each individual of the second. Thus, in the Almighty's saying: 'Take from their wealth a charity' it is necessary regarding every wealth, from livestock, cash, and goods, for each of the rich, that a charity be taken. But we say: Charity is not obligatory on every dirham and dinar by consensus, even though they are individuals of wealth; so it is not obligatory on every type of their wealth either, as mentioned in 'al-Adudi'. In our view, it requires comparing singulars to singulars, such that if a man said to his two wives: 'If you both give birth to two children, you are divorced,' and each one gave birth to one child, they are divorced; and it is not necessary that each woman give birth to two children. (al-Qamar) 'And it is said': the speaker is the majority of the Shafi'is. (al-Qamar) 'Added': what is meant by addition is absolute relation, not specifically grammatical idafa. (al-Qamar) 'Specifically.' (al-Qamar) 'When it occurs, etc.': when the plural is compared to the dual, the singulars are not divided; rather the dual applies to each individual of the plural. (al-Qamar) 'Regarding each one': i.e., of the individuals of both plurals. (al-Qamar) 'From each individual of the second, etc.': example: if he said to his wives: 'If you give birth to children, you are divorced,' it is necessary for each woman to give birth to three children (al-Sunbuliy). (al-Qamar) 'Necessary for every wealth, etc.': because the word 'wealth' is a plural and is added to a plural pronoun, so it operates with the reality of the group regarding each individual of both plurals; thus it is necessary for every wealth, etc. (al-Qamar) 'Charity is not obligatory, etc.': its clarification: it is apparent that charity is not obligatory on every dinar and dirham by consensus, even though it is wealth; so it cannot be that the meaning of the verse is 'take from each individual of the wealth of each of them a charity'; thus charity is not obligatory by this verse on every type of their wealth either. So said al-Adudi in his commentary on 'Usul Ibn al-Hajib'. (al-Qamar) 'It requires comparing, etc.': by evidence of inductive reasoning and immediate understanding, such as 'they rode their mounts' and 'they wore their clothes' and 'they put their fingers in their ears', etc. The meaning is each one rode his mount, and analogize to this. However, if external evidence indicates that it is necessary for each individual of the first plural from each individual of the second, then it is interpreted accordingly, such as 'Guard the prayers' (Quran 2:238). (al-Qamar) 'They are divorced': because the birth of two children is attributed to two women; since it is based on division of singulars over singulars, its meaning becomes: if this one gave birth to a child and that one gave birth to a child, then when each of them gives birth to one child, the condition is fulfilled, so the consequence follows.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage discusses the seventh invalid view in usul al-fiqh: that when a plural in the construct form (e.g., 'their wealth') is compared to a plural (e.g., 'take charity from their wealth'), it requires each individual of the first plural to be paired with each individual of the second plural. The Shafi'i majority holds that from the verse 'Take from their wealth a charity' it would necessitate taking charity from every single wealth item (dirham, dinar, camel, etc.) for every rich person. The Hanafis reject this, pointing out unanimously that charity is not due on every individual coin. Instead, the Hanafis hold that comparison of plural to plural implies a one-to-one correspondence of individuals (muqābalat al-āḥād bil-āḥād), not a combinatorial distribution. They prove this by linguistic induction and immediate understanding (tabādu): when we say 'they rode their mounts,' it means each person rode his own mount, not that each person rode everyone's mount. Likewise, in the marriage example, if a man says to his two wives, 'If you both give birth to two children,' the condition is fulfilled if each gives birth to one child (total two), not that each must give birth to two (total four). The commentary (al-Qamar) explains that the default is singular-to-singular correspondence unless external evidence suggests otherwise (e.g., 'Guard the prayers' implies guarding all prayers as a whole). The Hanafi position preserves the literal meaning of the language and avoids absurd consequences.

Key Terms
جمع مضاف إلى الجماعة (jamʿ muḍāf ilā al-jamāʿah): A plural noun in the construct form attached to a plural pronoun (e.g., 'amwālihim' – their wealth).
مقابلة الآحاد بالآحاد (muqābalat al-āḥād bil-āḥād): Comparing singulars to singulars: each individual of the first set corresponds to one individual of the second set (not each to all).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his two wives: 'If you both give birth to two children, you are both divorced.' According to the Hanafi position, what happens if each wife gives birth to one child (total two)?
A) They are not divorced because each wife must give birth to two children.
B) They are divorced because the condition is fulfilled by one child each.
C) The divorce is suspended until they give birth to more children.
D) The statement is invalid because it uses plural.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when a plural in construct is compared to another plural, the default interpretation is muqābalat al-āḥād bil-āḥād (one-to-one correspondence), unless external evidence indicates otherwise.

Interpreting Collective Nouns with Group Pronouns: A Hanafi-Shafi‘i Dispute
Discussion of the seventh invalid method (face) regarding collective nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of muqabilat al-ahad bi al-ahad to interpret collective noun constructions and distinguish it from the Shafi‘i interpretation of universal distribution.

Core Idea
A collective noun added to a group pronoun implies one-to-one correspondence, not universal distribution.

This passage from Nur al-Anwar examines a key usul dispute: how should we interpret a plural noun when it is attached to a group pronoun? The apparent meaning suggests universal distribution, but the Hanafi school, supported by common usage and inductive evidence, argues for a one-to-one correspondence. Understanding this rule is crucial for correctly deriving rulings from texts that use such constructions, such as the Qur'anic verse on charity or examples in contracts and oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the construction
Recognize when a plural noun (جمع) is grammatically added to a group pronoun (ضمير جماعة), e.g., 'أموالهم' (their wealths). This triggers the dispute.
2
State the opposing view
The Shafi‘i school holds that such constructions require universal distribution: every individual of the first group must correspond to every individual of the second group. For example, 'Take from their wealths a charity' would mean charity from every type of wealth for every wealthy person.
This is the apparent literal meaning (ظاهر).
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis reject this using inductive evidence (الاستقراء) and common usage (التبادر). Examples like 'they rode their mounts' show that common understanding is one-to-one, not universal. Also, consensus (الإجماع) proves the verse does not require charity from every single dirham/dinar.
The Hanafi position is called muqabilat al-ahad bi al-ahad.
4
Apply the rule to a practical example
Consider a man saying to his two wives: 'If you two give birth to two sons, you are divorced.' According to the Hanafi rule, each wife must give birth to one son (one-to-one). If each gives birth to one son, the condition is fulfilled and they are divorced.
The Shafi‘is would require each wife to give birth to two sons.
5
Note the exception
If external evidence (دليل خارجي) indicates that universal distribution is intended, the Hanafi rule can be overridden. An example is 'Guard the prayers' (حافظوا على الصلوات), where every prayer must be guarded.
This shows the Hanafi principle is default but not absolute.

فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع : وقيل: الجمع المضاف إلى الجماعة، هذا وجه سابع من الوجوه الفاسدة؛ فإن عندهم إذا وقعت مقابلة الجمع بالجمع. حكمه حكم حقيقة الجماعة في حق كل واحد أي لا بدّ لكل فرد من أفراد الجمع الأول من كل فرد من أفراد الثاني؛ ففي قوله تعالى: خُذْ مِنْ أَمْوَالِهِمْ صَدَقَةً (التوبة: ١٠٣) لا بد في كل مال من السوائم والنقود والعروض لكل أحد من الأغنياء أن تؤخذ الصدقة، ونحن نقول: لا تجب الصدقة في كل درهم ودينار بالإجماع مع أنهما من أفراد الأموال؛ فلا تجب في كل أنواعها أيضًا على ما ذكر في "العضدي". وعندنا يقتضي مقابلة الآحاد بالآحاد حتى إذا قال لامرأتيه: "إذا ولدتما ولدين فأنتما طالقتان" فولدت كل واحدة منهما ولدًا طُلّقتا، ولا يلزم أن تلد كل امرأة ولدين. وقيل: القائل جمهور الشافعية. (القمر) المضاف المراد بالإضافة مطلق النسبة لا الإضافة النحوية خاصة. (القمر) خاصةً.(القمر) إذا وقعت إلخ: وإذا قوبل الجمع بالمثنى فلا ينقسم الآحاد بل يجري المثنى على كل فرد من أفراد الجمع. (القمر) في حق كل واحد أي من أفراد الجمعين. (القمر) من كل فرد من أفراد الثاني إلخ: مثاله ما إذا قال لنسائه: "إن ولدتن أولادًا فأنتن طالقات" فلا بد لكل امرأة أن تلد ثلاثة أولاد (السنبلي) لا بد في كل مال إلخ لأن لفظ "الأموال" جمع وقد أضيف إلى ضمير الجمع، فيعمل بحقيقة الجماعة في كل واحد من أفراد كلا الجمعين، فلا بد في كل مال إلخ. (القمر) يقتضي مقابلة إلخ بدليل الاستقراء والتبادر نحو ركبوا دوابهم و لبسوا ثيابهم وجعلوا أصابعهم في آذانهم وغيرها. والمعنى ركب كل واحد دابته وقس على هذا، نعم إذا دل دليل خارجي على أنه لا بد لكل فرد من أفراد الجمع الأول من كل فرد من أفراد الثاني؛ فيحمل عليه نحو حَافِظُوا عَلَى الصَّلَوَاتٍِ (البقرة:۲۳۸) (القمر) طلقتا: لأنه نسب توليد الولدين إلى امرأتين فبناءً على انقسام الآحاد على الآحاد صار معناه إذا ولدت هذه ولدًا وهذه ولدًا، فإذا ولدت كل واحدة منهما ولدًا تحقق الشرط؛ فيترتب الجزاء. A chapter on the mention of invalid methods. Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division: And it is said: The collective noun added to the group (pronoun) — this is a seventh invalid method. For according to them, when a collective is confronted with a collective, its ruling is the ruling of the reality of the group with respect to each individual, i.e., each individual of the first collective must correspond to each individual of the second. So in the verse: "Take from their wealths a charity" (Tawba 103), it would require from every type of wealth — livestock, currency, and goods — from every wealthy person to take charity. But we say: charity is not obligated on every dirham and dinar by consensus, though they are among individuals of wealths; so it is also not obligated on every type of them, as mentioned in "al-Adudi". And according to us, it requires confronting individuals with individuals, such that if a man says to his two wives: "If you two give birth to two sons, then you are divorced" and each gives birth to one son, they are divorced, and it is not required that each woman give birth to two sons. And it is said: The proponent (of the opposing view) is the majority of the Shafi'is. ... (marginal comments follow).
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between the Hanafis and the Shafi'is (and others) regarding the interpretation of a collective noun (جمع) when it is added to a group pronoun (مضاف إلى الجماعة). The opposing view holds that such a construction implies universal distribution: every individual of the first group must correspond to every individual of the second group. For example, in the Qur'anic verse 'Take from their wealths a charity' (Surah Tawba 9:103), they argue that charity is required from every type of wealth (livestock, currency, goods) for every wealthy person. The Hanafis reject this, pointing out that it is agreed that charity is not due on every single dirham or dinar. Instead, the Hanafi position is that such constructions imply a one-to-one correspondence between individuals: each individual of the first group corresponds to each individual of the second, not all to all. They support this with common usage, such as 'they rode their mounts' meaning each person rode his own mount. The author cites the example of a man saying to his two wives 'if you two give birth to two sons, you are divorced' — each wife need only give birth to one son for the condition to be fulfilled (total of two sons), not each giving birth to two. The Shafi'is (like Zufar and al-Shafi'i) interpret this as a figurative use of the plural. The passage also mentions that if there is external evidence (like in the verse 'Guard the prayers'), the opposing interpretation may apply. This analysis is part of the 'invalid methods' (الوجوه الفاسدة) discussion in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
الجمع المضاف إلى الجماعة (al-jam‘ al-mudaf ila al-jama‘ah): A collective noun (plural) that is added (grammatically) to a group pronoun, e.g., 'amwalihim' (their wealths).
مقابلة الآحاد بالآحاد (muqabilat al-ahad bi al-ahad): Confronting individuals with individuals: each individual of the first group corresponds to one individual of the second group.
الاستقراء (al-istiqra'): Inductive evidence; examining multiple examples to derive a general linguistic rule.
التبادر (al-tabaddur): The immediate understanding or common usage; what spontaneously comes to mind when a phrase is uttered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, what is the ruling in the case where a man says to his two wives, 'If you two give birth to two sons, you are divorced,' and each wife gives birth to one son?
A) Both are divorced because the condition is satisfied.
B) Neither is divorced because each must give birth to two sons.
C) Only the wife who gives birth to a son is divorced.
D) The statement is invalid due to ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
A collective noun added to a group pronoun does not automatically imply universal distribution; the Hanafi rule of one-to-one correspondence is supported by common usage and consensus.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Hanafi Position on Command and Its Opposite: Implication of Dislike, Not Prohibition
Commentary refining command's implication on its opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a command implies dislike (karahah) of its opposite, unless the opposite causes loss (tafwit) of the commanded act, and distinguish between cases where the opposite is makruh versus haram.

Core Idea
A command implies dislike of its opposite, not prohibition; only if the opposite causes the commanded act to be missed does it become haram.

This passage refines a classic usul principle: 'al-amr bi al-shay' yanhi 'an diddihi' (commanding a thing prohibits its opposite). The Hanafi school qualifies this: the command only implies dislike (karahah) for the opposite, not absolute prohibition. The distinction hinges on whether the opposite act causes the commanded act to be lost (tafwit). We explore the legal reasoning, the concept of 'sunnah wajibah', and the practical application in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle under debate
What does a command imply regarding its opposite? The text addresses whether commanding a thing implies prohibition (tahrim) of the opposite or a lower ruling.
2
Understand the Hanafi position
The command implies dislike (karahah) of the opposite, not haram, because the necessity of compliance only requires the minimum; the opposite is a subsidiary concomitant and thus lower in rank.
The rational argument: performing the commanded act necessitates refraining from the opposite; this refraining is a necessary concomitant, so it is disliked, not forbidden.
3
Note the exception: tafwit (loss)
If engaging in the opposite causes the commanded act to be missed (tafwit), then it becomes haram by consensus. This qualification removes absolute implications.
For example, if one sits instead of standing in prayer to the extent that the standing is missed, the sitting becomes haram.
4
Apply to prayer scenario
The command to stand (qiyam) in prayer does not directly prohibit sitting; a brief sitting between actions is makruh, not haram, because it does not cause loss of standing. However, prolonged sitting that prevents standing invalidates the prayer and is haram.
The passage uses the example of sitting for the measure of 'Subhanallah' versus excessive delay.
5
Recognize 'sunnah wajibah'
When a prohibition implies its opposite, the opposite becomes an emphasized sunnah (sunnah wajibah), not an obligation, because it is established by transmitted evidence, not reason alone.
This term is a Hanafi intermediate category between sunnah and wajib.

فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع في معنى سنة واجبة؛ وذلك لأن الشيء في نفسه لا يدلّ على ضدّه، وإنما يلزم الحكم في الضد ضرورة الامتثال، فتكفي الدرجة الأدنى في ذلك، وهي الكراهة في الأول؛ لأنها دون التحريم والسنة الواجبة في الثاني؛ لأنها دون الفرض، وليس المراد بالاقتضاء المصطلح السابق بجعل غير المنطوق منطوقا لتصحيح المنطوق، بل إثبات أمر لازم فقط، وهذا إذا لم يلزم من الاشتغال بالضد تفويت المأمور به، فإن لزم منه ذلك يكون حراما بالاتفاق، الاشتغال بالضد تفويت المأمور به أي ضد المأمور به سنة واجبة أي كان مؤكدًا، وإنما أقحم المصنف له لفظ في معنى؛ لأن السنة المؤكدة لا يثبت إلا بالنقل لا بالعقل فكيف يصح أن يقال: إن النهي عن الشيء يقتضي أن يكون ضده سنةً واجبةً. ثم اعلم أن المراد من الواجبة السنة المؤكدة القريبة من الواجب؛ فالمراد بالواجبة الضرورية المؤكدة لا الوجوب الاصطلاحي، فلا يرد أن بين السنة والواجب تضادًا فكيف يكون شيء واحد سنةً و واجبًا؟ (القمر) سنة واجبة إلخ: أي سنة مؤكدة قريبة إلى الواجب. وفي "القواطع" المسألة مصورة فيما إذا كان الأمر للفور لا التراخي. (السنبلي) لأن الشيء إلخ خلاصة الاستدلال منها أن طلب الوجود بالأمر لا يكون بدون إعدام ضده، فكان اقتضاء؛ لأنه ضروري. ولما كان هذا النهي لا يثبت إلا الكراهة، وأما النهي فلأن المنع الأبلغ بطلب الضد، فكان الأمر ضمنيًا؛ فيثبت به الأقل من الواجب . (السنبلي وليس إلخ إذ ليس صحة المنطوق موقوفة عليه. (القمر) بل إثبات إلخ أي بل المراد إثبات أمر لازم؛ فإن الأمر لوجوب إتيان المأمور به فهو ضروري الإتيان، والكفّ عن ضده من لوازم إتيان المأمور به ولما كان الملزوم واجبًا، فاللازم أيضًا واجب فصار هذا الكف واجبًا وصار إتيان ضده حراما، ولما كان حرمة ضده بالتبع وما بالتبع أنزل من الحرمة الأصلية فانحطت رتبتها، وسميت بالكراهة، وكذا النهي لحرمة المنهي عنه، فهو ضروري الكفّ، والاشتغال بضدّه من لوازم الكف عنه، وبضرورة الملزوم يلزم ضرورة اللازم، فصار الاشتغال بضده ضروريًا، ولما كان ضرورة هذا الاشتغال بالتبع، وما بالتبع أنزل من الوجوب الأصلي فانحطت رتبتها وسميت بالسنة الواجبة و لمانع أن يمنع كون الاشتغال بالضد من لوازم الكفّ عنه فإن الكف عنه قد يتحقق بعدم تعلّق الإرادة به وليس ههنا اشتغال بالضد؛ فإنه فعل اختياري، والفعل الاختياري لا يتحقق بدون الإرادة فتأمل (القمر) وهذا أي كراهة ضد المأمور به. (القمر) وهذا إذا لم يلزم إلخ: هذا دفع دخل مقدر تقريره أن ترك صلاة الفرض يُعاقب عليه، والمكروه لا يعاقب عليه؟ وتقرير الدفع أن الكراهة فيما إذا لم يُفوّت الاشتغال به للمأمور به وإن فوّت حرم، وهو المقصود من قول المصنف له فيما بعد وفائدة إلخ. (السنبلي) بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة وهذا معنى ما قال: وفائدة هذا الأصل أن التحريم لما لم يكن مقصودًا بالأمر لم يعتبر إلا من حيث يُفوّت الأمر، فإذا لم يُفوّته كان مكروهًا كالأمر بالقيام يعني إلى الركعة الثانية بعد فراغ الأولى أو الثالثة بعد فراغ التشهد. معطوف على الثانية ليس بنهي عن القعود قصدًا حتى إذا قعد ثم قام لا تفسد صلاته بنفس القعود، ولكنه يكره؛ لأن نفس القعود وهو قعود مقدار تسبيحة لا يُفوّت القيام؛ فيكره، وإن مكث كثيرًا بحيث ذهب أو أن القيام يُفسد الصلاة ومن ههنا ظهر أن الاشتغال بالضد في الوقت الموسع للصلاة لا يحرم وفائدة إلخ : أي ثمرة هذا الأصل وهو أن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده، ولما كان المستفاد من الأصل السابق أن ضدّ المأمور به مكروه سواء كان مُفوّتًا له أو لا، والمستفاد من هذه الثمرة: أن الضد المفوّت له حرام، والضد الغير المفوّت له مكروه، فصارت الثمرة مغايرة لذي الثمرة؛ فلذا قال صاحب "الدائر": إن المراد بالفائدة: الحاصل أي حاصل الكلام في هذا الأصل أي أن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده أن التحريم - أي في ضد المأمور به لمّا لم يكن إلخ. والغرض من بيان الحاصل أن الأصل المذكور ليس مطلقًا بل هو مقيد بالضد الغير المفوت؛ فصار من قبيل تقييد الكلام المطلق. (القمر) لم يكن إلخ لأن الأمر بالشيء لم يوضع للتحريم في الضد. (القمر) لم يعتبر إلخ: أي لم يعتبر التحريم في الضد إلا في مقام يكون ذلك الضد مفوّتًا للأمر، أي المأمور به؛ فإن تفويت المأمور به حرام، فإذا لم يفوته إلخ. ثم اعلم أوّلاً: أن المراد من الأمر في قول المصنف: "يفوت الأمر" المأمور به مجازا؛ فإنّ كون الضد مفوّتًا لنفس الأمر لا يعقل وثانيًا: أنه قد ظهر من هذا الكلام أن الضد الذي يكون مفوتا للمأمور به حرام والضد الذي لا يكون مفوّتا له مكروه. ومن قال: إن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي النهي والحرمة عن ضده، فمراده من الضد هو الضد المفوت كما قال بحر العلوم مولانا عبد العلي له؛ فحينئذ لا يبقى النزاع بل آل النزاع إلى اللفظ. (القمر) لأن نفس إلخ دليل لقول المصنف: لا تفسد صلاته إلخ. (القمر) لا يفوّت إلخ الجواز أن يعود إلى القيام المأمور به لعدم تعين الزمان له القمر فيكره: لوجوب التوالي في الأفعال الصلاتية وتخلل الغير إن كان من جنسها موجب الكراهة وإن لم يكن من جنسها كالكلام والعمل الكثير يفسد، كذا قيل. (القمر) أو أن القيام إلخ فيه أن القيام إلى الركعة الثانية بعد الفراغ عن الأولى، أو إلى الركعة الثالثة بعد الفراغ عن التشهد ليس محدودًا مؤقتا بوقت حتى يذهب أوانه؛ ولذا قيل: إن صورة تفويت القعود والقيام أنه حرم قاعدًا مع القدرة على القيام ولم يقم أصلاً؛ فيفسد الصلاة؛ فيكون هذا القعود حراما، فتأمل. (السنبلي) ومن ههنا إلخ أي من أجل أن الضد المفوّت للمأمور به حرام، والغير المفوت له مكروه. (القمر) لا يحرم لأنه ليس بمفوّت للصلاة. (القمر)
Chapter on mentioning the corrupt aspects. Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division concerning the meaning of 'obligatory sunnah'. This is because a thing in itself does not indicate its opposite; rather, the ruling regarding the opposite is necessitated by the necessity of compliance. So the lowest degree suffices in that, which is disliked (karahah) in the first [case of command] because it is below prohibition, and 'obligatory sunnah' in the second [case of prohibition] because it is below obligation. The intended meaning of 'requirement' (iqtiḍā') is not the technical term previously used of making the unspoken spoken to correct the spoken, but rather establishing a necessary concomitant only. And this is when engaging in the opposite does not entail missing the commanded act; if it does entail missing it, then it is forbidden by agreement. Engaging in the opposite entails missing the commanded act. 'Obligatory sunnah' means it is emphasized. The author inserted the word 'in meaning' because the emphasized sunnah is established only by transmitted evidence, not by reason, so how can it be said that prohibition of a thing requires its opposite to be an obligatory sunnah? Then know that the intended meaning of 'obligatory' is the emphasized sunnah close to obligatory; so by 'obligatory' is meant the necessarily emphasized, not technical obligation; so it is not objected that there is a contradiction between sunnah and obligatory—how can one thing be both sunnah and obligatory? (Al-Qamar) 'Obligatory sunnah' etc.: i.e., an emphasized sunnah close to obligatory. In Al-Qawati', the issue is illustrated when the command is for immediate [action], not delayed. (Al-Sanbali) 'Because a thing' etc.: The summary of the inference is that the demand for existence by a command cannot be without negating its opposite, so it is a requirement because it is necessary. And since this prohibition does not establish anything other than dislike (karahah)—as for the prohibition, because the stronger prevention is by demanding the opposite, so the command is implicit; thus the lesser of the two obligations is established. (Al-Sanbali) 'And it is not' etc.: Because the correctness of the spoken is not dependent on it. (Al-Qamar) 'Rather, establishing' etc.: i.e., the meaning is establishing a necessary concomitant; for a command necessitates performing the commanded act, so it is necessary to perform it, and refraining from its opposite is one of the concomitants of performing the commanded act. And since the necessary of the necessary is also necessary, this refraining becomes obligatory and performing its opposite becomes forbidden. And since the prohibition of its opposite is subsidiary and what is subsidiary is lower than original prohibition, its rank is lowered and it is called disliked (karahah). Similarly, the prohibition [of a thing] is due to the forbiddenness of the prohibited thing, so it is necessary to refrain from it, and engaging in its opposite is one of the concomitants of refraining from it; by the necessity of the necessary, the necessity of the concomitant follows, so engaging in its opposite becomes necessary. And since the necessity of this engagement is subsidiary, and what is subsidiary is lower than original obligation, its rank is lowered and it is called obligatory sunnah. And an objector may object that engaging in the opposite is not a concomitant of refraining from it, because refraining may occur by not attaching intention to it, and there is no engagement in the opposite here; for it is a voluntary action, and voluntary action does not occur without intention—so reflect. (Al-Qamar) 'And this' i.e., the dislikedness of the opposite of the commanded act. (Al-Qamar) 'And this is when it does not entail' etc.: This is a rebuttal of an anticipated objection: its formulation is that leaving an obligatory prayer is punishable, and a disliked act is not punishable? And the rebuttal is that the dislikedness is when engaging in it does not cause the commanded act to be missed; if it does cause it to be missed, it is forbidden, and this is what the author intends by his later statement 'and the benefit' etc. (Al-Sanbali) Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division, chapter on mentioning the corrupt aspects. And this is the meaning of what he said: 'The benefit of this principle is that since prohibition is not intended by the command, it is not considered except as it causes the command to be missed; so if it does not cause it to be missed, it is disliked, like the command to stand' meaning to the second rakʿah after finishing the first, or to the third after finishing the tashahhud. It is conjoined to the second. It is not a direct prohibition of sitting, so that if he sits and then stands, his prayer is not invalidated by the sitting itself, but it is disliked; because the sitting itself—a sitting of the measure of one subḥānah—does not cause the standing to be missed; so it is disliked. And if he delays excessively such that [the standing] goes away, or that the standing invalidates the prayer—and from here it appears that engaging in the opposite in the expansive time for prayer is not forbidden. 'And the benefit' etc.: i.e., the fruit of this principle, which is that commanding something implies the dislikedness of its opposite. And since what is derived from the previous principle is that the opposite of the commanded act is disliked whether it causes it to be missed or not, and what is derived from this benefit is that the opposite that causes it to be missed is forbidden, and the opposite that does not cause it to be missed is disliked, so the benefit differs from that which bears benefit; hence the author of Al-Dā'ir said that the intended meaning of 'benefit' is 'the outcome,' i.e., the conclusion of the discussion on this principle, i.e., that commanding something implies the dislikedness of its opposite—that prohibition [of the opposite]—since it was not [intended] etc. The purpose of stating the conclusion is that the mentioned principle is not absolute, but rather qualified by the opposite that does not cause loss; so this falls under qualifying absolute speech. (Al-Qamar) 'Was not' etc.: Because the command for something is not set up for prohibition of its opposite. (Al-Qamar) 'Was not considered' etc.: i.e., prohibition in the opposite is not considered except in the situation where that opposite causes the command—i.e., the commanded act—to be missed; for causing the commanded act to be missed is forbidden, so if it does not cause it to be missed etc. Then know, firstly, that the intended meaning of 'command' in the author's statement 'causes the command to be missed' is the commanded act metaphorically; for the opposite causing the command itself to be missed is unintelligible. Secondly, it has appeared from this discussion that the opposite that causes the commanded act to be missed is forbidden, and the opposite that does not cause it to be missed is disliked. And whoever says that commanding something implies prohibition and forbiddenness of its opposite, his intended meaning of 'opposite' is the opposite that causes loss, as stated by Baḥr al-ʿUlūm Mawlānā ʿAbd al-ʿAlī to him; then there remains no disagreement, but rather the disagreement reduces to wording. (Al-Qamar) 'Because the very' etc. is evidence for the author's statement 'his prayer is not invalidated' etc. (Al-Qamar) 'Does not cause to be missed' etc.: The permissibility may refer back to the commanded standing because the time is not specified for it. (Al-Qamar) 'So it is disliked': Due to the necessity of continuity in prayer actions, and the insertion of another action if it is of the same kind causes dislike, and if it is not of its kind like speech or excessive action, it invalidates—as it is said. (Al-Qamar) 'Or that the standing' etc.: It contains that standing for the second rakʿah after finishing the first, or for the third after finishing the tashahhud, is not limited or timed to a specific moment such that its time passes; therefore it is said: The scenario of causing loss by sitting or standing is that one remains sitting while able to stand and does not stand at all; thus the prayer is invalidated; so this sitting becomes forbidden—so reflect. (Al-Sanbali) 'And from here' etc.: i.e., because the opposite that causes the commanded act to be missed is forbidden, and the one that does not cause it to be missed is disliked. (Al-Qamar) 'It is not forbidden' because it is not causing loss of the prayer. (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a usul al-fiqh principle: commanding a thing implies a ruling regarding its opposite. The commentator (likely from the Hanafi tradition) refines this principle by distinguishing between two scenarios. Initially, the text identifies two 'corrupt aspects'—misunderstandings about the implication. The first part clarifies that 'obligatory sunnah' (sunnah wajibah) here means an emphasized sunnah close to wajib, not actual obligation. The commentator explains that the principle of requirement (iqtida') is not about making implicit text explicit to correct the wording, but about establishing a necessary concomitant: performing the commanded act necessitates refraining from its opposite. Thus, the command implies dislike (karahah) for the opposite, not prohibition (tahrim). However, if engaging in the opposite causes the commanded act to be missed (tafwit), then it becomes forbidden by consensus. The second part applies this to prayer: the command to stand (qiyam) in prayer does not directly prohibit sitting (qu'ud); sitting is disliked unless it delays the standing excessively, in which case it becomes forbidden. The benefit (fa'idah) of this principle is to clarify that the opposite is only makruh when it does not cause loss; otherwise it is haram. This resolves apparent contradictions in earlier statements. The commentator also addresses potential objections: the opposite being 'obligatory sunnah' is not rationally derived but based on transmitted evidence. Finally, he notes that in expansive time for prayer (like performing an obligatory prayer in its broad time), engaging in the opposite (e.g., doing other acts) is not forbidden because it does not cause loss of the prayer itself. The entire discussion is a Hanafi refinement of the rule 'al-amr yanhi 'an diddihi', ensuring it is applied correctly without overextension.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): Here means a necessary concomitant derived from the command, not the technical usul term of making unspoken text spoken.
سنة واجبة (sunnah wajibah): An emphasized sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah) that is close to wajib in strength, but not technically obligatory.
ضد (didd): The logical opposite of the commanded act (e.g., sitting is the opposite of standing in prayer).
تفويت (tafwit): Loss or missing of the commanded act; the decisive factor that changes the ruling from karahah to haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position explained in this passage, if a person sits briefly (the amount of saying Subhanallah) instead of standing immediately after finishing the first rak'ah in prayer, what is the ruling on that sitting?
A) It is haram because it is the opposite of the commanded standing.
B) It is makruh because it does not cause the standing to be missed.
C) It is permissible (mubah) because there is no prohibition.
D) It invalidates the prayer because it is an interruption.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: a command implies dislike (karahah) of its opposite, not prohibition; only if the opposite causes the commanded act to be lost (tafwit) does it become haram.

The Four Types of Rukhsah in Hanafi Usul
Defining and classifying rukhsah into four types with degrees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four types of rukhsah according to the Hanafi scheme and distinguish between real and metaphorical rukhsah based on the operation of the 'azimah.

Core Idea
Rukhsah is not a single concept; it has four types, each defined by the degree the original strict ruling remains operative.

In this passage, the author explains why rukhsah cannot be given a single definition. Instead, he introduces a fourfold classification that is essential for understanding legal concessions. By parsing this taxonomy, you will learn to distinguish real concessions from metaphorical ones and appreciate the nuance in Hanafi legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Determine why rukhsah cannot be defined as a single term
The text states that rukhsah does not have a single meaning common to all its types (لا مشتركة معنى). Therefore, the author divides it into types first, then defines each type separately.
This shows rukhsah is not univocal; its classification is necessary before definition.
2
Distinguish real (haqiqah) from metaphorical (majaz) rukhsah
Real rukhsah is that in which the original ruling ('azimah) remains operative. Metaphorical rukhsah is that in which the 'azimah is not operative, making the concession a figurative relaxation.
This distinction affects whether the original obligation still binds in principle.
3
Identify the two subtypes within each category
Within real rukhsah, one type is 'more deserving' because the 'azimah is fully operative; the second is less real because the 'azimah is only partially operative. Within metaphorical rukhsah, one type is 'more complete' in its metaphorical quality.
This creates a fourfold taxonomy: two real, two metaphorical, each with degrees.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الرخصة وأنواعها. ورخصة عطف على قوله عزيمة، ولم يعرفها؛ لأنها ليست بمشتركة معنى، وليس لها حقيقة متحدة توجد في جميع أنواعها على السوية، بل قسمها أولاً إلى الأنواع، ثم عرّف كل نوع على حدة. وتقسيمها باعتبار ما يطلق عليه اسم الرخصة، فقال: وهي أربعة أنواع: نوعان من الحقيقة أحدهما أحق من الآخر، ونوعان من المجاز، أحدهما أتمّ من الآخر. وتفصيله: أنّ الرخصة الحقيقية هي التي تبقى عزيمته معمولة، فكلما كانت العزيمة ثابتة كانت الرخصة أيضًا في مقابلتها حقيقة، ففي القسمين الأولين لما كانت العزيمة موجودة معمولة في الشريعة كانت الرخصة في مقابلتها أيضًا حقيقة ثابتة ثم في القسم الأول منهما لما كانت العزيمة موجودة من جميع الوجوه كانت الرخصة أيضًا حقيقة من جميع الوجوه، بخلاف القسم الثاني؛ فإن العزيمة فيه موجودة من وجه دون وجه فلا تكون الرخصة أحق أيضا.
Explanation of rukhsah and its types. And 'rukhsah' is connected (as a conjunct) to his saying 'azimah. He did not define it because it does not have a common meaning, and it does not have a single reality found equally in all its types, but rather he first divided it into types, then defined each type separately. And its division is based on what the term rukhsah is applied to. He said: It is four types: two types of reality, one more deserving than the other, and two types of metaphor, one more complete than the other. And its detail: That the real rukhsah is that in which its 'azimah remains operative, so whenever the 'azimah is established, the rukhsah in its opposite is also real. So in the first two sections, when the 'azimah exists and is operative in the Shariah, the rukhsah in its opposite is also a fixed reality. Then in the first section of them, when the 'azimah exists in all aspects, the rukhsah is also real in all aspects, contrary to the second section; for the 'azimah exists in one aspect but not another, so the rukhsah is not more deserving either.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies rukhsah (license/concession) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by stating that rukhsah is not a single term with a common meaning; instead, it applies to four distinct types. He divides them into two categories: real (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz). Within each, there is a hierarchy: one type is more 'deserving' or 'complete' than the other. The real rukhsah is defined as a concession that coexists with the original ruling ('azimah) remaining operative. If the 'azimah is fully in effect, the rukhsah is fully real; if only partially, the rukhsah is less real. Similarly, metaphorical rukhsah has degrees. This classification is crucial for understanding when a rukhsah truly applies as a legal concession versus a mere relaxation. The Hanafi school uses this to differentiate between situations like travel concessions (e.g., shortening prayer) where the original obligation remains, versus cases like eating carrion in necessity where the prohibition is temporarily lifted.

Key Terms
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): A legal concession that allows a dispensation from a stricter original ruling due to a valid excuse.
العزيمة (al-azimah): The original, strict ruling that applies generally; opposite of rukhsah.
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): Real; here, a type of rukhsah that is genuinely a concession with the original ruling still in place.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical; a type of rukhsah that is not a real concession but a figurative relaxation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the author not define rukhsah with a single definition?
A) Because it has no lexical meaning.
B) Because it does not have a single meaning common to all its types.
C) Because it is only metaphorical.
D) Because it is synonymous with 'azimah.

Key Takeaway
Rukhsah is a multi-faceted concept requiring classification into real and metaphorical types based on the operative status of the original ruling ('azimah).
